# Advances in functional studies of plant MYC transcription factors

Zewei Li1  · Yunshuai Huang1  · Zhiwei Shen1  · Meifang Wu1  · Mujun Huang1  · Seung‑Beom Hong2  · Liai ${ \tt X } { \tt u } ^ { 1 }$ · Yunxiang Zang1 $\textcircled{1}$

Received: 14 March 2024 / Accepted: 17 July 2024 / Published online: 5 August 2024

$\circledcirc$ The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer-Verlag GmbH Germany, part of Springer Nature 2024

# Abstract

Myelocytomatosis (MYC) transcription factors (TFs) belong to the basic helix-loop-helix (bHLH) family in plants and play a central role in governing a wide range of physiological processes. These processes encompass plant growth, development, adaptation to biotic and abiotic stresses, as well as secondary metabolism. In recent decades, signifcant strides have been made in comprehending the multifaceted regulatory functions of MYCs. This advancement has been achieved through the cloning of MYCs and the characterization of plants with MYC defciencies or overexpression, employing comprehensive genome-wide ‘omics’ and protein–protein interaction technologies. MYCs act as pivotal components in integrating signals from various phytohormones' transcriptional regulators to orchestrate genome-wide transcriptional reprogramming. In this review, we have compiled current research on the role of MYCs as molecular switches that modulate signal transduction pathways mediated by phytohormones and phytochromes. This comprehensive overview allows us to address lingering questions regarding the interplay of signals in response to environmental cues and developmental shift. It also sheds light on the potential implications for enhancing plant resistance to diverse biotic and abiotic stresses through genetic improvements achieved by plant breeding and synthetic biology eforts.

# Introduction

Plants are frequently exposed to a wide range of environmental challenges throughout their growth and development. These challenges encompass abiotic stresses like salinity, low temperatures, and drought, as well as biotic stresses in the form of disease and pests. These stresses change the

Key Laboratory of Quality and Safety Control for Subtropical Fruit and Vegetable, Ministry of Agriculture and Rural Afairs, Collaborative Innovation Center for Efcient and Green Production of Agriculture in Mountainous Areas of Zhejiang Province, College of Horticulture Science, Zhejiang A&F University, Hangzhou 311300, Zhejiang, China

endogenous levels of phytohormones to exert a substantial impact on various aspects of plant life, including immune defense, growth, reproduction, and overall yield. In response to diverse stresses, plants activate specifc receptor-mediated response mechanisms that modulate the levels of reactive oxygen species (ROS) and calcium that serve as a second messenger in signaling pathways. This is followed by phytohormones-mediated regulation of numerous transcription factors (TFs) that subsequently govern the expression of a cascade of target genes (Berrios and Rentsch 2022; Martin et al. 2022). Among these TFs, MYC stands out for its pivotal roles in modulating gene expression across various biological processes and in enhancing defense responses (Hao et al. 2021; Qian et al. 2021). MYC2 was identifed through the screening of jasmonate-insensitive (jin) mutants, which displayed reduced sensitivity to methyl jasmonate (MeJA)- induced inhibition of root growth and decreased production of vegetable storage protein (VSP) in Arabidopsis leaves (Berger et al. 1996). The elucidation of MYC2’s function was expedited following the cloning of the JIN1 gene, which encodes MYC2 (Lorenzo et al. 2004). The comparative analysis of diferentially expressed genes (DEGs) in myc2 mutant with their MeJA-responsive expression in the wild type indicated that the dependency of gene expression on

MYC2 was primarily evident under MeJA treatment (Dombrecht et al. 2007). As the core TFs in jasmonate (JA) signaling pathway, MYC proteins, in concert with other TFs, orchestrate the activation of JA-responsive genes through extensive TF networks that trigger global changes in transcriptome, epigenome, proteome, and phosphoproteome (Zander et al. 2020). Exposing Arabidopsis etiolated seedlings to JA for two hours led to the identifcation of 2522 DEGs, one-third of which (843 DEGs) were found to have their promoters directly bound by MYC2 or MYC3. Thus, MYC proteins hold a central position in the multifaceted regulation of phytohormone signaling pathways, serving as a key regulator for essential processes such as regulating cell proliferation and growth, modulating cell diferentiation, and controlling metabolism. This review aims to bridge existing gaps in understanding the interactions between the MYCinduced JA signaling and other signaling pathways mediated by diferent phytohormones and phytochromes, introduce new perspectives, and integrate current knowledge for practical applications.

# Roles of MYCs in JA signaling and gene regulation

As a member of bHLH family, MYCs play a pivotal role in the regulation of jasmonate (JA) signaling pathways. In Arabidopsis thaliana, MYC2 (AtbHLH006), MYC3 (Atb-HLH005), MYC4 (AtbHLH004), and MYC5 (AtbHLH28) belong to the IIIe group of bHLH subfamilies (Chini et al. 2016), characterized by three conserved domains: an N-terminal JID (JAZ-interacting domain) followed by a TAD (transcription activation domain) and a C-terminal bHLH domain. They are nuclear proteins, as shown by the experiment that the full-length MYC2 protein fused with GFP was observed exclusively within the nucleus of transiently transformed tobacco cells (Lorenzo et al. 2004). Conversely, MYC2 lacking a C-terminal bHLH structural domain exhibited an absence of nuclear localization signals. This compelling evidence underscores the essential role of the bHLH structural domain in facilitating nuclear localization (Lorenzo et al. 2004). GFP-protein fusion assays showed that MYC3 and MYC4 also are localized in the nucleus (Fernández-Calvo et al. 2011). The basic bHLH domain is involved in binding to the G-box sequence $5 ^ { \prime }$ -CACGTG-3′) present in the MYCs target gene promoters (López-Vidriero et al. 2021). Notably, the palindromic G-box sequence is a core part of the light-responsive cis-element $5 ^ { \prime }$ -TCTTACACG TGGCAYY-3′) (Giuliano et al. 1988). For further detailed information on the structure and functionality of MYC2- related TFs conserved in plants, readers are directed to recent articles (López-Vidriero et al. 2021; Song et al. 2022). In light of the functional redundancy observed among MYC

TFs (Chini et al. 2016; Fernández-Calvo et al. 2011), we collectively refer to MYCs as MYC2/3/4/5 throughout this review. While they also may have specialized roles in regulating certain aspects of growth and development (Johnson et al. 2023; Wang et al. 2021a), this terminology simplifes our discussion of their overlapping functions. Unlike MYCs, MYC1 (AtbHLH0n12) stands apart as it falls within the IIIf bHLH clade (Heim et al. 2003) and is implicated in specifc biological processes such as trichome formation and iron homeostasis (Song et al. 2023; Zhao et al. 2012). Earlier studies have suggested that MYC1 may function either independently or redundantly with MYCs in various biological processes. Notably, both the IIIe and IIIf bHLH clades are associated with favonoid/anthocyanin biosynthesis (Heim et al. 2003; Hichri et al. 2010; Dombrecht et al. 2007).

When endogenous levels of jasmonate (JA) fall below a certain threshold, the expression of JA-responsive genes is inhibited through the active repression of MYCs, mediated by direct binding of JASMONATE ZIM-DOMAIN (JAZ) proteins, which serve as key negative regulators of the JA signal transduction pathway (Chini et  al. 2016). Additionally, in the absence of JA, MYC-bound JAZ proteins interact with a bridging protein known as NOVEL INTERACTOR OF JAZ (NINJA) adaptor protein to recruit the transcriptional co-repressor TOPLESS (TPL) (Pauwels et al. 2010). The interaction between the Jas motif of JAZs and the N-terminus of MYCs inhibits the access of MYCs to the transcriptional co-regulator MEDIATOR 25 (MED25) subunit within the MEDIATOR complex (Çevik et al. 2012; Chen et al. 2012; Kazan 2017). The specifcity of JA signaling is underscored by the alternative splicing of individual JAZ genes, generating multiple JAZ isoforms, and their spatiotemporal expression patterns (Chini et al. 2016; DeMott et al. 2021; Pauwels and Goossens 2011). Furthermore, apart from interacting with MYCs, JAZ family proteins can also interact with various other TFs involved in diferent phytohormone signaling pathways independently of MYCs, thus orchestrating specifc biological responses to diverse environmental stimuli (Wager and Browse 2012). Diferent MYC and JAZ genes may have specialized roles in regulating various aspects of growth, development, and defense responses. When plants experience stress, JA is produced and then transformed into the active molecule jasmonate-isoleucine (JA-Ile), which binds to its receptor F-box protein CORONATIN INSENSITIVE 1 (COI1), a component of Skp1/Cullin/F-box $( \mathrm { S C F } ^ { \mathrm { C O I 1 } }$ ) multi-subunit E3 ubiquitin ligase (SCFCOI) complex. This binding promotes the ubiquitination and degradation of JAZ from the SCFCOI1-JAZ coreceptor complex via 26S proteasome, which results in releasing MYC2 and allowing the MED25 to bind and activate MYC2 as a master regulator that either stimulates or inhibit the transcription of JA-responsive downstream genes (Thines et al. 2007; Wu et al. 2020). In

the fnal transcriptional activation stage, MED25 not only brings COI1 to MYC2 target promoters and facilitates COI1- dependent JAZ degradation but also recruits HISTONE ACETYLTRANSFERASE1 (HAC1) and polymerase II to the promoters of MYC2 target genes (An et al. 2017). MYC3/4/5, the closest homologs of MYC2, are also known to physically associate with JAZs and MED25 (Aerts et al. 2021; Fernández-Calvo et al. 2011; Niu et al. 2011). MYC2 forms either homodimers or heterodimers with MYC3 and MYC4, and MYC5 functions redundantly with MYC2/3/4 to modulate diverse JA-mediated gene expression and defense responses (Niu et  al. 2011; Song et  al. 2017). Besides JAZs, MED25, and MYC3/4/5, MYC2 interacts with many other TFs to regulate diverse hormonal and development responses (Dombrecht et al. 2007; Wang et al. 2017).

In Arabidopsis, MYC2/3/4/5 all transactivate JAZ promoters, establishing an autoregulatory negative feedback circuit to terminate JA signaling (Chini et al. 2007; Figueroa and Browse 2015; Niu et al. 2011). MYC2 also induces the expression of the JAMs, which encode the bHLH subgroup IIId factors JASMONATE-ASSOCIATED MYC2-LIKE proteins known to act as transcriptional repressors that negatively regulate JA-responsive genes independently of MYC2 interaction by competing with MYCs for DNA binding sites and interacting with certain JAZ proteins in Arabidopsis (Putterill et al. 1995; Qi et al. 2015b; Sekimoto et al. 2013a). By contrast, in tomato, JA-inducible MYC2-TARGETED BHLH proteins (SlMTBs) hinder the formation of MYC2- MED25 complex and compete with MYC2 for binding to its target gene promoters, forming a negative feedback regulatory loop (Liu et al. 2019a, b, c). This process would initiate a brief JA response, leading to cellular desensitization to JA over the long term. Accordingly, MYC2 may primarily control the expression of early JA-responsive genes. Some of JA-induced efects may be indirectly mediated by MYC2 due to a hierarchical transcriptional cascade efect on other downstream MYC2-targeted TFs that regulate late JAresponsive genes. This notion is supported by the previous fndings that MYC2-targeted JA-responsive genes in tomato are enriched for early JA-responsive genes, including JAZ and JA biosynthesis genes, and that late JA-responsive genes are enriched for defense-related TFs (Du et al. 2017). Analysis of the cis-elements in the 2-kb promoters of TaMYC, OsMYC, BdMYC, CsMYC, and BoMYC genes present in the respective wheat, rice, stif brome, tea, and cabbage plants revealed that they have commonly three types of numerous cis-elements related to plant growth/development, hormone, and abiotic stresses, and that their individual genes of MYC family exhibited varied expression patterns with distinct but overlapping functions in driving JA-responsive gene expression depending on tissues of roots, stems, leaves, and inforescences (Chen et al. 2019b, 2021b; Li et al. 2022b). Accordingly, MYCs primarily govern the initial surge of

JA-induced gene expression, orchestrating a species-specifc reprogramming of cellular growth, development, and metabolism. This process involves the collaboration of multiple other TFs that aid in the genetic control switch.

# Function of MYCs in plant growth and development

MYCs serve as multifunctional TFs, acting as signaling mediators that integrate environmental cues into transcriptional networks of plants. They are involved in many aspects of plant growth and development, including but not limited to seed size and weight, seed germination, photoperiod, fowering, root hair formation, leaf senescence, and photosynthesis. The intricate interplay between MYCs and JAZs, through epistatic interactions, regulates various aspects of growth and development with high combinatorial complexity (Figs. 1, 2, 3). MYCs play a pivotal role in orchestrating a transcriptional hierarchy that intricately balances growth and defense mechanisms. This delicate equilibrium is fnely modulated by the repression mediated by JAZs and DELLAs and their transcriptional activation by MYCs, superimposed upon the dynamic interplay with phytohormones and phytochromes. JA-activated MYCs may diferentially control various biological processes in response to the levels of JA-Ile that are tightly controlled by internal and external factors during growth and development (Staswick and Tiryaki 2004; Suza and Staswick 2008). Supporting this concept, MYC2 negatively and positively regulated photosynthesis at 6 h and $4 8 \mathrm { { h } }$ of post-MeJA treatment, respectively, while consistently up-regulating genes associated with oxidative stress tolerance 48 h after MeJA treatment (Guo et al. 2012). The triple mutations in MYC2/3/4 (mycT) in Arabidopsis led to increased $\mathrm { C O } _ { 2 }$ assimilation compared to wild-type leaves, despite exhibiting photosynthetic rates similar to wild type (Campos et al. 2016; Major et al. 2017).

# Regulation of seed size, weight and germination

MYC2/3/4 collectively assumes overlapping functions in seed size, weight, stamen development, and regulation of seed storage protein accumulation. Particularly, single and double myc mutants display a marked increase in seed size compared to the wild type, while triple mycT mutants (myc2myc3myc4) exhibit the most significant enhancement in seed size (Gao et al. 2016). This augmentation is accompanied by the elevation in seed storage protein content. However, it is worth noting that triple mutants show a decrease in the amount of 2S albumins (arabins) (Gao et al. 2016). In a similar vein, MYC5 shares redundant roles with MYC2/3/4 in the regulation of seed yield and stamen development. Interestingly, single and double mutants do not

![](images/0f7ca17580d5a7d839a3660f52668c512d496a4520741715eee62648d0238b7e.jpg)  
Fig. 1 Schematic diagram depicting the roles of MYCs in regulating and coordinating plant growth and developmental processes through the infuence of plant hormones and light (drawing based on wellknown concepts from Arabidopsis). Abbreviations: ABA, abscisic acid; ABI5, ABA-insensitive 5; ARF2, auxin-responsive factor 2; AUX/IAA, auxin/indoleacetic acid protein; COI1, coronatine-insensi-

exhibit stamen development defects, and even mycT mutants remain unafected in this aspect. It is only in the quadruple mutants (myc2myc3myc4myc5) that defects in seed germination in the dark and stamen development in the light become apparent, ultimately resulting in reduced seed production (Ortigosa et al. 2020; Qi et al. 2015b). When MYCs are derepressed due to mutations in a group of JAZs (quintuple jazQ and decuple jazD), both jazQ and jazD mutants showed reductions in the number, mass, and yield of seeds, with more pronounced efects observed in jazD (Guo et al. 2018). Genetic epistasis analyses of a jazD mycT tredecuple mutant revealed that MYCs decrease fruit size, seed mass, and seed yield (Guo et al. 2022).

With respect to seed germination, myc2 mutants showed higher sensitivity to ABA since the percentage of seed germination in myc2 mutants was less than that of wild-type plants after ABA treatment (Berger et al. 1996). On the other hand, myc2 mutants displayed insensitivity to JA and ABA with respect to seedling emergence and growth above the ground (Abe et al. 2003; Lorenzo et al. 2004). The results suggest that in the dark, MYC2 may mainly derepress ABAinduced germination inhibition, resulting in higher sensitivity to ABA, whereas in the light, the interplay of MYC2 with

tive 1; ET, ethylene; EIN3, ethylene insensitive 3; EIL1, EIN3-like 1; JA-Ile, jasmonate isoleucine; GA, gibberellic acid; GL2, GLABRA2; HLS1, hookless 1; IAA, indole acetic acid; NPR1, nonexpressor of pathogenesis-related genes 1; PYL, pyrabactin resistance-like; SA, salicylic acid; SLY1, SLEEPY 1; TTG1, TRANSPARENT TESTA GLABRA1

both ABA and JA pathways can lead to diferent responses in seedling establishment and early growth. MYC2 normally functions to modulate or attenuate the plant's response to ABA, and its absence leads to a more pronounced response to this hormone. This notion is corroborated by the fnding that MED25, which physically and functionally interact with MYC2, negatively regulates ABA-mediated gene expression through interacting with the basic domain/leucine zipper (bZIP) transcription factor ABSCISIC ACID INSENSI-TIVE 5 (ABI5) (Chen et al. 2012) (Fig. 1). ABI5 was found to be mostly active in seeds and prevents germination and post-germinative growth under unfavorable conditions (Collin et al. 2021). Seed germination is primarily regulated by a photoreceptor PHYTOCHTOME B (PHYB) (Shinomura et al. 1994), and PHYB is primarily required for MYC2/3/4 stability as indicated by reduced levels of MYC2/3/4 protein accumulation in phyB mutant plants (Chico et al. 2014). The triple (myc2myc3myc4) and quadruple (myc2myc3myc4myc5) mutants showed a defect in seed germination specifcally in the dark, with no discernable impact in the light, which was typically reminiscent of phyB germination defects (Ortigosa et al. 2020). These observations underscore the intricate interplay between MYCs and the PHYB

photoreceptor, with light playing a crucial role in modulating the efects of MYC defciency on seed germination. In the presence of light, especially light detected by the PHYB photoreceptor, these light-dependent regulatory mechanisms might compensate for the absence of MYC2/3/4, allowing

seed germination to proceed relatively unafected. On the other hand, in the absence of light, these compensatory mechanisms may be less efective or non-existent, leading to the observed defect in seed germination in the myc mutants. The specifc defects observed in the dark suggest that MYCs,

in coordination with PHYB, may have a more prominent role or diferent regulatory mechanisms under conditions of reduced or absent light.

# Formation of trichomes and root hairs

Trichomes serve as protective shields for plants against the harmful efects of UV radiation, strong winds and insect damage (Balkunde et al. 2010). On the other hand, root hairs play a crucial role in enhancing water and nutrient uptake while facilitating interaction with both biotic and abiotic factors in soil environment (Ishida et al. 2008). The formation of trichome and root hair depends on the coordinated action of three groups of TF proteins, which comprise WD40 repeat protein TRANSPARENT TESTA GLABRA1 (TTG1), R2R3 repeat MYB protein GLABRA1 (GL1), and bHLH protein GLABRA3 (GL3) (Wei et al. 2019). The regulatory core complex formed by GL1-GL3-TTG1 is directly inhibited through physical interaction with both DELLAs, repressors of GA signaling, and JAZs, repressors of JA signaling in the absence of GA and JA (Qi et al. 2014). The core complex infuences the expression and activity of many downstream genes and proteins that control the diferentiation of trichomes and root hair cells. One of the genes activated by this complex is GLABRA2 (GL2), a key homeodomain TF, which promotes trichome outgrowth in shoots and inhibits root hair formation in roots (Chen and Wang 2019; Ishida et al. 2008; Szymanski et al. 1998). AtMYC1 is required for the complete expression of GL2, and mutations in AtMYC1 result in incomplete expression of GL2, leading to a reduction trichome density and an increase in the number of root hairs (Zhao et al. 2012). MYC1 may destabilize GL1-GL3-TTG1 complex by interacting with GL1 and relocating GL1 from the nucleus to the cytoplasm and forming a complex with TTG1 (Pesch et al. 2013; Zhao et al. 2012). Consequently, AtMYC1 emerges as a positive regulator of GL2 and negative regulator of GL1-GL3-TTG1 activator complex, further underlining its signifcance in the intricate regulation of trichome and root hair development.

# Regulation of root growth

Apart from trichome and root hairs, JA-activated MYC2 was shown to inhibit primary root growth and root meristem activity by directly binding to the promoters of auxinresponsive PLETHORA 1 and 2 (PLT1/2), which encode AP2/EREBP TFs that regulate root stem cell niche maintenance and cell division (Chen et al. 2011). This is consistent with the previous observations that JA-mediated inhibition of root growth is suppressed in myc2 mutant plant and that MeJA-insensitive jin1 and jin4 mutants showed reduced root growth inhibition by MeJA (Berger et al. 1996); Lorenzo et al. 2004). Intriguingly, MYC2 mediates xylem

diferentiation and apical growth of roots in response to JA (Jang et al. 2017). One of the developmental mechanisms that plants employ to restrain their growth and survive environmental stresses could involve the inhibition of primary root growth as well as the promotion of xylem differentiation from meristematic cells. MYC2/3/4 negatively regulate adventitious root formation in Arabidopsis as indicated by the fact that myc2 mutant developed the higher average number of adventitious roots than wild type, with myc2myc3myc4 triple mutant having the highest number of adventitious roots (Gutierrez et al. 2012). In contrast, MYC2 positively regulates lateral root formation as a blue light (BL)-specifc repressor of photomorphogenesis (Yadav et al. 2005). This aligns with the observation that JA induces lateral root formation by promoting auxin biosynthesis (Sun et al. 2009). Since high activity of ABI5 inhibits lateral root formation (Skubacz et al. 2016), suppression of ABI activity by MED25 associated with MYCs would promote lateral root growth. Meanwhile, MED25 interacts with and assists ARF7 and 19 to activate the genes related to lateral root formation (Ito et al. 2016). JA-activated MYC2 binds to the promoter of SPA1 (SUPPRESSOR OF PHYTOCHROME A-105 1) and suppresses its expression. SPA1 is a key regulator in phytochrome signaling responsible for controlling circadian rhythms and transmitting red (R)/far-red (FR) light signal (Fig. 2). This adds to its combined impact on the regulation of lateral root development, consistent with the fnding that myc2 spa1 double mutants developed drastically fewer lateral roots compared to the wild type, with the double mutants exhibiting a similar number of lateral roots as myc2 single mutants (Gangappa et al. 2010).

In the dark, expression of ABI5 and ARFs is activated and suppressed, respectively, by both PIFs and EIN3/EIL1, and MYCs are destabilized. In the light, MYCs repress the activities of both PIFs and EIN3/EIL1 via physical interaction, while trans-activating the expression of DELLA gene, specifcally RGL3. SA-activated NPR1 interacts with EIN3/EIL1 to repress the activity of EIN3/EIL1. MED25 associated with MYCs represses the activity of ABI5, while activating ARF7 and ARF19 through physical interaction, thereby promoting lateral root formation t. PYL8/9 enhance PIF4 binding to the ABI5 promoter but inhibit PIF4-mediated activation of ABI5. HLS1 up-regulates the expression of ABI5, but down-regulates ARF2 in dark/shade conditions. In the absence of GA and presence of JA, high levels of stabilized DELLAs interact with JAZs and facilitate the release MYCs from JAZs-MYCs complex, thus activating JA response. GA and JA synergistically and mutually dependently regulate trichome formation by de-repressing DELLAs and JAZs repressors. However, excessive levels of DELLAs inhibit MYCs function to suppress JA signaling, whereas excessive levels of JAZs inhibit DELLAs function to suppress GA signaling. Dark/Shade triggers a rapid degradation

of DELLAs, which interact with PIFs to coordinate light and GA response. SA-activated NPR1 interacts with EIN3 to inhibit its binding to the promoter of HLS1. ARFs interact with AUX/IAA repressor, MYCs interacts with MED25 co-regulator, and PIFs interact with ABA receptor PYL8/9. MYC1 not only work together with GL1-GL3-TTG1 activator complex to transcriptionally activate GL2 but also destabilize L1-GL3-TTG1 complex. This type of repressor and activator activity of MYC1 could serve to fne-tune the expression of GL2. See the text for detail.

# Regulation of fowering time and fower morphogenesis

The fowering process is elaborately linked to light quality and intensity (Fig. 2). MYC2 and MYC3 can specifcally bind to the promoter of the forigen gene FLOWERING LOCUS T (FT), which promotes the transition from vegetative growth to fowering, thereby assuming a regulatory role in fower morphogenesis (Yu et al. 2023). PHYA was shown to be required for the morning FT expression (Lee et al. 2023). JA treatment is known to inhibit plant fowering by activating MYC2/3/4, which in turn represses the expression of genes associated with fowering process (Wang et al. 2017; Li et al. 2020). Especially, double mutants myc2myc3 and myc2myc4 exhibited an earlier fowering phenotype than wild type, while the triple mutant (myc2myc3myc4) displayed the earliest fowering time under both long-day and short-day conditions of high R/FR. However, single myc2 and double myc3/4 mutants showed the fowering times comparable to wild type. These results imply that the three MYCs additively regulate fowering time, with MYC2 playing a more important role in foral transition. The stability of MYC2 and MYC4 is mediated by CRY-PIFs and PHYB-PIFs signaling modules. Under white light (high R/FR), CRY1 and PHYB enter the nucleus to inhibit PIFs activity, leading to the stabilization of MYC2/4. In contrast, under shaded light (low R/FR), CRY1 and PHYB do not enter the nucleus, allowing PIFs to interact with and destabilize MYC2. Additionally, MYC2 and MYC4 bind to the promoter of NAC SECONDARY WALL THICKENING PRO-MOTING FACTOR1 (NTS1) to activate the NTS1-directed secondary cell walls (SCW) thickening of inforescence stem (Luo et al. 2022; Zhang et al. 2018e).

CONSTANS (CO) is a zinc fnger TF that promotes the onset of fowering under long-day conditions (Putterill et al. 1995). In Arabidopsis, during the light period in short days (SDs), low levels of GAs stabilize DELLAs and MYC3. Consequently, MYC3 binds to the FT promoter, preventing the low levels of CO from binding to the FT promoter and thereby suppressing FT expression. Conversely, during the light period of long days (LDs), elevated CO levels enable it to efectively bind to the $F T$ promoter, preventing binding of

the low levels of MYC3 resulting from less stabilized DEL-LAs and higher GA levels, and this facilitates FT expression (Bao et al. 2019). PHYB plays a pivotal role in light signal transduction, including modulation of the expression of $F T$ , particularly under suboptimal light conditions, by interacting with MED25, previously known as PHYTOCHROME AND FLOWERING TIME 1 (PFT1) (Cerdán and Chory 2003). MED25/PFT1 directly interacts with MYC2 (Çevik et al. 2012) and plays a pivotal role in regulating fowering by controlling the expression of $F T$ and CO as a transcriptional activator (Iñigo et al. 2012a). In high R/FR conditions, elevated active PHYB represses $C O$ transcription by stabilizing MED25/PET1, while PHYA and CRYPTOCHROME 1/2 (CRY1/2) enhance $C O$ transcription, leading to the activation of $F T$ transcription (Endo et al. 2013; Klose et al. 2012). Conversely, under low R/FR conditions, the opposite efects occur. Specifcally, proteasome-mediated degradation of MED25/PFT1 enhances its activity, resulting in increased transcription of $C O$ and FT (Iñigo et al. 2012b). Consequently, PHYB and PHYA, along with CRY1/2, antagonistically regulate the transcription of CO mRNA through MYCs associated with MED25/PET1 to serve as a molecular switch that regulates the timing of fowering in response to changes in light quality.

CONSTITUTIVELY PHOTOMORPHOGENIC1 (COP1) is an E3 ubiquitin ligase that targets several photomorphogenesis-promoting TFs for degradation (Li et  al. 2011). Its critical role in regulation of fowering time is mediated by interaction between the COP1-SPA1 complex and CO, which results in CO degradation via the 26S proteasome and thereafter decreases FT expression (Yu et al. 2023). During the light period in LDs, as COP1 is localized in the cytoplasm and CO is in the nucleus, CO remains stable and accumulates. BL-activated CRY1/2 directly interacts with the foral repressors TARGET OF EARLY ACTIVATION TAGGED 1 (TOE1) and TOE2 to suppress their repressor activities on FT by disrupting the interaction between TOE1/2 and CO, thereby promoting CO transcriptional activity of FT (Du et al. 2020). During the dark period in LDs, as COP1 and CO interact, CO undergoes ubiquitination and degradation, leading to reduced CO accumulation and thereafter the suppression of $F T$ transcription and fowering (Du et al. 2020). It has been reported that MYC2 directly binds to the SPA1 promoter and modulates the expression of SPA1 in a COP1-dependent manner, and that MYC2 works together with SPA1 as an integral component of COP1/ SPA1 E3 complex to regulate the fowering time under LDs (Gangappa and Chattopadhyay 2010). The interactions of CRY1/2 with COP1 and SPA1 result in the repression of the COP1/SPA complexes, thereby stabilizing CO to activate FT transcription for induction of fowering (Du et al. 2020). Hence, the abundance of CO protein is regulated through a combination of COP1-dependent degradation and

BL-dependent stabilization mediated by CRY1/2. Moreover, COP1 and PHYB have a role in regulating the stability and activity of MYC2 since COP1 and PHYB are required for dark-activated degradation and light-activated stability of MYC2, respectively (Chico et al. 2014). Consequently, MYC2 appears to act as phytochrome-mediated signal intermediaries by indirectly interacting with PHYB, PHYA, and CRY1/2 to integrate light signals and control the temporal expression of key fowering genes in response to changing light conditions and diurnal cycles.

Within the JA signaling pathway, receptor COI1 and repressor JAZ proteins take center stages in orchestrating the transition of fowering (Zhai et al. 2015). Notably, chestnut buds have high MeJA content before winter and high JA-Ile content after winter. Of particular interest is the positive correlation between the concentration of JA-Ile and FT expression, as well as between MYC2-1/2 and FT expression in post-winter (Cheng et al. 2022). JAZ1-3 and MYC2-1 proteins play a key role in the regulation of chestnut fowering transition, during which MYC2-1 promotes FT expression, while JAZ1-3 suppresses MYC2-1 expression, collectively contributing to the negative feedback regulation of chestnut fowering. What is more, MYC2/3/4/5 induce the expression of MYB21/24/57/108 and interact with the MYB 21/24 to form a transcriptional activator complex and cooperatively regulates development of stamen, calyx, pollen grain, anther dehiscence, and foral spikelet, ultimately afecting seed production (Cai et al. 2014; Figueroa and Browse 2015; Qi et al. 2015a). In the absence of JA, JAZ repressors interact with MYCs and repress the formation of MYCs-MYBs complex, thereby inhibiting the expression of downstream genes essential for fower development. Additionally, JAZ1/3/4/9 associate with TOE1/2 TFs to impede TOEs activities in Arabidopsis (Zhai et al. 2015). This repression of TOEs promotes the expression of $F T$ and accelerate fowering. This process could partly explain the fact that the expression of truncated forms of JAZ1 and JAZ4 (JAZ1ΔJas and JAZ4 ΔJas), which are resistant to degradation, resulted in earlier fowering than wild type (Oblessuc et al. 2020; Zhai et al. 2015). In the presence of JA under stress conditions, TOEs are released from JAZs, allowing them to repress the expression of FT and thereby delay fowering. In tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum), NtMYC2a assumes a crucial role in starch metabolism and the conversion of starch to sugar by regulating the expression of genes associated with starch metabolism, including ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase (AGP), starch synthase 2 (SS2), and $\beta$ -amylase 1 (BAM1) in pollen grains, anther walls, and ovaries during fower development (Bian et al. 2021). Overexpression of NtMYC2a signifcantly increased the starch content and accelerated the process of pollen germination and maturation, while the silencing of NtMYC2a resulted in a signifcant decrease of starch content in pollen grains.

# Regulation of photomorphogenesis, thermomorphogenesis, and skotomorphogenesis

The process of photomorphogenesis is intricately linked to light quality and intensity (Fig. 2). The inhibitory efect of white light (WL) on hypocotyl elongation of Arabidopsis seedlings is markedly enhanced with the increase of WL intensity, and light stimulates JA biosynthesis to activate MYC2/3/4 and inhibit hypocotyl elongation, a pivotal characteristic of seedling photomorphogenesis (Chico et al. 2014; Yi et al. 2020). The stabilities of both MYC2/3/4 and JAZ proteins are modulated by light spectrum, subsequently triggering the JA-mediated response (Hsieh and Okamoto 2014). Under conditions of darkness, MYC2/3/4 exhibit reduced stability, leading to the suppression of JA-mediated responses (Chico et al. 2014). Upon exposure to WL $( 5 4 \mu \mathrm { m o l } \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } ,$ ), myc2/3/4/5 quadruple mutants of Arabidopsis seedlings exhibit longer hypocotyls than wild types, suggesting that MYCs play a role in promoting photomorphogenesis (Ortigosa et al. 2020). ELONGATED HYPOCO-TYL 5 (HY5) is a basic leucine zipper (bZIP) TF that plays a pivotal role in photomorphogenesis across various wavelengths of light. It accomplishes this by integrating light and hormone signaling pathways, directly or indirectly controlling the expression of over 3000 genes (Gangappa and Botto 2016; Xiao et al. 2022). HY5 levels reach their peak during the early stages of seedling development upon light exposure, consistent with its function in facilitating photomorphogenesis (Hardtke 2000). The COP1 facilitates degradation of HY5 in the dark (Hardtke 2000), whereas MYC2/3/5 activate expression of HY5 under PHYB-dependent red light $( 3 2 ~ \mu \mathrm { m o l } \ \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \ \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ (Ortigosa et al. 2020), and MYC2/3/4 activate expression of HY5 in white light $( 2 5 \mu \mathrm { m o l } \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ ), blue $( 8 ~ \mu \mathrm { m o l } ~ \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } ~ \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ , red $( 6 ~ \mu \mathrm { m o l } \ \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \ \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ ), and far-red light $( 1 . 5 \mu \mathrm { m o l } \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ conditions (Yi et al. 2020).

Contrary to the positive role of MYCs in photomorphogenesis, it has been reported that Arabidopsis myc2 mutant seedlings exhibit signifcantly shorter hypocotyls in constant BL conditions $( 3 0 \mu \mathrm { m o l } \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ and develop signifcantly fewer lateral roots compared to the wild type (Yadav et al. 2005). This suggests that MYC2 plays a role in inhibiting photomorphogenesis and promoting skotomorphogenesis, since they are typically associated with shorter and longer hypocotyls, respectively. MYC2/3/5 bind to E-box cis-element in the promoter of HY5 and repress its expression primarily in BL $( 3 0 \mu \mathrm { m o l } \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ ), while HY5 inhibits the expression of MYC2 at various wavelengths, thereby forming a negative feedback loop in the regulation of BLinduced seedling development (Chakraborty et al. 2019). This would result in positive feedback on HY5 under BL $( 3 0 ~ \mu \mathrm { m o l } ~ \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { \ s } ^ { - 1 } )$ condition. Furthermore, MYC2 physically interacts with HY5 to regulate the activity of HY5 (Chakraborty et al. 2019). MYC2, which is constitutively

expressed in dark and light-grown Arabidopsis seedlings (Yadav et al. 2005), was shown to negatively regulate HY5 expression in both photosynthetic and non-photosynthetic tissues, and the activity of the HY5 promoter was found to be maximally increased in myc2 mutants under BL $( 3 0 \mu \mathrm { m o l } \ \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } ,$ ) condition (Chakraborty et al. 2019). In addition, MYC2 and HY5 work antagonistically as a negative and positive regulator, respectively, to control accumulation of chlorophyll, light-harvesting pigments, and anthocyanin, as well as light-inducible gene expression (Chakraborty et al. 2019; Yadav et al. 2005). Thus, MYC2 plays dual roles as both a negative or positive regulator of BL-mediated photomorphogenesis by repressing and activating HY5 expression. This dual regulation appears to be dependent on diferent BL intensities. Specifcally when the light intensity is $3 0 \ \mathrm { \mu m o l \ m ^ { - 2 } \ \mathrm { s ^ { - 1 } } }$ , MYC2 acts as a negative regulator (Chakraborty et al. 2019; Yadav et al. 2005), whereas at a lower intensity of 8 or $1 9 \mu \mathrm { m o l } \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 }$ , MYC2 functions as a positive regulator (Chico et al. 2014; Yi et al. 2020). This suggests that the regulatory interaction between MYC2 and HY5 is sensitive to the specifc conditions of BL exposure. MYC2 may directly down-regulate HY5 to prioritize defense responses over photomorphogenesis or interact with other TFs to form heterodimers that negatively infuence HY5 (Koene et al. 2022). Consequently, the regulatory relationship between MYC2 and HY5 is most likely to be highly context-dependent. The balance between positive and negative regulation may be infuenced by factors such as light quality, intensity, and duration, as well as other abiotic and biotic stresses. The intricate nature of these interactions highlights the complexity of plant signaling networks and their ability to integrate diverse environmental signals for optimal responses. Meanwhile, independently of MYC2, a B-box (BBX)-containing zinc fnger TF named BBX21 is recognized for its direct binding to the HY5 promoter, activating its expression under various light conditions (white, blue, red, and far-red) to promote photomorphogenesis (Xu et al. 2016a). In darkness, BBX21 undergoes destabilization by COP1 through its E3 ubiquitin ligase activity. Since a low BL intensity of $6 . 3 7 \ \mathrm { \mu m o l \ m } ^ { - 2 } \ \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 }$ was used to test the efect of BBX21 overexpression on photomorphogenesis, it is not yet clear whether BBX21 acts as a negative regulator at higher BL intensities, like MYC2.

Besides PHYB, BL-BL-activated CRY1/2 not only stabilize MYC2 (and likely MYC3 and MYC4), but also stabilize HY5 indirectly by suppressing the repressor activity of SPA1/COP1 complex to promote photomorphogenesis in BL (Chico et al. 2014; Hardtke 2000; Ponnu and Hoecker 2022). The transmission of BL signals to MYC2 was found to be mediated by mitogen-activated protein kinase (MAPK) signaling cascade (Sethi et al. 2014). In this pathway, BLactivated MKK3 phosphorylates MPK6, which subsequently phosphorylates MYC2 at the S123 motif, leading to its

activation. Concurrently, the phosphorylated MYC2 engages in a feedback loop regulation mechanism in which the activated MYC2 can either enhance MPK6 activity or suppress the expression of MPK6 by binding its promoter. In the light, unphosphorylated HY5 actively binds to the promoters of light-responsive genes, enhancing photomorphogenesis. In the dark, SPA1 physically interacts with COP1 to enhance its activity, facilitating the ubiquitination-mediated degradation of unphosphorylated HY5 and thereby inhibiting photomorphogenesis (Lu et al. 2015; Wang et al. 2021b). In addition, SPAs phosphorylate HY5, and the phosphorylated HY5 has less afnity for the COP1–SPA complex, making it relatively more stable. Thus, seedlings can maintain a small pool of HY5 in the dark. Meanwhile, in the dark, MYC2 directly binds to the promoter of SPA1 and inhibits its expression, coupled with the attenuated expression of HY5 due to the decreased access of MYC2 to HY5 promoter (Gangappa et al. 2010). Consequently, when plants are in dark conditions, MYC2 and SPA1 together hinder the light-responsive morphological growth, particularly suppressing BL-mediated morphological growth by repressing gene expression regulated by BL (Gangappa and Chattopadhyay 2010). At high temperature, SPA1 is stimulated to not only directly phosphorylate HY5 to prevent its degradation from COP1/ SPA1 complex (Lee et al. 2021), but also promote destabilization of PHYB and phosphorylation of PHYOTCHROME INTERACTING FACTOR 4 (PIF4) (Lee et al. 2020) The phosphorylated PIF4 promotes hypocotyl elongation at elevated temperatures. The phosphorylated HY5 controls the expression of a set of genes associated with auxin, brassinosteroid, and gibberellin pathways to control both shoot and root thermomorphogenesis (Lee et al. 2021). Thus, as a downstream phytochrome signaling mediator, MYCs work together with SPA1 to promote thermomorphogenesis. In response to warm temperatures, HY5 is up-regulated only in the root tissue to promote root thermomorphogenesis, whereas PIF4 is up-regulated only in the shoot tissue to enhance shoot thermomorphogenesis (Lee et al. 2021). On the other hand, low temperatures suppress the COP1-mediated degradation of CRY2, and the cold-stabilized, phosphorylated CRY2 disrupts the COP1-HY5 interaction, thereby promoting HY5 accumulation in Arabidopsis seedlings in the light (Li et al. 2021b).

Apart from CRY1/2 dedicated to sensing BL, PHYA and PHYB, primarily responsive to far-red and red light, respectively, promote photomorphogenic development through signaling pathways that involve rapid degradation of bHLH TFs PIFs and suppression of nuclear accumulation of COP1 (Lu et al. 2015). In the daylight, the suppression of COP1-SPA1 complex is mediated by the physical association of the active Pfr (far-red absorbing) form of PHYA and PHYB with SPA1. In addition to PHYA/B-mediated inhibition of the repressor activity of COP1-SPA complex

and MYCs-mediated transcriptional inhibition of SPA1 in the light, JA-activated MYC2 additively contributes to the repression of activity of PIFs via physical interaction with PIFs in the light (Zhang et al. 2018c). It is noteworthy that both MYC2 and PIF3/4/5 share the ability to bind to the identical G-box sequence motif $5 ^ { \prime }$ -CACGTG- $. 3 ^ { \prime }$ (López-Vidriero et al. 2021; Toledo-Ortiz et al. 2003). This implies the potential for transcriptional activation and repression of the same target gene promoters by MYC2 and PIFs, contingent upon the levels of their proteins, which are infuenced by light intensity. In the dark, the COP1/SPA complex is stabilized to promote the stability of PIFs, which are abundant in the dark and primarily function as negative regulators of photomorphogenesis but as positive regulators of skotomorphogenesis that inhibits light-responsive genes in darkness (Pham et al. 2018). For instance, PIFs function in maintaining apical kook formation in etiolated seedlings by transactivating the expression of HOOKLESS (HLS1), a critical gene encoding histone acetyltransferase required for apical hook development (Fig. 1). HLS1 directly interacts with the ABI5 sequence, mediates H3 acetylation, and positively regulates ABI5 expression. (Liao et al. 2016). Germination and seedling growth of the hls1 mutant are insensitive to ABA, whereas the HLS1 overexpression line is hypersensitive to ABA. In addition, PIF4 interacts with ABA receptors PLY8 and PYL9, which enhance PIF4 binding to the ABI5 promoter but negatively regulate PIF4-mediated ABI5 activation during seedling stage in darkness (Qi et al. 2020). This regulation promotes seed germination, seedling growth, and lateral root development. Independently of PIFs, two TFs, EIN3 and EIL1 involved in ethylene signaling, transactivate HLS1 expression (An et al. 2012). In contrast, SAactivated NPR1 interacts with EIN3 to inhibit its binding to the promoter of HLS1, thereby suppressing apical hook formation (Huang et al. 2020a, b). Moreover, MYC2/3/4 were shown to physically interact with and repress EIN3 and EIL1 to impede apical hook development (Song et al. 2014). This aligns with the observations that light promotes the stability of MYCs, as well as enhances the degradation of EIN3/EIL1 and PIFs (Chico et al. 2014; Dong et al. 2017; Shi et al. 2016). In darkness, active PIFs induce the expression of genes involved in the biosynthesis and signaling of phytohormones such as ET, GA, and auxin, as well as other signals associated with skotomorphogenesis (Deepika et al. 2020).

Parallel to MYCs-mediated repression of EIN3/EIL1 and PIFs activities (Zhang et al. 2018c), DELLA proteins, which acts as negative regulators in gibberellin (GA) signaling, physically interact with MYCs, JAZs, PIFs and EIN3/ EIL1 to inhibit their activities (Hong et al. 2012). Increased levels of JA and GA result in decreased levels of JAZs and DELLAs, releasing a fully active MYC2. Meanwhile, auxin induces the expression of auxin-responsive genes

through the degradation of AUXIN/INDOLE-3-ACETIC ACID (Aux/IAA) repressors that interact with AUXIN RESPONSE FACTOR (ARF) (Leyser 2018) (Fig. 3). This combined action would accelerate vegetative shoot growth. Accordingly, MYCs links diferent photosensory and hormonal pathways to control plant growth and development.

MYCs work with the MKK3-MPK6 signal cascade through a feedback loop mechanism in which BL-activated MPK6 phosphorylates MYCs, and the phosphorylated MYCs either activate MPK6 activity or inhibit MPK6 transcript accumulation to regulate photomorphogenic growth and gene expression. CRY1/2, PHYA, and PHYB photoreceptors transduce the light signals to HY5 through suppression of SPA1/COP1 activity, a potent inhibitor of HY5 function. In the light, unphosphorylated HY5 actively binds to its target promoters of light responsive genes, enhancing photomorphogenesis. In the dark, the stabilized SPA-COP1 complex degrades HY5. Phosphorylated CRY2, stabilized by cold conditions, disrupts the interaction between COP1 and HY5, thereby facilitating the accumulation of HY5 in the presence of light. In shade light conditions, the ratio of R to FR decreases because photosynthetic pigments in plant leaves strongly absorb R $4 5 0 \ \mathrm { n m } )$ ) and BL $( 6 5 0 \ \mathrm { n m } )$ for photosynthesis, and the resulting shade causes an enrichment of FR. PIFs are stabilized by SPA1-COP1 complex in the dark but are degraded in the light. High levels of PHYA are present in etiolated seedlings and after prolonged periods of darkness, whereas the abundance of PHYA decreases drastically and rapidly following illumination with red or white light. The shade light reduces PHYB and CRY1/2 activities and increases PHYA activity, which allows the stabilized PIFs to directly activate the transcription of auxinsynthesis genes, leading to the suppression of photomorphogenesis and the stimulation of shade avoidance syndrome responses of skotomorphogenesis. In light-grown plants, PHYB becomes the most abundant phytochrome. MYC2 is expressed in the dark and at various wavelengths of light at the seedling stage. MYC2 acts as not only a negative regulator of BL-mediated photomorphogenesis in which hypocotyl elongation as part of skotomorphogenesis is typically inhibited, but also a positive regulator of fowering morphogenesis and fowering time. MYCs may act in concert with CRY1/2 and PHYB to promote HY5 activity while inhibiting the activity of PIFs, modulating plant growth and development in response to light conditions. MYC2 not only binds to the promoter of HY5 to activate the expression of HY5, but also physically interacts with HY5 to inhibit the activity of HY5. Both HY5 and MYC2 can bind to the promoter of HY5 to regulate its expression. Regulation of HY5 transcription and HY5 activity by MYC2 may vary depending on the stages of plant development, as well as the specifc environmental conditions including light quality and quantity. As a positive regulator of photomorphogenic

light signaling, BBX21 directly binds to HY5 promoter and activates its expression. As a thermosensor, PHYB directly inhibits PIF4 and indirectly stabilizes HY5 to coordinately regulate shoot and root thermomorphogenesis. The PFT1/ MED25 promotes fowering by inducing the expression of $C O$ and FT. MYCs induces the expression of MYBs and interacts with MYBs to form a transcriptional activator complex and regulate the development of fowers. JAZs act as a positive regulator of fowering time by repressing the activity of TOEs, which inhibit the expression of $F T$ . The interaction between MYCs and MYBs is disrupted by JAZ repressors under the conditions that elicit JA production. In the absence of GA, DELLAs directly interact with and inhibit JAZs and MYCs. MYCs attain full activation only in the presence of both GA and JA (as referenced in Section "MYCs in JA-GA crosstalk signaling"). See the text for further details.

# Regulation of leaf senescence

Leaf senescence represents the culmination of plant leaf development and is infuenced by a range of environmental stresses and phytohormones. As a plant enters a phase of leaf senescence, MYC2/3/4 mediate JA-induced leaf senescence by directly transactivating the G-box-containing promoter of SENESCENCE-ASSOCIATED GENE 29 (SAG29) to facilitate the onset of leaf senescence, while JAM1/2/3/14 (bLHLH03/13/14/17) TFs bind to the promoter of SAG29 and repress its expression to attenuate MYCs-activated JAinduced leaf senescence (Qi et al. 2015b). While MYC5 promotes JA-induced leaf senescence, it’s worth noting that its expression does not respond to JA, unlike MYC2/3/4 (Song et al. 2017). The activities of both MYCs and JAMs are inhibited by JAZ repressors in the absence of JA (Fonseca et al. 2014). The evening complex (EC), a core component of the Arabidopsis circadian clock, directly binds to the promoter of MYC2 and repress its expression, thereby inhibiting leaf senescence (Zhang et al. 2018d). This is in line with the observation that in rice, overexpression of OsMYC2 under darkness considerably enhanced leaf senescence and loss of chlorophyll content (Uji et al. 2017).

The Dof (DNA binding with one fnger) protein, a plantspecifc transcription factor, plays a critical role in leaf senescence in Arabidopsis (Yanagisawa 2002). Notably, the deletion of Dof2.1 signifcantly attenuates JA-induced leaf senescence and root growth inhibition. Dof2.1 actively promotes JA-induced leaf senescence by enhancing the expression of MYC2. Interestingly, MYC2 and Dof2.1 exert a positive transcriptional regulation on each other’s activities (Zhuo et al. 2020). Consistent with this fnding, Atmyc2 mutant exhibits a higher level of chlorophyll and anthocyanin accumulation compared to the wild type (Yadav et al. 2005).

Degreening caused by chlorophyll degradation is a major feature of senescence. In Arabidopsis, MYC2/3/4 bind to and trans-activate the promoter of PAO gene encoding PHENO-PHORIDE A OXYGENASE, a key enzyme of chlorophyll breakdown (Zhu et al. 2015). In addition, MYC2/3/4 induce expression of other chlorophyll catabolic genes (NYE1/2, NYC1) either directly or indirectly via the mediators of ANAC TFs, ANAC019/055/072. Expression of tomato PAO, SIPAO, is also up-regulated by SlMYC2 during JAinduced leaf senescence (Ding et al. 2022a). Additionally, the genes encoding key enzymes in the carbon fxation pathway, Rubisco activase (RCA) and sedoheptulose-1,7-bisphosphatase (SBPase) were signifcantly down-regulated during JA- and dark-induced leaf senescence in both tomato and Arabidopsis (Ding et al. 2018; Shan et al. 2011). Beyond their roles in photosynthesis, these enzymes infuence various aspects of plant biology, including growth and development. The defciency of RCA, for instance, leads to premature leaf decay in plants. Considering that RCA promoter contain G-box sequences, and RCA transcripts are decreased upon JA treatment, MYCs might participate in negatively regulating the expression of RCA during leaf senescence in plants (Fig. 1).

MYCs induce the expression of Dof1.2, and Dof1.2 activates the expression of MYCs. Thereby, JA-induced leaf senescence is enhanced through a forward transcriptional feedback loop involving MYC2 and Dof2.1. The EC transcriptionally represses the expression of MYCs as a negative regulator of leaf senescence. In the absence of JA-Ile, both MYCs and JAMs compete for the cis-acting G-box elements in the promoter of SAG29. Therefore, the resulting response will depend on the balance of activity between them. See the text for further details.

# Regulation of fruit ripening

ET plays a key role in climacteric fruit ripening (Li et al. 2017b). In apple (Malus domestica), MdMYC2 promotes the expression of ET biosynthesis genes encoding 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid synthase (ACS1) and 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid oxidase (ACO1) to facilitate fruit ripening (Hu et al. 2022). In tomato (Solanum lycopersicum), fruit frmness and the contents of soluble sugars, carotenoid, lycopene, and favonoids were signifcantly enhanced in the SlMYC2 overexpressing plants, and SIMYC2 binds to the promoter of TARGET OF RAPA-MYCIN (TOR) and activates TOR expression to regulate tomato growth and fruit quality (Zhang et al. 2022). ABA is assumed to be the main hormone that positively regulates non-climacteric fruit ripening (Li et al. 2022a; Wang et al. 2022b). With respect to other hormones than ET, the role of MYCs in fruit development and ripening process in non-climacteric fruits has not been explored to date, despite

the fact that ABA activates the transcription of MYC2 and enhances the binding of the ABA receptor PYL6 to MYC2 (Aleman et al. 2016).

# Function of MYCs in plant biotic stresses

# The role of MYCs in resisting insect pests

In response to herbivorous insect feeding that very commonly cause wounding, plants synthesize JA very quickly from linolenic acid released from membrane lipids to deploy a range of defense mechanisms. These strategies include physical barriers, the production of antidigestive proteins, and the synthesis of diverse secondary metabolites (Erb and Reymond 2019; Wari et al. 2022). In addition to JA, cell wall-derived oligosaccharides (OGAs) and ET are typically generated in response to injury. Solanaceous plants utilizes a signaling network involving JA, OGAs, and ET to express wound-induced and defense-related genes, both locally in the damaged area and in non-damaged distal leaves. However, in Arabidopsis, OGAs stimulate the production of ET, which subsequently inhibits JA-induced gene expression in the locally damaged leaves, while JA induces expression of wound-responsive genes in non-damaged systemic leaves (León et al. 2001). Notably, Arabidopsis MYC2 (AtMYC2) diferentially regulates the expression of two sets of JAinduced genes; it represses genes associated with pathogen defense responses while simultaneously activating systemic wound response genes (Lorenzo et al. 2004). This nuanced expression would aid the plant in protecting the damaged tissues from secondary microbial infections and the nondamaged tissues from additional herbivorous feeding. This notion is supported by the observations that myc2/3/4 triple mutants (mycT) showed heightened susceptibility to a lepidopteran herbivore and resistance to bacterial pathogen infection, respectively, whereas jaz1/3/4/9/10 quintuple mutant (jazQ), displaying constitutive JA responses with derepressed MYCs, exhibited the opposite phenotype (Campos et al. 2016; Major et al. 2017; Schweizer et al. 2013a). However, unlike the jazQ mutant, quadruple jaz1/2/5/6 mutants were found to be more resistant to necrotrophic pathogens but not insect herbivores compared to the wild type (Johnson et al. 2023). Thus, the functional diversifcation within the JAZ family allows for fne-tuned regulation of JA responses, enabling the plants to respond appropriately to various types of biotic stress. The jazD mutant defective in $1 0 ~ J A Z$ genes displayed strong resistance to insect herbivores and fungal pathogens, along with reduced vegetative (shoot and root) growth and impaired reproductive performance (Guo et al. 2018; Johnson et al. 2023). Genetic epistasis analyses of a jazD mycT tredecuple mutant demonstrated that MYC TFs impart robust resistance to insect

herbivory, albeit with compromised vegetative growth and reproductive performance (Guo et al. 2022). The dual transcriptional capability of AtMYC2, functioning either as activators or repressors, may be achieved by recruiting or interacting with diferent TFs as co-regulators. In contrast, tomato (Solanum lycopersicum) MYC2 (SlMYC2) positively regulates both wound response genes and pathogen response genes (Du et al. 2017; Wu et al. 2024). In rice (Oryza sativa), OsMYC2 interacts with OsMYL1 and OsMYL2, which closely resemble the bHLH-type AtJAMs known to function as repressors to negatively regulate JA signaling without interaction with AtMYC2 (Putterill et al. 1995; Sekimoto et al. 2013a). Unlike AtMYC2, transactivation activity of OsMYC2 is enhanced by the interaction with OsMYLs. Thus, although the regulatory role and interaction partners of MYC2 may exhibit variations across diferent plant species in plant–insect interaction, MYCs are crucial in balancing the trade-of between growth and defense (Fig. 4). While most plant secondary metabolites, as described in Sect. "MYCs-mediated signaling in plant secondary metabolites biosynthesis", exhibit some degree of anti-insect properties, their efectiveness and primary biological roles can vary signifcantly. Here, we specifcally focus on the role of MYCs in regulating secondary metabolites and proteins primarily involved in insect herbivory resistance.

# MYCs in regulation of glucosinolates

Glucosinolates (GS) constitute a diverse group of sulfurrich secondary compounds present in cruciferous plants, exhibiting repellent or deterrent efects on herbivorous insects upon hydrolysis of GS driven by herbivore attacks (Liu et al. 2021c). The biosynthesis GS can be induced by mechanical damage via JA signaling. Recently, it was demonstrated that GS biosynthesis in Arabidopsis depends on the COP1/SPA-mediated degradation of DELLAs, which can inhibit MYCs function and suppress JA signaling (Frerigmann et al. 2021). The triple mycT mutant was almost devoid of both aliphatic and indolic GS and was highly susceptible to African cotton leafworm (Spodoptera littoralis) (Schweizer et al. 2013a, b). In contrast, leaves of the jazD mutant accumulated increased levels of nearly all indolic GS derivative (Guo et al. 2022). These fndings suggest that MYC2/3/4 are required for transcriptional activation of GS biosynthesis genes to promote insect resistance (Schweizer et al. 2013a, b; Guo et al. 2022). Although MYC2/3/4 can form complexes with six GSrelated R2R3-MYB TFs to coordinately regulate aliphatic and indolic GS biosynthesis, expression of MYBs is not regulated by MYCs (Schweizer et al. 2013a, b). The MYBs are crucial for determining the target specifcity of the MYCs-MYBs complex-activated GS biosynthesis pathways (Frerigmann 2016). Competitive binding between

![](images/ac62d0122ff7b5be8283bcb395c48a83c6d764e76ae1c794ae1e05868d08b17e.jpg)  
Fig. 4 Schematic diagram illustrating the role of MYCs in providing resistance against herbivorous pests. MYBs and JAZs to MYCs may regulate the magnitude of GS biosynthesis. Contrary to the triple mutants, single myc2 (i.e., jin1) mutant plant showed strongly elevated levels of tryptophan and indolic GS (IG) in response to MeJA treatment (Dombrecht et  al. 2007), suggesting that MYC2 negatively regulates IG biosynthesis. In fact, MYC2 was found to bind to the promoters of 14 GS biosynthesis genes, including the core biosynthesis pathway genes for indolic (CYP79B3 and SOT16) and aliphatic GS (IPMDH1, BCAT4) (Schweizer et al. 2013a, b). Hence, MYC3 and MYC4 able to bind to GS-related MYBs exert stronger positive efects on IG biosynthesis in the absence of MYC2. This notion is consistent with the fnding that MYC3 and MYC4 are the major regulators of JA-induced biosynthesis of tryptophan from which IG is derived (Johnson et al. 2023). Proteomic analysis of myc2 mutant and wild-type plants treated with MeJA revealed that MYC2 exerts both negative and positive efects on enzymes involved in indolic glucosinolate biosynthesis and the myrosinase system, respectively (Guo et al. 2012). This system plays a role in producing toxic compounds through the breakdown of stored glucosinolates by myrosinase, serving as a defense mechanism against herbivores and pathogens.
