# MYCs in regulation of auxin

Two YUCCA family genes, YUC8 and YUC9, which encode favin-containing monooxygenases, catalyze the rate-limiting irreversible oxidative decarboxylation of indole-3-pyruvate acid to form indole-3-acetic acid (IAA) as the main route of auxin biosynthesis in higher plants (Cao et al. 2019). These genes are induced by JA through MYC2/3/4 that bind to the G-box motif-containing promoters of YUC8 and YUC9 when plants experience mechanical damage (Pérez-Alonso et al. 2021). The magnitude of expression of these two genes primarily depends on the abundance of MYC2, along with additive contribution of MYC3/4. Expression of YUC8/9 in myc2 knockout mutants is strongly reduced, whereas overexpression of YUC9 rendered Arabidopsis plants more resistant to the spider mites (Tetranychus urticae) that feed by piercing leaf tissue and sucking up plant fuids. This suggests that MYC2 plays a major role in resistance to wound-induced biotic stress processes in Arabidopsis by up-regulating YUC9 expression (Pérez-Alonso et al. 2021). Although the mechanism through which YUC8/9 expression confers resistance to spider mites remains unclear, it has been speculated that increased ET production triggered by IAA-responsive ACS (ACC synthase) gene causes elevated

lignin contents, enhancing leaf cell rigidity and reducing palatability.

# MYCs in regulation of nicotine, steroidal glycoalkaloids, and phenylpropanoid‑polyamine conjugates

Nicotine produced by tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum) acts as a broad-spectrum toxin against insects and plays a pivotal role in plant response to biotic stress. Activated by JA, NtMYC2 not only binds to the G-box element in the promoter of PMT2 (Putrescine N-Methyltransferase 2) to activate its transcription but also induces NIC2-locus ERF genes comprising at least seven closely related ET response factors either directly or indirectly. The ERF189 and ERF221 proteins, which recognize the GCC-box in the proximal promoter regions of the PMT2 and QPT2 (Quinolinate Phosphoribosyltransferase 2), collaborate with NtMYC2 for maximum transactivation of the genes (Zhang et al. 2012; Shoji and Hashimoto 2011). ORCA1, one of the ERF genes, was mainly expressed in the roots of tobacco plants and peaked at 30 min after MeJA elicitation, and this was followed by increased transcript levels of PMT and QPRT (quinolinate phosphoribosyltransferase) over a period of $^ { 2 4 \mathrm { h } }$ (Boer et al. 2011).

Solanaceae family plants accumulate phenylpropanoidpolyamine conjugates (PPCs) in response to insect and pathogen attacks, while they constitutively produce protective metabolites of steroidal glycoalkaloids (SGAs). In tomato (Solanum lycopersicum) SlMYC1 and SlMYC2 redundantly increase PPCs biosynthesis by up-regulating ODC (ornithine decarboxylase) expression, and SlMYC1/2 increase constitutive SGA biosynthesis by up-regulating C5-SD2 (sterol C-5(6) desaturase 2) expression (Swinnen et al. 2022). SlMYC1/2 interact with AP2-ERF TF GAME9 (GLYCOALKALOID METABOLISM 9) also known as JA-Responsive ERF4 (JRE4), and they act synergistically to transactivate the promoter of C5-SD2 to increase biosynthesis of steroidal alkaloids (Cárdenas et al. 2016; Swinnen et al. 2022). Competitive binding between GAME9 and JAZs to SlMYC1/2 regulates the biosynthesis of SGA, and this competition is diminished greatly upon JA elicitation because of proteasomal degradation of JAZs. SlMYC1/2 may inhibit GA biosynthesis by inducing the transcription of GA catabolism genes (SIGA2ox5 and $S I G A 2 o x 7 )$ ) or, specifcally in the case of SlMYC1, by suppressing the transcription of GA biosynthetic gene (SIGA20ox2) (Panda et al. 2022). This modulation serves to control GA-mediated plant growth under stressful conditions. A similar regulatory mechanism is known to occur in Arabidopsis plants where ERF5/6 induce expression of GA oxidase genes (GA2-OX 5/6) (Dubois et al. 2013). Additionally, MYCs can infuence GA signaling by regulating the transcription of DELLA, thereby playing a role in the modulation of GA-mediated plant growth.

# MYCs in regulation of monoterpene linalool

In rice (Oryza sativa), a vast array of JA-responsive genes is transcriptionally regulated by OsMYC2, and a bHLH-type TF, RERJ1, is indispensable for the inductive transcriptional expression of multiple JA-responsive genes. The inductive expression of RERJ1 peaked at 30 min after wounding, and RERJ1 specifcally induced proteinase inhibitor (PI) genes and terpene synthase (TPS) family genes including OsLIS (OsTPS3) encoding a linalool synthase (Valea et al. 2022). The expression of RERJ1 is independent of OsMYC2 expression since its transcript level remains unchanged in osmyc2-RNAi line after wounding, while OsMYC2 expression is induced in the rerj1 mutant. However, RERJ1 physically interacts with the OsMYC2 and OsJAZ proteins. Because OsLIS, PIs, and JAZs are regulated by OsMYC2, RERJ1 may work coordinately with OsMYC2 to activate the RERJ1 downstream target genes OsLIS and PIs. PIs inhibit insect digestion, whereas volatile emission of monoterpene linalool not only attracts the natural enemies of herbivore but also indirectly induces defense-related PRs (PATHO-GENESIS-RELATED), including genes for chitinases, $\beta$ -1,3- glucanases, trypsin inhibitor, and peroxidases (Arimura et al. 2009; Taniguchi et al. 2014). In response to brown planthopper (Nilaparvata lugens) feeding on rice plants, OsMYC2 binds directly to the promoter of OsCslF6 and induces its transcription; its encoded protein produces mixed-linkage $\beta$ -1,3;1,4-d-glucans (MLGs) that reinforce cell wall thickness in vascular bundles and stimulate OsLecRK1 (Lectin receptor kinase 1)-mediated defense signaling (Dai et al. 2023). In Arabidopsis, MYC2 interact with PIF3/4, and both PIF3 and MYC2 can directly bind to the TPS promoter and synergistically regulate its expression to promote terpene production (Zhao et al. 2021).

# MYCs in regulation of anti‑insect proteins

Proteinase inhibitors (PIs) inhibit the digestive enzymes of insects, impairing their ability to digest food. Upon herbivore feeding, JA-activated MYCs promotes accumulation PIs (Erb and Reymond 2019; Wari et al. 2022). Arginase (ARG) and threonine deaminase (TD) interfere with insect nutrition and digestion in the gut of lepidopteran herbivore (Chen et al. 2005). In tomato, MYC2 directly activates the transcription of PLANT U-BOX 22 (PUP22), which encodes a plant U-box type E3 ubiquitin ligase that physically interacts with and ubiquitinates JAZ1/3/4/6 proteins to promote their degradation via the 26S proteasome pathway. This, in turn, induces the expression of defense genes PI-I, ARG2, and TD2 to confer resistance against Helicoverpa armigera (Wu et al. 2024). VSPs are proteinaceous storage reserves identifed in various plant species. Apart from the temporary storage of amino acids, it was discovered that Arabidopsis

VSP2 encodes an acid phosphatase enzyme with anti-insect activity (Liu et al. 2005). VSP1/2 expression is induced via JA signaling pathway upon exposure to MeJA and ABA, as well as abiotic stresses including salt, drought, and wounding. Transcriptome analysis of Arabidopsis plants injured by insect herbivory revealed that MYC2/3/4 are main regulators of resistance to a generalist herbivore by activating 41 TFs, including ANAC019/055 that induce expression of numerous downstream target genes (Schweizer et al. 2013a). MYCs-activated expression of VSP1/2 is described elsewhere (refer to Fig. 5). Sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas Lam.) produces sporamin, a tuberous storage protein with trypsin inhibitory activity that confers insect resistance, and its gene expression in leaves is induced by IbNAC1 TF in response to herbivore attack (Chen et al. 2016).

In rice, RERJ1 collaborates with OsMYC2 to induce expression of the genes for linalool biosynthesis. Similar to OsMYC2 transactivating activity, RERJ1 transactivating transcriptional activity is controlled through binding to OsJAZ repressors. Brown planthopper’s feeding on rice plants triggers OsMYC2-mediated expression of OsCslF6, resulting in strengthening cell wall. MYCs stimulate the biosynthesis of anthocyanins by up-regulating DFR. In tobacco,

NtMYC2 directly up-regulates PMT2, QPPT2, and ERFs and works with ERF189/221 to maximally trans-activate PMT2 and QPPT2, leading to high production of nicotine. Herbivore attack triggers JA biosynthesis and JA signaling pathway that induces MYC2-driven IAA biosynthesis via activation of YUC8/9. VSPs are induced by MYCs-activated ANAC TFs upon perception of JA generated by insect wounding stress. The target specifcity of MYCs is governed by their interaction with R2R3-MYB TFs in GS biosynthesis. Competitive binding of JAZs with MYBs disrupts the transcriptional activity of MYCs-MYBs complex to regulate the magnitude of GS biosynthesis. Similarly, competitive binding of JAZs with GAME9 interferes with the activities of SlMYCs to regulate the extent of SGA biosynthesis, and this competition is diminished greatly upon JA elicitation because of proteasomal degradation of JAZs. In Solanaceae plants, SlMYCs directly trans-activate the promoter of ODC to induce accumulation of PPCs. In tomato SIMYCs may decrease endogenous GA level by inducing GA catabolic genes $( G A 2 o c 5 / 7 )$ and repressing GA biosynthetic gene (GA20ox2) to inhibit plant growth under stress challenges. In addition, MYCs may up-regulate DELLAs to suppress GA signaling-mediated plant growth. When stress challenges

![](images/bed6287fe95b8ec076120c77aa4502f3eedf1f277b4477b86f556ae7b38b40fd.jpg)  
Fig. 5 Schematic diagram of synergistic and antagonistic interactions between JA, ET, SA, GA, and ABA signaling pathways during pathogen infection (based on well-known concepts from Arabidopsis)

are relieved, DELLAs interact with MYCs to attenuate the activities of MYCs that trigger expression of biosynthesis genes for specialized secondary metabolites, as well as GA metabolic genes. The reciprocal inhibition between DELLA and JAZs may additionally play a role in maintaining the trade-of balance between growth and defense. MYCs initiate a feedback loop by inducing the expression of JAZs, thereby auto-regulating the synthesis of defense metabolites controlled by MYCs and coordinating the primary and secondary metabolism. As a central repressor of light signaling in darkness, the COP1/SPA complex plays a crucial role in balancing the JA-GA phytohormone network. The absence of the COP1/SPA complex can potentially increase DELLA protein abundance, which subsequently represses both GA and JA signaling and inhibits MYCs. See the text for further details.

# The role of MYCs in resisting microbial infection

In Arabidopsis, ethylene treatment to the wild-type plants signifcantly suppressed AtMYC2 expression relative to the untreated plants; myc2 mutants exhibited fve-fold higher expression level of PDF1.2 and increased resistance to the necrotrophic Fusarium oxysporum; ethylene treatment to the myc2 mutants resulted in a tenfold higher induction of PDF1.2 (Anderson et al. 2004). Transcriptomic and proteomic analyses of MeJA-treated and untreated myc2 mutants and wild-type plants indicated that MYC2 acts as a positive regulator of insect defense, wound responses, favonoid metabolism, carbohydrate metabolism, and oxidative stress tolerance, while negatively regulating the expression of pathogen defense and glucosinolate biosynthesis genes during JA signaling (Dombrecht et al. 2007; Guo et al. 2012).

Besides herbivore attack, microbial infection evokes rapid accumulation of ROS followed by enhanced biosynthesis of defense hormones jasmonic acid (JA), ethylene (ET), salicylic acid (SA), and abscisic acid (ABA) at various levels in a time-dependent and pathogen-specifc manner. The accumulation of these hormones and their responsiveness is intricately adjusted by external factors such as light quality, time of day, and previous or concurrent interactions with abiotic stresses, followed by experiencing specifc shifts in other phytohormones’ responses as well (Aerts et al. 2021). The age of plant also afects the susceptibility to pathogens (Barna 2022). Necrotrophic pathogens, which produce cytotoxins and cell wall-degrading enzymes to damage plant cells, prefer senescent plant tissues and often induce the production of JA and ET (Macioszek et al. 2023). On the other hand, biotrophic pathogens. Which preferably produce or induce plant growth hormones to get their nutrients from living plant cells with active photosynthesis, prefer juvenile plant tissues and induce the induction of SA in the host plant. Hence, necrotrophic pathogens often promote senescence,

whereas biotrophic pathogens delay senescence in the host cells (Häffner et  al. 2015). Hemi-biotrophic pathogens, which are biotrophic in their frst life cycle and are necrotrophic in their second life cycle, can trigger a combination of defense hormones. In the early stages, they may induce SA signaling to establish a biotrophic phase, and later, as they switch to a necrotrophic phase, JA and ET signaling may be activated (Huang et al. 2020a, b). SA plays a crucial role in systemic acquired resistance (SAR) triggered after the local hypersensitive response (HR) induced by efectortriggered immunity (ETI) that is highly specifc to a pathogen (Peng et al. 2021). Unlike ETI, pattern-triggered immunity (PTI), which is not specifc to pathogens, primarily activates SA-dependent pathway contributing to the onset of SAR as the frst line of immune defense in plants. ETI also can activate ET/JA-dependent pathway leading to induced systemic resistance (ISR) (Peng et al. 2021). Unlike SAR induced by biotrophic or hemi-biotrophic pathogens interacting with leaves, ISR is elicited by plant growth-promoting nonpathogenic rhizosphere bacteria and fungi (Yu et al. 2022). Colonization of Arabidopsis roots by rhizobacteria triggered ISR that primed the expression of the 1879 MeJAresponsive genes, among which 442 genes having MYC2- binding G-box motifs in their promoters were expressed in the shoot tissue to counterattack Pseudomonas syringae pv. tomato DC3000 (Pst DC3000) and downy mildew pathogen Hyaloperonospora parasitica (Pozo et al. 2008). The myc2 mutant displayed a defciency in rhizobacteria-induced ISR to those pathogens, suggesting the regulatory role of MYC2 in ISR.

During plant immune response, extensive transcriptional reprogramming and protein interactions are initiated by numerous TFs involved in hormone crosstalk. For instance, a temporal transcriptomic analysis of Arabidopsis plants infected with B. cinerea unveiled a sequence of events in response to infection. The biosynthesis of ET and JA occurred frst in early stages, followed by induction of ET- and JA-responsive genes. Subsequently, there was an induction of auxin biosynthesis, along with the suppression of genes associated with SA and ABA biosynthesis and responsiveness (Windram et al. 2012). MYCs serve as a central hub in the regulation of plant immunity by acting as a positive or negative regulator for the expression of numerous downstream genes that is infuenced by the levels of phytohormones in response to diverse biotic challenges. Transgenic rice plants overexpressing the rice (Oryza sativa) MYC2, OsMYC2, exhibited up-regulation of JAZs and many defense-related genes, along with enhanced resistance to rice leaf blight caused by hemi-biotrophic pathogen Xanthomonas oryzae pv. Oryzae (Uji et al. 2016), suggesting the positive regulatory role of OsMYC2 in JA signaling pathway. In wheat (Triticium aestivum), a homolog $4 1 \%$ similarity) of Arabidopsis MYC2, TaMYC4, assumes a

positive regulatory role in JA pathway through accumulation of ROS and expression of pathogenesis-related genes TaPR1 and TaPR2 associated with wheat resistance against biotrophic pathogen powdery mildew (Blumeria graminis f. sp. tritici) (Jing et al. 2019). Similarly, tomato (Solanum lycopersicum) MYC2, SlMYC2, is associated with at least 655 downstream target genes enriched for TFs and early JA-responsive proteins (Du et al. 2017). These components subsequently activate the late JA-responsive genes involved in defense mechanism. This positive regulation contributes to the activation of both wounding-responsive and pathogenresponsive genes, promoting the plant’s ability to counteract the necrotrophic pathogen gray mold (Botrytis cinerea) (Du et al. 2017). The SIMYC2-silenced fruits treated with MeJA are less resistant to gray mold compared to the wild type treated with MeJA, as shown by signifcantly higher lesion diameter and disease incidence than those of wild type (Min et al. 2020). This suggests a positive regulatory role of SIMYC2 in JA signaling pathway. This aligns with the observation that overexpression of SIMYC2 enhances resistance to gray mold in tomato fruits, accompanied by enhanced accumulation of total phenolics and favonoids, a response akin to the efects induced by MeJA treatments in tomatoes (Min et al. 2020).

In contrast to SIMYC2, AtMYC2 plays a negative regulatory role in JA pathway leading to pathogen resistance since myc2 mutants exhibit increased resistance to necrotrophic fungi, Fusarium oxysporum and B. cinerea (Anderson et al. 2004; Lorenzo et al. 2004), whereas AtMYC2 plays a positive regulatory role in JA pathway leading to woundresponsive genes since myc2 mutants display increased susceptibility to insect pests, such as Helicoverpa armigera (Dombrecht et al. 2007). In Arabidopsis, the susceptibility to B. cinerea fungus and S. exigua insect increases in the order of the quadruple mutants (myc2myc3myc4myc5), triple mutants (myc2myc3myc4), and myc5 single mutant, which shows no signifcant diference from the wild type (Song et al. 2017). These results indicate that AtMYC5 acts redundantly with AtMYC2/3/4 to up-regulate the wound- and pathogen-induced defense genes, ultimately enhancing plant resistance to S. exigua and B. cinerea. Having similar DNA binding (G-box-like motif $5 ^ { \prime }$ -CACATG-3′) specifcities to MYC2, both MYC3 and MYC4 were shown to serve as activators of JA-regulated processes, and they work in concert with MYC2 to collectively oversee distinct subsets of the JAdependent transcriptional response (Fernández-Calvo et al. 2011). When the repressive function of AtMYC2 for necrotrophic pathogen-responsive genes is lacking, AtMYC3/4/5 step in to trigger the activation of these genes. Likewise, even when the activating role of AtMYC2 for wound (herbivory insect)-responsive genes is absent, AtMYC3/4/5 continue to boost the expression of these genes. We present a functional model (Fig. 5) illustrating the synergistic and

antagonistic interactions among the components of phytohormonal signaling pathways during pathogen infection, which will be further elaborated in the subsequent discussion on MYCs as an epicenter of the signaling networks.

Stress signals (MAMPs, HAMPs, DAMPs) derived from invading organisms are first perceived by membrane receptors and then transduced through CDPK and MAPK pathways to induce distinct stimulus-specifc stress responses mediated by stress hormones (Aerts et al. 2021; Erb and Reymond 2019; Wari et al. 2022). Calcium is an essential stress-sensing secondary messenger in plants. Herbivorous insect attacks trigger JA- and ABA-dependent signaling pathways, while infections by necrotrophic pathogens and (hemi)biotrophic pathogens induce JA/ETand SA-dependent signaling pathways, respectively. Both branches are subject to antagonistic regulation. MYCs can act as both positive and negative regulator in ET, JA and SA signal transduction pathways through either direct protein–protein interaction or transcriptional regulation of numerous genes for other TFs that infuence expression of defense-related genes. MED25 is required for both transcriptional activation and repression activity of MYCs, but JAZ proteins inhibit the binding of MED25 to MYCs. Conversely, MED25 not only facilitates the breakdown of JAZ proteins by recruiting COI1 but also modifes JAZ splicing by recruiting splicing factors, thereby afecting JAZ sensitivity to JA. The conjugated hydrolysis of JA-ACC may infuence the supply of JA and ET (Staswick and Tiryaki 2004). JA-Ile activates COI, and active COI removes JAZs repressors. This results in de-repression of the activities of MYCs and EIN3/EIL1. COI also inhibits expression of SA-responsive genes irrespective of JA-Ile. The activated MYCs indirectly inhibit expression of SA-responsive genes (ICS1, EDS1, PAD4, etc.) via ANAC109/055/072 and also attenuate expression of ETresponsive genes (PDF1.2, PR3/4, B-CHI, etc.) through both transcriptional activation of EBF1/2 and physical interaction with EIN3/EIL1, while activating the expression of JA-responsive genes (VSP, LOX2, CYP79B3, TAT1, MYCs, JAZs, etc.) by ANAC109/055/072. This results in the inhibition of ET- and SA-dependent pathways, while activating the JA-dependent pathway. On the other hand, ET activates EIN2, which stabilizes EIN3 and EIL1 proteins and degrades EBF1 and EBF2 proteins to maintain EIN3/EIL1 proteins accumulation. This results in the inactivation of MYCs to inhibit MYC2-VSP branch pathway, as well as the induction of ERF1/ORA59 genes to stimulate EIN3/EIL1-PDF1.2 branch pathway. Conversely, MYCs interact with EIN3/EIL1 to repress their transcriptional activation of ERF1/ORA59 genes and suppress the expression of both ERF1/ORA59 genes. MYCs also induce expression of EBF1/EBF2 genes which encode ubiquitin ligase F-box protein that degrade EIN3/EIL1, thereby

inhibiting the ET-responsive pathway. ANA032 activates expression of JAZs and suppress expression of MYC2 and NIMIN1, thereby repressing JA-responsive pathway and activating SA-responsive pathway. MYCs also trigger the expression of JAZs, forming an auto-regulatory negative feedback loop to conclude JA signaling. ABA receptor PLY6 interacts with MYC2 to enhance activator or repressor activity of MYC2 on various genes.

When JA level is high, JA-induced MYC2 trans-activates the expression of DELLA (RGL3) gene, leading to the accumulation of DELLA(RGL3), which interacts with JAZ to repress JAZs activity. This results in releasing the active MYCs from JAZs-MYCs complex, which in turn accelerates the expression of JA-responsive genes for defense. MYC2 also activates the transcription of JAZs, forming a negative feedback regulatory loop for the short-term self-activation and long-term self-inhibition of MYC2. When JA level is low, more JAZs interact with DELLA and JAMs to suppress their repressor activities for GA and JA signaling, respectively, and less MYCs induce the expression of DELLAs and JAMs. This results in derepression of GA signaling for growth and repression of JA signaling for defense.

JA activates both WRKY75 and EIN3/EIL1 by removing JAZs to induce the expression of ERF1/ORA59, while promoting activator activity of MYC2 for ANOC019/055/072 and EBF1/2. ET stabilizes EIN3/EIL1 that suppresses the MYC2 ability to inhibits expression of ERF1/ORA59, as well as directly enhance the expression of ERF1/ORA59. JA stabilizes WRKY75, EIN3/EIL1, and MYCs by removing JAZs. Hence, when JA and ET levels are high, WRKY75, EIN3/EIL1, and MYCs would be derepressed to enhance expression of ERF1, ORA59, and ANOC019/055/072 genes. When JA land ET levels are low, WRKY75, EIN3/EIL1, and MYCs would be repressed to attenuate expression of ERF1, ORA59, and ANAC.019/055/072 genes. Because of reciprocal inhibition between EIN3/EIL1 and MYCs whose protein stabilities and concentrations are governed by ET and JA levels, the trajectory to the two branches of ET/ JA pathways would be dictated by the endogenous levels of ET and JA. ABF3 and ABF4 can directly trans-activate ANAC019/055/072 genes in MYCs-independent ABA signaling pathway.

When SA level is high, NPR1-EDS1-PAD4 complex associates with TGAs, WRKYs, and CDK8 TFs to induce expression of SA-responsive genes responsible for PTI, ETI, and SAR. The NPR1 and EDS1-PAD4 complex also suppress transcriptional activator activity of MYC2 by disrupting the interaction of MYC2-MED25 complex at G-box motifs of JA-responsive promoters of MYC2-VSP branch pathway. Concurrently, NPR1 also interacts with EIN3/EIL1 to suppress their activities, and TGA2/5/6-GRX280 complex inhibits the expression of ERF1/ORA59. All these concerted

action leads to the suppression of JA and ET signaling pathways and activation of SA signaling pathway.

When SA level is low, NPR3/4 not only interact with JAZs to suppress the repressor activities of JAZs, but also disrupts the interaction of NPR1 with EDS1-PAD4-PBS3 complex to inhibit the activator activity of NPR1, thus promoting ET/JA-dependent pathway and impeding SA-dependent pathway. ANAC032-mediated activation of SA pathway is abrogated by the action of NPR3/4, whereas NPR3/4- mediated activation of ET/JA pathways is counteracted by the action of ANAC032. Besides NPR3/4 and ANAC032, EDR1 interferes with EDS1-PAD4 complex required for NPR1 activity, thus interrupting SA signaling pathway. In addition, EDR1 suppresses the repressor activity of MYC2 that inhibits EIN3/EIL1 transcriptional activator activity and expression of ERF1/ORA59 genes, leading to enhanced expression of plant defense genes associated with ISR and resistance to necrotrophic pathogens. COI1 also directly represses the expression of SA-responsive genes (EDS1, PAD4, PBS3 and ICS1) in a JA-Ile- and JAZ-independent manner. Additionally, in the absence of SA, TGA2/5/6 activates the expression of ERF1/ORA59 to enhance the defense response against necrotrophic pathogens. As a result, apart from JAZs and MYCs, diferent amplitudes of spatiotemporal expression of COI1, NPR1/3/4, ANAC032, and EDR1 would transiently afect the synergistic and antagonistic interactions of ET/JA and SA signaling pathways.

The solid lines ending in red, black, and gray arrows indicate transcriptional activation, activation of activity by physical interaction, and activation of synthesis via MAPK/ CDPK signal transduction pathway, respectively. The solid and dotted green arrows denote ABA-stimulated MYC2 activator activity by physical interaction and ABA-induced transcriptional activation, respectively. The solid and dotted blue arrow lines denote enzymatic synthesis or conjugation and hydrolysis, respectively. The solid blunt end lines of red, black, and green bars depict transcriptional repression, inhibition of activity by physical interaction, and ABAstimulated MYC2 repressor activity by physical interaction, respectively. The bidirectional and dashed black line arrows represent the active complex via protein–protein interaction and multi-step signaling pathway, respectively. The italic blue letters represent only a few of the terminal genes among many regulated by TFs in the pathways. The blank arrows refect the phenotypic efects. See the main text for further details on the molecular process underlying the hormonal cross-talks.

Abbreviations: CDPKs, calcium-dependent protein kinases; CHIB, basic chitinase; DAMPs, damage-associated molecular patterns; EDR1, enhanced disease resistance 1; ERD1, early responsive to dehydration 1; ETI, efectortriggered immunity; HAMPs, herbivore-associated molecular patterns; HEL, hevein-like PR protein; ISR, induced

systemic resistance; MAMPs, microbe-associated molecular patterns; MAPKs, mitogen-activated protein kinases; PTI, pattern-triggered immunity; TAT, tyrosine aminotransferase; SAR, systemic acquired resistance.

# MYCs in JA‑ET crosstalk signaling

The JASMONATE-INSENSITIVE 1 (JIN1) encodes MYC2 whose expression is rapidly up-regulated by JA in Arabidopsis (Lorenzo et  al. 2004). Overexpression of MYC2 represses the genes involved in defense response against necrotrophic pathogens, while activating the genes associated with JA-mediated wound responses (Niu et al. 2011). Consistent with this, the jin1 (i.e., myc2) mutant plant substantially increased transcription of pathogen defense genes (PDF1.2, PR4) in response to ET and/or JA treatment and consequently enhanced resistance to necrotrophic pathogens, such as Botrytis cinerea, Plectosphaerella cucumerina, and Fusarium oxysporum (Berger et al. 1996; Lorenzo et al. 2004). The jin1 mutant also showed increased resistance to hemibiotrophic pathogen Pst DC3000 with higher accumulation of SA than the wild type (Nickstadt et al. 2004). However, the jin1 mutant exhibited considerably reduced expression of wound-inducible genes (VSP2, LOX3, TAT) in leaves than the wild type in response to JA treatment or wounding (Berger et al. 1996; Lorenzo et al. 2004). Thus, MYC2 functions as a positive regulator in the JA signaling pathway triggered by wounding but assumes a negative regulatory role in the ET/JA and SA signaling pathways that respond to necrotrophic and (hemi)biotrophic pathogen attacks, respectively. According to the analysis of temporal gene expression associated with JA/ET pathway in Arabidopsis (An et al. 2017), the positive regulation of wound response by MYC2 occurs relatively early (peaked expression occurs within 6 h after JA elicitation), whereas the negative regulation of pathogen response by MYC2 occurs relatively late (peaked expression occurs after $4 8 \mathrm { h }$ after JA application). MYC2 also directly regulates the expression of a group of immediate JA-responsive genes (peaked expression occurs within 1 h after JA treatment), including JAZs and JA biosynthetic genes.

It has been reported that both the ET and JA signaling pathways need to be simultaneously triggered, rather than sequentially activated, to stimulate the expression of PDF1.2 upon a necrotrophic fungus Alternaria brassicicola infection, which provokes accumulation of signifcantly higher levels of both ET and JA than the uninoculated wild-type plant (Penninckx et  al. 1998). The ET treatment to the wild-type Arabidopsis plants significantly suppressed the expression of AtMYC2 induced by JA relative to the untreated plants; the myc2 mutants treated with MeJA and ET showed 52- and tenfold higher induction of PDF1.2 than the wild-type plants, respectively (Anderson et al. 2004;

Lorenzo et al. 2004). Over-expression of MYC3 or MYC4 in Arabidopsis plants caused signifcantly increased transcript levels of wound-responsive genes (VSP2, LOX3, TAT3), whereas MYC4 overexpression resulted in decreased transcript level of a pathogen defense gene (PDF1.2), and MYC3 overexpression incurred a slightly induced level of PDF1.2 (Niu et  al. 2011). These results suggest that AtMYC2 and AtMYC4 negatively regulate the expression of ET/ JA-responsive PDF1.2. The observation that MejA application caused about fvefold higher induction of PDF1.2 than ET application in myc2 plants could be ascribed to the increased ratio of MYC3 to MYC2, in conjunction with the lack of MYC2 that represses the transcriptional activities of two ET TFs, ETHYLENE-INSENESITIVE 3 (EIN3) and EIN3-LIKE 1 (EIL1) that stimulate the expression of pathogen defense genes (Li et al. 2015). Along this line, luciferase assays indicated that MYC2 and MYC3 have different regulatory capacities with the strongest transcriptional activation of JA-regulated genes by MYC2 tetramer and the weak transcriptional activation by MYC3 dimer (Lian et al. 2017). Analysis of JA-responsive transcriptome revealed that among 1495 diferentially expressed genes (DEGs), $1 6 . 3 \%$ of DEGs are co-regulated by EIN3/EIL1 and MYC2, and that $< 3 0$ genes are antagonistically regulated by them (Zheng et al. 2017). The results imply that ENI3/EIL1 and MYCs play key roles in an interplay or coordination between the ET and JA signaling pathways that enables plants to adapt to a specifc stress.

It has been demonstrated that the ET-responsive bZIP TFs ETHYLENE RESPONSIVE FACTOR 1 (ERF1) and OCTADECANOID-RESPONSIVE ARABIDOPSIS AP2/ ERF-domain protein 59 (ORA59) activate the expression of necrotrophic pathogen defense genes including PDF1.2 in the ET/JA pathway (Pré et al. 2008; Zarei et al. 2011). In Artemisia annua, JA-induced AaERF1/2 activate key structural genes for artemisinin biosynthesis and elevate the artemisinin content to confer resistance to B. cinerea (Lu et al. 2013; Yu et al. 2012). The myc2 mutant plant exhibited signifcantly higher expression of ERF1 and ORA59 and their downstream target gene PDF1.2 than the wild-type Arabidopsis plant irrespective of JA, whereas the ein3/eil1 double mutant plant lacking the EIN3 and EIL1 displayed markedly lower expression of ERF1, ORA59, and PDF1.2 (Song et al. 2014). Arabidopsis ein3 or ein3/eil1 mutant plants were more susceptible to a necrotrophic fungus B. cinerea, whereas myc2 plant was more tolerant to the fungus (Zhu et al. 2011). These results suggest the antagonistic interaction between MYC2 and EIN3/EIL1 TFs in the ET/ JA pathway leading to the expression of PDF1.2 and resistance to necrotrophic pathogens via ERF1 and ORA59. In fact, MYC2/3/4 were shown to physically interact with and repress EIN3 and EIL1 to impede their transcriptional activating efects on JA/ET-mediated pathogen-induced genes

(PDF1.2, ERF1, and ORA59), thereby suppressing disease resistance against B. cinerea (Song et al. 2014). Moreover, MYC2 not only enhances the expression of EIN3-BINDING F-BOX PROTEIN 1 (EBF1) that encodes ubiquitin ligase F-box protein to promote the degradation of EIN3/EIL1 (Zhang et al. 2014) but also suppresses the expression of both ERF1 and ORA59 genes (Dombrecht et al. 2007). Similarly, MYC2 up-regulates JAM1/2/3, which then down-regulate ERF1 and ORA59 (Sasaki-Sekimoto et al. 2013). These extra levels of transcriptional control besides protein–protein interaction would diminish the activities of EIN3/EIL and ERF1/ORA59, thus imposing an antagonistic efect on ET pathway. JAMs also negatively regulate ANAC019 expression leading to reduced VSP2 transcript level. However, in the absence of JA, JAZ repressors directly interact with JAM1/2/3 to counteract the activities of JAM1/2/3 (Chini et al. 2016). Meanwhile, a huge calcossin-like protein BIG (aka, DOC1/TIR3/ASA1/UMB1) involved in polar auxin transport exerts dual efects by negatively regulating MYC2, leading to reduced resistance to phytophagous insects, while positively regulating ERF1, resulting in enhanced resistance to necrotrophic B. cinerea (Zhang et al. 2019). BIG suppresses JA accumulation and promotes ET production, and transcription of BIG (At3G02260) is down-regulated by MeJA but is not afected by auxin and SA. Hence, BIG is likely to be a late JA response gene since MYC2 primarily controls the expression of early JA-responsive genes. It remains unknown as to how BIG regulates the expression of MYC2 and ERF1.

It has been shown that upon infection of Arabidopsis with B. cinerea, endogenous JA produced induces degradation of JAZ4/7/8/9 repressors that interact with WRKY75 TF, and the released WRKY75 binds to the promoter of ORA59 gene to activate its expression and its downstream necrotrophic pathogen defense genes such as PDF1.2 (Chen et al. 2021b). Moreover, JA facilitates the release of EIN3/EIL1 by removing JAZs, which recruit a histone deacetylase (HAD6) as a co-repressor to physically interact with and repress EIN3/ EIL1 that enhance the transcription of ERF1 and ORA59, but the released EIN3/EIL require ET for keeping their stabilization from EBF1/2-mediated degradation (Zhu et al. 2011). Thus, JA-induced activation of ORA59 and EIN3/ EIL1, coupled with ET-induced EIN3/EIL1 stabilization, serves as the basis of the synergistic crosstalk between JA and ET in response to necrotrophic fungal infection in Arabidopsis that triggers production of ET and JA (Penninckx et al. 1998; Windram et al. 2012). However, JA and ET act antagonistically in Arabidopsis in responses to chewing insect Pieris rapae, which stimulates MYC2-VSP2 branch and suppresses MYC2-PDF1.2 branch of the JA pathway (Verhage 2011). This could be mediated by the reciprocal inhibition between MYC2 and EIN3/EIL1. EIN3 and EIL1 were found to directly interact with and repress MYC2,

MYC3 and MYC4 to inhibit their transcriptional activating efects on JA-induced expression of wound-responsive genes (e.g., VSP2, LOX2, TAT3) and herbivory-inducible genes (e.g., CYP79B3) (Song et al. 2014). This is consistent with the independent observation that the ein3/eil1 double mutant did not express ERF1, ORA59, and PDF1.2 but markedly increased expression of wound response genes (e.g., VSP2) regardless of JA treatment (Zhu et al. 2011). As a result, MYC2/3/4, the master TFs in the JA pathway, diferentially regulate two groups of genes by activating the genes involved in wound response while repressing the genes associated with resistance to necrotrophic pathogens. Conversely, EIN3/EIL, the key TFs in the ET pathway, repress the former group of genes but activate the latter. This dynamic results in trade-ofs in resistance to diferent biotic stresses. It has been reported that mechanical stresses such as needle stamping or touch induce a strong immunity of A. thaliana plants to B. cinerea (Benikhlef et al. 2013; Chassot et al. 2008). Accordingly, the physical interaction between MYCs and EIN3/EIL1 appears to play a pivotal role in modulating the antagonistic and synergistic relationship between the exclusive JA responses against herbivore attack and cooperative JA/ET responses mounted against necrotrophic pathogens.

The myc2 mutant plants displayed elevated expression of ERF1 and PDF1.2 and increased resistance to necrotrophic pathogens compared to the wild-type (Nickstadt et  al. 2004; Song et  al. 2014), suggesting that MYC2 acts as a negative regulator to inhibit expression of the pathogen defense genes. However, in contrast to the myc2 mutant, the JA-insensitive coi1 mutant suppressed the induction of ERF1, ORA59, and PDF1.2 genes even in the presence of ET, resembling the response of the ETinsensitive ein3/eil1 double mutant (Lorenzo et al. 2003; Pré et al. 2008; Song et al. 2014). This implies that the reduced activities of COI1-downstream JA signaling repressor JAZs are required to induce ERF1, ORA59, and PDF1.2 expression in the ET-mediated pathogen defense pathway. This would explain the observation that the activation of PDF1.2 gene is signifcantly diminished in either ET-insensitive ein2 or JA-insensitive coi1 mutants (Penninckx et al. 1998). Accordingly, low accumulation of both JAZs and MYC2 coupled with ET-mediated stabilization of EIN3/EIL1 would lead to a synergistic efect on the MYC2-PDF1.2 branch pathway for pathogen defense. This notion of the integrated JA and ET signaling pathways is partly supported by the observation that low accumulation of the MYC2 protein correlates with peaked expression of late pathogen-responsive genes that are negatively regulated by MYC2, whereas high accumulation of the MYC2 protein correlates with peaked expression of early woundresponsive genes that are positively regulated by MYC2 (Zhai et al. 2013).

# The multilateral mechanisms controlling the abundance and activity of MYCs

It remains enigmatic how plants concurrently decrease the levels of JAZs and MYCs when low levels of JAZs beget high levels and activities of MYCs, and vice versa. Along this line, MYC2 is capable of negatively regulating its own transcription and positively regulating expression of JAZs, contributing to the fne-tuning of MYC2 and JAZs levels (Chini et  al. 2007; Dombrecht et  al. 2007). This could explain the synergistic activation of ERF1 by both JA and ET in Arabidopsis (Lorenzo et al. 2003). In Arabidopsis, MYC2 and JAMs positively and negatively regulate CYP94B3 encoding JA-Ile-12 hydroxylase, respectively (Sasaki-Sekimoto et al. 2013). In tomato, MYC2 directly activates the expression of CYP94B1 and CYP94B2 by binding to their promoters to facilitate the conversion of JA-Ile to its less active form 12-OH-JA-Ile (Yang et al. 2023). Notably, a double mutation of CYP94B1 and CYP94B2 increased the resistance to B. cinerea in tomato. MYC2 and JAMs would achieve a balance in the magnitude and duration of JA signaling through their competitive binding to the promoters of CYP94Bs. Moreover, the extent of both the translational and post-translational modifcations (PTMs) levels of MYCs and JAZs may vary in the plants exposed to difering stress conditions. PTMs are known to be essential for plant immunity to implement rapid defense responses at the appropriate time (Gough and Sadanandom 2021). MYC2/3/4 are shortlived proteins degraded by proteasome (Chico et al. 2014). So far, at least three E3 ubiquitin ligases, COP1, PUB10, and CUL3BPM, are known to afect the protein stability of MYC2/3/4 via ubiquitination-mediated degradation (Chico et  al. 2020, 2014; Jung et  al. 2015). Overexpression of PUB10 resulted in considerable up-regulation of PDF1.2 and down-regulation of TAT upon MeJA treatment (Jung et al. 2015), indicating that PUB 10 play a vital role in modulating MYC2 activity that attenuates MYC2-PDF1.2 branch and enhance MYC2-VSP branch of JA/ET signaling pathways. Phosphorylation-coupled proteolysis of a 12-amino acid element (T328 motif) in the transcription activation domain of MYC2 was demonstrated to be required to stimulate its transcription activity that induces the expression of early wound-responsive marker gene LOX2 and inhibits the expression of late pathogen-responsive marker gene ORF59 (Zhai et al. 2013). On the other hand, the MPK6 of MKK3-MPK6 signaling cascade activated by BL was shown to physically interact with and phosphorylate MYC2, leading to the decreased wound-responsive VSP2 and increased pathogen-responsive PDF1.2 gene expression (Sethi et al. 2014). Meanwhile, the MKK3-MPK6 module activated by JA negatively regulates the expression of MYC2 gene (Takahashi et al. 2007). The MPK6 also not only phosphorylates ACS (1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid synthase)

that induces ET biosynthesis, but also phosphorylates several ERF TFs including ERF1A that up-regulates defensin PDF1.2 gene expression (Liu and Zhang 2004; Wang et al. 2022c). Thus, the MKK3-MPK6 module is not responsible for the phosphorylation-coupled proteolysis of MYC2. There are four potential phosphorylation sites in MYC2 protein (Zhai et al. 2013). There is a report indicating that wound-induced JA production activates MAP3Ks-MKK3- MAPK1/2/7, which serves to counteract insect feeding early during wound signaling (Sözen et al. 2020). Apparently, there are several distinct phosphorylation mechanisms that alter the regulatory activity of MYC2 for a specifc set of genes in a temporally diferent manner to modulate the magnitude and timing of JA/ET signaling. It is probable that phosphorylation-coupled proteolysis of MYC2, together with MAP3Ks-MKK3-MAPK1/2/7 module, primarily functions during the early stage of JA signaling, while MYC2 phosphorylation by MKK3-MPK6 assumes a more prominent role during the late stage of JA signaling. The relationship between the phosphorylation-coupled proteolysis of MYC2 and the two separate MAPK modules with respect to JA-mediated defense response remains to be further clarifed.

Additionally, JA-induced JAZ splice variants that lack JA-Ile- and COI-binding Jas motif but include cryptic MYCinteracting domain have been shown to be generated by MED25, which controls the recruitment of the splicing factors PRP (PRE-mRNA-PROCESSING PROTEIN) 39a/40a to fnely regulate JA signaling (Wu et al. 2020). Besides PTMs, protein–protein interactions (PPIs) may afect the activity of MYCs, contributing to the regulatory complexity of MYCs. It’s worth noting that the domain structure within the JAZ protein family can vary, and that distinct PPIs between JAZ proteins and other regulators including MYCs, MED25, EIN3/EIL1, and DELLAs may contribute to functional specifcity within the ET/JA signaling pathway (Miccono et al. 2023). Last but not least, an ABC transporter, AtJAT1, facilitates the nuclear entry of JA-Ile and cellular export of JA (Li et al. 2017b). This process maintains a critical nuclear JA-Ile concentration, helping to attenuate excessive JA signaling. Besides AtJAT1, Arabidopsis glucosinolate transporter 1 (GTR1) was found to translocate JA-Ile from wounded to undamaged leaves (Ishimaru et al. 2017). Given that ISR promotes sensitivity to JA or ET rather than enhancing the biosynthesis of JA or ET (Ton et al. 2002), JAT1 and GTR1 are likely to be involved in JA/ET-mediated ISR against pathogens and insect herbivores. The promoters of the ISR-primed genes are signifcantly enriched for G-box like sequences targeted by MYC2 (Pieterse et al. 2014). In summary, we provide a feedback regulatory loop diagram illustrating the multilateral mechanisms that prevent runaway activation of JA signaling and control the abundance and activities of MYCs in a temporal manner (Fig. 6).

![](images/2884d83551c3ec14967e93d97cc71f0df72c02df18a1e398d5dc62651e5eaa7d.jpg)  
Fig. 6 Working model depicting the multilateral mechanisms controlling the abundance and activity of MYC2 in a temporal manner (based on well-known concepts from Arabidopsis). Abbreviations: CYP94B, cytochrome P450 94B; EDS1, enhanced disease susceptibility 1; GTR1, glucosinolate transporter 1; JAR1, jasmonate resistant 1; JAT1, jasmonate transporter 1; JIH1, jasmonoyl-isoleucine hydro-

The agar-grown sterile Arabidopsis plants treated with either 5 ppm of ET or $5 0 ~ \mu \mathrm { M }$ MeJA showed substantially lower induced transcript of $P D F I . 2$ than the soil-grown nonsterile plants treated with the same dose of ET or MeJA (Penninckx et al. 1998). Moreover, when sterile plants were treated with a combination of 5 ppm of ET and $0 . 0 5 ~ \mu \mathrm { M }$ MeJA, the level of PDF1.2 transcripts increased to a level similar to that observed in sterile plants treated with $5 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ MeJA alone. Hence, the levels of JA and ET would alter the stochiometric ratios between the JA-induced MYC2/3/4 and the ET-induced EIN3/EIL1 proteins, each of which may be expressed to the variant levels at the transcriptional and (post)translational levels. This variation of the key regulatory factors, coupled with other accessory interacting partners and PTMs, would ultimately determine the trajectory of ET/JA signal transduction pathways that integrate the information of diferent stress signals, including circadian clock and photoperiod (Kazan 2017; Macioszek et al. 2023). As a transcriptional co-regulator, MED25 not only physically interacts with MYC2/3/4, EIN3/EIL1, ERF1/ORA59, PIF1/3/4, but also promotes the breakdown of JAZs by recruiting JA-Ile receptor COI1. Conversely, JAZs interfere with MYC2-MED25 interaction (Aerts et al. 2021; An et al. 2017; Çevik et al. 2012; Kazan 2017; Sun et al. 2020). The MED25-mediated PPIs interplay among JAZs, MYC2/3/4,

lase 1; JMT, jasmonate carboxyl methyltransferase; MKK3, mitogenactivated protein kinase kinase 3; MPK6, mitogen-activated protein kinase 6; MTBs, MYC2-targeted bHLHs; NPR1, nonexpresser of pathogenesis-related genes 1; PAD4, phytoalexin defcient 4; PCP, unidentifed protein catalyzing phosphorylation-coupled proteolysis of a 12 amino acid motif of MYC2

COI1, EIN3/EIL1, and PIFs intricately fne-tunes synergistic and antagonistic interactions to regulate the amplitude and timing of defense gene induction within the ET and JA pathways. This regulation is highly sensitive to the dynamic variations in cellular levels of ET and JA through enzymatic synthesis or conjugation/hydrolysis and transporters (Fàbregas and Fernie 2022; Piotrowska and Bajguz 2011). These levels are infuenced by ever-changing environmental factors such as type of stress, duration and intensity of stress exposure, as well as plant age and spatiotemporal factors. In summary, we present a diagram illustrating the PPIs between MED25 and other phytohormone responsive key TFs to coordinately regulate gene transcription (Fig. 7).

AtJAT1 is primarily expressed in the stele of roots, whereas GTR1 and CYP94Bs are mainly expressed in the leaf and leaf veins. Several negative feedback loops work together to limit the duration and amplitude of JA-induced defense responses. The transcriptionally active MYC2- MED25 complex can enhance the expression of MTBs, which weaken the stability of MYC2-MED25 complex, thereby reducing the expression of JA-responsive genes and establishing a negative feedback regulatory mechanism to control MYC2 activity. The PCP promotes MYC2 activity by phosphorylation-coupled proteolysis during the initial stage of JA signaling. The MKK3-MPK6 cascade module

MED25 acts as a bridge, transmitting information from genespecifc TFs to the basal RNA polymerase II transcription machinery.
