inhibits the expression of MYC2 gene but activates MYC2 protein by phosphorylation during the late stage of JA signaling. Under stress-free conditions, JAZs and CYP94B work in concert to restrain MYC2-mediated JA signaling pathway. The MYC2–Dof2.1–MYC2 transcriptional loop to enhance short-term responses to JA is described in Section "Regulation of photomorphogenesis, thermomorphogenesis, and skotomorphogenesis" (Fig. 2). COP1 reduces MYC levels in the dark and canopy shade, and photoreceptors counteract its activity in the light. Additional mechanisms of regulating MYC2 activity by NPR1, EDS1-PAD4 complex, DEL-LAs, circadian clock components, and phytochromes are described in Sections "MYCs in JA-SA crosstalk signaling", "MYCs in JA-GA crosstalk signaling" and "MYCs in phytohormones-phytochromes-circadian clock components crosstalk signaling" (Fig. 8).

# MYCs in JA‑SA crosstalk signaling

Arabidopsis has two separate hormonal signaling pathways in defense against diferent microbial pathogens, SA- and JA-dependent defense response pathways (Thomma et al. 1998). These two hormones act either synergistically at low concentrations or antagonistically at high concentrations, allowing plants to fexibly translate their hormonal responses in the face of diferent biotic stresses (Cui et al. 2018; Mur et al. 2006). Suppression of other signaling

weight and germination", "Regulation of root growth", "Regulation of fowering time and fower morphogenesis", "MYCs in phytohormones-phytochromes-circadian clock components crosstalk signaling", and "Regulation of drought stress") for further details. The diagram was constructed based on the published literatures (Aerts et al. 2021; An et al. 2017; Çevik et al. 2012; Chen et al. 2012; Cui et al. 2019; Elfving et  al. 2011; Guo et  al. 2023a, Ito et  al. 2016; Kazan 2017; Li et al. 2022c; Nomoto et al. 2021; Qin et al. 2022; Shin et al. 2007; Skubacz et  al. 2016; Sun et  al. 2020; Wu et  al. 2020a; Zhao et al. 2021)

pathways with negligible efects on the invading pathogens, coupled with activation of a particular signaling pathway with signifcant impacts, would be conducive to avoiding excessive use of the limited energy and resources to prioritize one pathway over the other, depending on the sequence and type of pathogens encountered. In general, JA brings about broad-spectrum resistance against necrotrophic pathogens and masticating insects, whereas SA triggers broad-spectrum resistance against biotrophic and hemi-biotrophic pathogens as well as stinging insects (Glazebrook 2005). In defense against necrotrophic pathogens (e.g., Alternaria brassicicola, Botrytis cinerea), the JA and ET signaling pathways collaborate to synergistically activate a distinct group of defense genes (e.g., PDF1.2, PR3, and PR4) (Thomma et al. 1998; Zarei et al. 2011). In defense against biotrophic pathogens (e.g., Peronospora parasitica), SA signaling pathway elicits induction of diferent sets of antimicrobial defense genes (e.g., PR1, PR2, PR5) (Thomma et al. 1998). The SA- and ET/JAmediated signaling pathways are traditionally regarded as the central components of plant immune responses against biotic invaders. Meanwhile, ABA, auxin, brassinosteroid, gibberellin, and cytokinin are believed to participate in the plant’s immune response by infuencing the numerous components of SA and ET/JA signaling pathways, but this is outside of this review and is described elsewhere (Li et al. 2019a; Pieterse et al. 2012).

![](images/0b1992a23f8442f885a47232c994d279be44bd7f8eddb10225c03544de8e9390.jpg)  
Fig. 8 Schematic diagram illustrating the crosstalk among JA, GA, phytochrome, and circadian clock components that govern both growth and defense through temporal regulation of MYCs (based on well-known concepts from Arabidopsis). Abbreviations: R, red light; FR, far-red light; SAS, shade avoidance syndrome

SA suppresses ET/JA-induced plant defensin gene PDF1.2, which is primarily involved in necrotrophic pathogen defense responses (Penninckx et al. 1998). The NON-EXPRESSOR OF PATHOGENESIS-RELATED 1 (NPR1), which functions as a transcriptional coactivator upon binding to SA (Liu et al. 2020), plays a key role in the antagonistic crosstalk between SA and JA since the SA-induced suppression of JA-responsive genes encoding plant defensin (PDF1.2), lipoxygenase2 (LOX2) and vegetative storage protein (VSP) was abolished in npr1 mutant plants (Spoel et al. 2003). Additionally, Arabidopsis plants unable to synthesize SA produced 25-fold higher levels of JA and increased expression of LOX2, PDF1.2, and VSP. When SA level is high and JA level is low, intermolecular disulfde bonds of NPR1 oligomers are reduced by thioredoxins TRX-H3/5 to produce NPR1 monomers, which translocate from the cytosol to the nucleus (Caarls et al. 2015). The monomeric NPR1 in the nucleus interferes with MYC2 activity, inhibiting the transcriptional activation of downstream JA-response genes (ANAC019/055/072, VSP2, LOX2, JAZ9). This interference occurs by disrupting the interaction between MYC2 and MED25 at G-box motif sites of JA-responsive promoters (Nomoto et al. 2021). Furthermore, the heterodimer activity of ENHANCED DISEASE SUSCEPTIBILITY1 (EDS1) and its co-regulator PHYTOALEXIN DEFICIENT4 (PAD4), which interact with NPR1 and activate transcription of SA-induced genes, is capable of suppressing the activator activity of MYC2 for the MYC2-VSP1 branch of ET/JA pathway associated with resistance to insect wounding (Cui et al. 2017; Khan et al. 2022). This collaborative partnership between NPR1 and the EDS1-PAD4 complex efectively represses the activator activity of MYC2 in the

MYC2-VSP1 branch pathway. Concurrently, EDS1-PAD4 complex suppress a small subset of JA/ET-responsive necrotrophy resistance genes including PDF1.2 and CHIB independently of the ERF1 (Brodersen et al. 2006). This is in line with the observation that overexpression of EDS1 and PAD4 renders Arabidopsis plants more susceptible to necrotrophic B. cinerea and more resistant to hemibiotrophic Pst DC3000 (Xing and Chen 2006). Unlike EDS1-PAD4 complex that suppresses JA-mediated MYC2 activity at early time points in ETI and basal immunity, the chromatin-binding EDS1- NPR1 complex directly activates SA-mediated defense genes and plays a major function at a later stage of local and systemic immunity (Chen et al. 2021a).

Overall, NPR1-EDS1-PAD4 complex promotes the expression of SA-responsive genes including PR1 encoding pathogenesis-related protein 1 and ICS1 encoding isochorismate synthase 1, a critical enzyme for SA biosynthesis during pathogen infection. The NPR1-EDS1-PAD4 complex-mediated suppression of ET/JA signaling pathway is complemented by TGA2/5/6 and a redox-sensitive glutaredoxin-like protein GRX480, both of which are recruited at the ORA59 promoter, where they collaborate to repress its transcription and the downstream target gene PDF1.2 in response to elevated levels of SA, which increases the glutathione level. (Ndamukong et al. 2007; Zander et al. 2014). These concerted actions are instrumental in triggering SAR to strengthen plant defense against biotrophic/ hemi-biotrophic pathogens, albeit at the cost of compromised resistance to necrotrophic pathogens. This perspective aligns with the observation that when Arabidopsis is simultaneously infected by both biotrophic and necrotrophic pathogens, it becomes more susceptible to the necrotrophic

pathogens (Spoel et al. 2007). Apart from EDS1 and PAD4, NPR1 acts a transcriptional coactivator that recruits and interacts with TGACG-binding basic leucine zipper (bZIP) TFs (TGA)2/5/6, cyclin-dependent kinase 8 (CDK8), and WRKY6/18 TFs to form a transactivating complex that stimulates the transcription of its own gene and target genes including PR1, ICS1, EDS1, and PAD4 (Chen et al. 2021a, 2019a). Although NPR1 is essential for the inhibition of JAresponsive gene expression and the activation of SA-responsive gene expression by SA, nuclear localization of NPR1 is not required for SA-mediated suppression of the JA pathway (Spoel et al. 2003). It has been reported that cytosolic NPR1 under low concentration of SA strongly stimulates the expression of WRKY62, which in turn down-regulates JAregulated gene expression (Mao et al. 2007). The molecular mechanisms involved in the cytosolic NPR1-mediated suppression of JA pathway need to be further elucidated.

Besides NPR1, NAC (NAM, ATAF and CUC) TF, ANAC032, was shown to not only inhibit MYC2 gene expression, but also activate JAZs expression, leading to repression of the two branches of ET/JA response defense genes including VSP1 and PDF1.2 (Allu et al. 2016). As a result of transcriptional inhibition of MYC2 by ANAC032, expression of three NAC family TFs, namely ANAC019, ANAC055, and ANAC072 is down-regulated, leading to reduction of their protein levels followed by inhibition of stomatal reopening to prevent pathogen invasion. In addition, ANAC032 inhibits the expression of NIM1(allelic to NPR1)-INTERACTING 1 (NIMIN1) whose protein interacts with NPR1 to inhibit NPR1-mediated SA-responsive genes (Allu et al. 2016). This inhibition results in the transcriptional activation of SAresponsive genes. Consistent with this, transgenic Arabidopsis plants that overexpress ANOC032 exhibited a strongly enhanced resistance against a hemi-biotrophic pathogen Pst DC3000, while anac032 mutant plants showed increased susceptibility to Pst DC300 compared to the wild-type plants (Allu et al. 2016). Expression of ANAC032 was shown to be rapidly induced by SA, MeJA, and ET, particularly in coordination with JA (Allu et al. 2016; Nakano et al. 2006). Accordingly, like NPR1-EDS1-PAD4 complex, ANAC032 acts as a positive regulator of SA signaling and negative regulator of JA/ET signaling. However, ANAC032 difers from NPR1-EDS1-PAD4 complex in that the former is induced by SA, JA and ET, whereas the latter is induced by SA. In contrast to NPR1, MYC2 suppresses SA-dependent pathway via ANAC019/055/072 (Lapin et al. 2020). The three NAC TFs, whose gene expression is up-regulated by MYC2/3/4 (Gimenez‐Ibanez et al. 2017), serve as positive regulators of the wound-inducible genes in JA signaling pathway, while acting as negative regulators of SA accumulation by suppressing ICS1 gene (encoding isochorismate synthase 1) involved in SA biosynthesis and by inducing SAGT1 (encoding SA glucosyltransferase that produces SA O- $\beta$ -glucoside)

and BSMT1 (encoding benzoic acid/SA methyltransferase that produces methyl SA) genes through direct binding to their gene promoters (Bian et al. 2020; Yuan et al. 2019; Zheng et al. 2012).

Pst DC3000 a hemi-biotrophic pathogen of Arabidopsis, tomato, and tobacco, produces and secretes coronatine (COR), a phytotoxin that functions as a mimic of JA-Ile, into the plant host cell using the type III secretion system (Thilmony et al. 2006). The COR not only facilitates bacterial entry into the plant apoplast by inhibiting stomatal closure but also suppresses microbe-associated molecular patterns (MAMPs)/damage-associated molecular patterns (DAMPs)- mediated PTI responses triggered by SA signaling pathway (Xin and He 2013). The COR-triggered stomatal reopening involves its perception by COI1 and degradation of JAZ2, followed by induced expression of ANAC019/055/072 by MYC2. The subsequent expression of the three ANACs in guard cells contributes to stomatal reopening (Kazan and Manners 2013; Zheng et al. 2012). Currently, it remains unknown as to how the three ANACs that are not required for ABA-mediated stomatal closure stimulate the CORregulated stomatal reopening. MYC3 and MYC4 act redundantly with MYC2 in regulating stomatal closure in guard cells, where a cell-specifc JAZ2 targets MYC2/3/4 to inhibit ANAC gene expression (Gimenez‐Ibanez et al. 2017). The COR-mediated MYC2‐dependent activation of three NAC family TFs also results in suppression of SA accumulation, thus inhibiting SA signaling pathway. This aligns with the report that the myc2 (jin1) mutants displayed higher resistance to Pst DC 3000, along with more elevated levels of SA than the wild types (Nickstadt et al. 2004). This increased resistance can be explained by the enhanced SA signaling pathway incurred by the lack of MYC2-induced ANACs. Pst DC3000 grew better in MYC5-overexpressing transgenic Arabidopsis plants and worse in myc5 single mutants than in wild-type plants (Song et al. 2017), suggesting that MYC5 is also additively involved in COR-regulated systemic susceptibility to pathogen. Thus, a signaling module of COI1- JAZ2-MYCs-ANAC019/055/072 is exploited by bacterial COR to promote infection.

When SA level is low and JA level is high, reduced disulfde bonds of NPR1 monomers are oxidized to form a large, inactive NPR1 oligomer, which is susceptible to PTMs, such as sumoylation and ubiquitination (Peng et al. 2021). Consequently, it undergoes degradation via the 26 proteasomes, contributing to the suppression of SA-mediated immunity. Both NPR3 and NPR4, which are SA receptors that bind SA with diferent afnities, act as a negative regulator of SA-responsive pathway by functioning as adaptors of Cullin3 (CUL3)-based E3 ligase to mediate the degradation of EDS1 and NPR1 via 26 proteasomes, thereby disrupting the activity of NPR1 (Chang et al. 2019; Spoel et al. 2009). Low SA level facilitates binding between

NPR4 and NPR1, whereas high SA levels promotes binding between NPR3 and NPR1, leading to NPR1 degradation to a certain extent (Fu et al. 2012). When SA levels are at an intermediate range, the interaction between NPR3 and NPR1 is inhibited, enabling NPR1 to accumulate and activate SA-dependent defenses. The degradation of NPR1 by NPR3 at the infection site where high SA level accumulates is believed to contribute to the activation of ETI-associated programmed cell death (PCD), wherein NPR1 serves as a negative regulator. Moreover, NPR3 and NPR4 have been shown to play a role in promoting ETI by circumventing COI1 and facilitating the degradation of the JAZs. This allows induction of de novo JA synthesis and JA-responsive genes that stimulate the expression of defense genes against necrotrophic pathogens at the early stage of ETI (Liu et al. 2016a). Thus, activation of both SA and JA signaling pathways during ETI empowers plants to employ PCD-associated defense strategy against biotrophic pathogens without compromising resistance to necrotrophic pathogens.

The removal of JAZs by NPR3/4 releases free MYCs that can activate expression of ANAC019/055/072, which in turn suppress the expression of SA-dependent genes including ICS1, PAD4, EDS1, and PBS3. Apart from MYCs-induced ANAC019/055/072, COI1 is also known to directly repress the expression of SA-responsive genes, including ICS1, EDS1, PAD4, and PBS3, in a JA-Ile- and JAZ-independent manner, contributing to the inhibition of SA pathway that renders Arabidopsis more susceptible to a hemi-biotrophic fungal pathogen Verticillium longisporum (Ulrich et al. 2021). Hence, MYCs, NPR3/4 and COI collaborate to repress SA signaling pathway. The balance between SA and ET/JA dictates the host defense signaling pathway. In addition to NPR3/4 that activate JA signaling pathway, TGA2/5/6 TFs that are required for SA-mediated defense response also up-regulates the expression of ERF1 and ORA59 to activate defensive genes against necrotrophic pathogens independently of MYC2 in the absence of SA stimulus (Zander et al. 2010). In the presence JA stimulus, WRKY75 released from JAZ repressors would activate the expression of ORF59 and the downstream defense-related genes (Chen et al. 2021b). Therefore, overall balance between SA and JA levels during plant-pathogen interaction dictates the host defense signaling. Depending on the relative concentrations of SA and JA and the duration of exposure, the hormonal efects are either synergistic or antagonistic. Consistent with this notion, when tobacco leaves were infltrated with $1 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ JA and $2 5 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ SA, expression of PR-1 (SA marker gene) was peaked with a threefold increase at $2 4 \ \mathrm { h }$ following treatment but was markedly reduced at 48 h compared to $2 5 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ SA alone, while $1 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ JA failed to induce PR1 expression (Mur et al. 2006). Similarly, co-application of $1 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ JA and $2 5 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ SA to Arabidopsis leaves substantially enhanced expression of PDF1.2 (JA marker gene) compared to $1 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ JA alone,

while $2 5 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ SA failed to induce PDF1.2 expression. Conversely, their synergistic expression efect declined at higher concentrations of JA and SA or the prolonged co-accumulation of both JA and SA irrespective of concentration. At very high concentration $( 5 0 0 \mu \mathrm { M } )$ of SA and JA, either SA or JA alone did not elicit HR, but co-treatment triggered HRmediated necrotic cell death in tobacco leaves.

# MYCs in JA‑polyamines crosstalk signaling

During plant–microbe interactions, plants that can produce polyamines such as putrescine, spermidine, and spermine during the early stages of pathogen invasion display resistance to multiple pathogens (Asija et al. 2023). The diamine putrescine is a precursor of the triamine spermidine and the tetraamine spermine. Among them, spermine is known to be a stress-associated signaling molecules due to its unique role in inducing plant defense response including genes for PR and resistance (R) proteins, HR, MAPK, defense-related TFs, and phytoalexin biosynthesis (Seif and Shelp 2019). Inoculation of Arabidopsis plants with Pst DC3000, a producer of COR (JA-Ile analog), enhanced the expression of ADC2 encoding arginine decarboxylase 2, a key enzyme in putrescine biosynthesis, and SPMS encoding spermine synthase (Zhang et al. 2023). Genome-wide transcriptional analysis of Arabidopsis plants inoculated with Pst DC3000 revealed that numerous JA-responsive genes including MYC2 and ANAC 019/055/072 were up-regulated (Thilmony et al. 2006). Arabidopsis mutants lacking spermine synthase (spms) challenged with Pst DC3000 displayed a delayed transcriptional activation but produced higher transcript levels of the JA-responsive genes (LOX2/3, MYC2, JAZs, ANAC019, and VSP1/2) and higher levels of JA and JA-Ile than the wild types challenged with Pst DC3000 (Zhang et al. 2023). This was accompanied by a down-regulation of SA-responsive genes (ICS1, EDS5, PAD4, and PR1) and upregulation of BSMT1. The coi1-1 and myc2 mutants showed impaired up-regulation of ADC2 and SPMS (Zhang et al. 2023). Along this line, the expression of genes involved in JA biosynthesis and signaling including JAZs and MYCs was found to be highly induced by thermospermine (tSPM), an isomer of SPM (Zarza et al. 2017). Consequently, the defciency of SPMS would promote the conversion of its substrate spermidine into tSPM, a process facilitated by a competitive tSPM synthase (ACL5). Taken together, it appears that JA positively regulates the biosynthesis of putrescine and spermine, which then attenuates JA signaling pathway via a negative feedback regulatory loop. This assumption is substantiated by the fnding that under basal mock conditions and in response to COR, the spms mutants displayed increased expression of MYC2 and ANAC019, though there was no diferential expression of ANAC055/072 when compared with the wild types (Zhang et al. 2023). MYC2 was

found to directly interact with ANAC019 to co-regulate the expression of the chlorophyll catabolic gene NYE1 (Zhu et al. 2015). Polyamines inhibit senescence, which is facilitated by MYC2/3/4- and ANAC019/055/072-mediated induction of major chlorophyll catabolic genes (Sasi et al. 2022; Zhu et al. 2015).

Regarding pathogen resistance, the Arabidopsis spms mutants showed the increased JA/ET-mediated resistance to necrotrophic Botrytis cinerea, coupled with no signifcant changes in putrescine and spermidine contents compared with the wild types, while they exhibited more susceptibility to hemi-biotrophic Pst DC3000 infection (Zhang et al. 2023). This is consistent with the previous report that adc2 Arabidopsis mutants, characterized by the reduced levels of polyamines and decreased expression of PR1, were more susceptible to Pst DC3000 infection (Kim et al. 2013). Overexpression of SPMS gene in Arabidopsis conferred higher resistance to biotrophic Pseudomonas viridifava, while the opposite was found in spms mutant lines (Gonzalez et al. 2011). The lack of SPMS results in a decrease in total endogenous content of spermine whose oxidative de-amination by polyamine oxidases (PAOs) produces $\mathrm { H } _ { 2 } \mathrm { O } _ { 2 }$ , a key redox signal molecule regulating HR (Cona et al. 2006; Gonzalez et al. 2011). Depending on the H2O2 concentration, plant cells may initiate either a defense response or senescence that precedes cell death program (Häfner et al. 2015). The infection by the necrotrophic fungus Sclerotinia sclerotiorum led to increased ADC gene expression and activity in tobacco leaf tissues, along with increased putrescine and spermine accumulation in leaf apoplast, ultimately contributing increased necrosis and susceptibility to S. sclerotiorum (Marina et al. 2008). The increase of polyamine accumulation and PAOs activities would be disadvantageous for plant defense against necrotrophic pathogens that feed on necrotic tissue. Conversely, this response plays a benefcial role in plant defense against biotrophic pathogens that rely on living tissue for successful host colonization. This notion is in line with the fndings that severity of tobacco leaf rot caused by S. sclerotiorum was attenuated upon treatment with PAOs inhibitor, and that infltration with polyamines led to an increase in the percentage of leaf necrosis (Marina et al. 2008). The ADC overexpression or vacuum infltration of spermine into tobacco leaf disks decreased susceptibility to biotrophic P. viridifava (Marina et al. 2008).

However, there is a contradictory report that ADC1/2- silenced Arabidopsis plants producing low levels of putrescine were more susceptible to Botrytis cinerea with down-regulated expression of JA-responsive genes including PDF1.2, while they were more resistant to $P .$ . syringae infection with up-regulated expression of SAresponsive genes including PR1 and higher ROS levels than the wild types (Chávez-Martínez et al. 2020). Three arguments could explain this apparent discrepancy. First,

unlike knockout mutations, RNAi silencing is a gene knockdown whose efect may be incomplete and diminished with time, thus leading to a range of phenotypes of difering severity. Second, in Arabidopsis, putrescine is exclusively synthesized through the ADC pathway, where ADC1 and ADC2 are the key enzymes responsible for putrescine accumulation, and ADC1 is constitutively expressed, while the expression of ADC2 is induced under stress conditions (Janowitz et  al. 2003; Yariuchi et  al. 2021). Third, as Arabidopsis ages, it develops resistance to hemi-biotrophic $P .$ syringae pv. tomato $( P s t )$ , a defense response known as age-related resistance (Kus et al. 2002). Hence, ADC-silenced Arabidopsis plants may have higher levels of spermine and PAOs activity than the adc mutants.

Impairment of MYC2 function enhanced plant resistance against both necrotrophic (B. cinerea) and hemibiotrophic (Pst DC3000) pathogens (Lorenzo et al. 2004; Nickstadt et al. 2004). This is consistent with the fndings that MYC2-activated ANAC019/055/092 suppress the expression of both the JA/ET and SA-mediated pathogen resistance genes (Fig. 5). The increased resistance of myc2 mutants against B. cinerea is partly due to the lack of inhibition of EIN3/EIL activity and ERF1/ORF59 expression by MYC2, whereas the heightened resistance of myc2 mutants against Pst DC3000 can be attributed to the absence of transcriptional activation of ANAC019/055/092 by MYC2, leading to the enhanced SA accumulation and SA-mediated defense mechanisms. Unlike Pst DC3000 infection or MeJA treatment that triggers the up-regulation of MYC2 and ANAC019/055/072 in Arabidopsis (Thilmony et al. 2006; Zhu et al. 2015), B. cinerea infection did not give rise to diferential expression of MYC2 but downregulation of SA biosynthesis genes in Arabidopsis (Windram et al. 2012). This response is advantageous for plant defense against B. cinerea, as MYC2 activity not only promotes senescence by activating expression of SAG29 and chlorophyll catabolic genes (NYE1/2, NYC1), but also suppresses ERF1/ORF59, which activates the expression of defense genes against necrotrophic pathogens. Additionally, decreased SA levels contribute to the reduction of HR-mediated necrotic cells.

In tomato, SIMYC2 was found to activate the transcription of ADC gene encoding a key enzyme for biosynthesis of putrescine, a precursor of spermine (Min et al. 2021). Collectively, MYCs seems to play a role in regulating the equilibrium between JA- and SA-mediated defense mechanisms by modulating the levels of polyamines. An elevated content of spermine and increased PAOs activity may be associated with increased resistance to biotrophic pathogens and decreased resistance to necrotrophic pathogens, while lower contents of spermine and decreased PAOs activity may exhibit the opposite efect. The specifc threshold levels of spermine and PAOs activity that dictate the phenotype of

pathogen resistance may difer depending on the age of host plants and are yet to be fully elucidated.

# MYCs in ET‑SA crosstalk signaling

A protein kinase, known as ENHANCED DISEASE RESISTANT 1 (EDR1), plays a dual role in plant defense. It negatively regulates the SA signaling pathway by interfering with the EDS1-PAD4 interaction, crucial for NPR1 activity. This disruption results in attenuated PR1 expression and programmed cell death (PCD) (Neubauer et al. 2020). Additionally, EDR1 positively regulates the PDF1.2 gene expression and increased resistance to the necrotrophic pathogen Alternaria brassicicola by abolishing the repressor activity of MYC2. (Hiruma et al. 2011). Notably, MYC2 inhibits the trans-activation function of EIN3/EIL1 that induces the expression of ERF1/ORA59 genes (Zhang et al. 2014; Zhu et al. 2011). However, it is not yet clear whether EDR1 afects the positive regulatory activity of MYC2 in the MYC2-VSP branch of ET/JA pathway and EDR1-mediated phosphorylation of MYC2 promotes its destabilization. It has been hypothesized that in the absence of (hemi)biotrophic pathogens (at low SA levels), EDR1 maintains low or inactive levels of defense response genes by phosphorylating TFs, such as WRKY and AP2/ERF, subsequently marking them for degradation via the proteasome, and that upon pathogen detection, EDR1 function is inhibited, enabling elevated expression levels of SA defense response genes (Christansen et al. 2011).

The EIN3/EIL1, when released from JAZs repressor proteins by NPR3/4, can also directly target the promoter of ISC1 encoding isochorismate synthase and negatively regulate the biosynthesis of SA (Chen et al. 2009). Hence, when SA level is low, NPR3 and NPR4 work in concert with EDR1 and EIN3/EIL1 to suppress SA pathway and stimulate the MYC2-PDF1.2 branch of ET/JA pathway. This efect triggered by low SA level would be augmented when EIN3/EIL1 is stabilized by high ET level. In addition, expression of ORA59, which is activated by ET and JA but suppressed by SA, was shown to be maximally induced by TGA2/5/6 TFs, which can work together with ORA59 to trans-activate transcription of ANAC032 that promotes SA signaling pathway (Allu et al. 2016; Zander et al. 2014). Conversely, expression of ORA59 is suppressed by the protein complex of TGAs and NPR1-induced GRX480 (GLU-TAREDOXIN480) in the presence of SA (Ndamukong et al. 2007; Zander et al. 2014). SA-activated NPR1 was shown to physically interact with EIN3/EIL1 and interfere with the binding of EIN3/EIL1 to target gene promoters in Arabidopsis (Huang et al. 2020a, b). This could explain a previous observation that the repressive efect of SA on MeJA-induced PDF1.2 expression was completely abolished in npr1 mutant plants (Spoel et al. 2003). SA- and

ET/JA-inducible ANAC032 appears to play a role in a regulatory feedback loop that tightly modulates the interplay between SA and the JA/ET signaling pathways. Depending on the pathogen lifestyles that elicit diferent timing of production and concentrations of the stress hormones in the host cells, ANAC032 may prioritize either SA or ET/JA pathway. Upon initiation of the SA response before JA and ET signaling pathways are activated, SA has the potential to down-regulate the expression of JA- and ET-responsive genes. In the JA pathway, ANAC032 mediates the activation of JAZs and inhibition of MYC2 expression, while in the ET pathway, NPR1 suppresses EIN3/EIL1 activities and the TGAs/GRX480 complex induces ERF1/ORA59 expression. However, if the JA/ET pathways are fully induced before SA induction, the antagonistic impact of SA on the JA/ET pathways is eliminated, leading to the suppression of the SA response. COI, ANAC019/055/072, and EIN3/EIL1 play a role in inhibiting the expression of SA-responsive genes in this context. Along this line, it was observed that the reciprocal antagonism of defense gene expression between PDF1.2 (ET/JA-responsive marker gene) and PR1 (SA-responsive marker gene) is dependent on the dosage and duration of SA and JA treatment, with synergistic efects on PCD becoming more apparent at high concentrations of SA and JA in both Arabidopsis and tobacco (Mur et al. 2006). The feedback regulatory circuit would not only allow successive activation of ISR and SAR provisionally to exert an additive efect on the level of induced pathogen resistance (Van Wees et al. 2000), but also help to establish defense mechanisms against biotrophic/hemi-biotrophic pathogens at the early stage of SAR without making plant tissues vulnerable to secondary infection by subsequent necrotrophic pathogens on local decaying tissues caused by HR (Spoel et al. 2007).

Despite the crucial role of NPR1 in SAR and negative regulation of ET/JA pathway, NPR1 also is known to be required for ET- and JA-dependent ISR (Pieterse et  al. 1998). Currently, the exact role of NPR1 in ISR remains elusive. In the expression of PR1, two key TFs, TGA2 and NPR1, are independently recruited to PR-1 promoter even in the absence of SA treatment, and in the presence of SA, NPR1 interacts with TGA2 to form a transactivating complex (Rochon et al. 2007). In a similar way, NPR1-TGA2 complex might activate expression of the genes involved in ISR in the marginal level of SA. In general, MYC2 acts as a positive regulator of the JA-dependent pathway and as a negative regulator of ET- and SA-dependent pathway (Kazan and Manners 2013), whereas NPR1 acts as a positive regulator of the SA pathway and as a negative regulator of ET/ JA pathway.

In summary, the negative regulation of SA-dependent pathway by NPR3/4 is further enhanced synergistically via ANAC019/055/072 whose expression is induced by MYC2/3/4. Moreover, COI1 and EDR1 act independently

to contribute to this synergy. The results underline key regulatory roles played by MYC2/3/4, COI1, EDR1, ANAC032, and NPR1/3/4 in the intricate interplay between the JA/ETand SA-mediated defense pathways. This interplay involves not only multilayers of transcriptional and posttranslational regulation of ET, JA, and SA receptors and regulators but also protein–protein interactions among the TFs that respond to fuctuating levels of stress hormones. This results in a fnely tuned balance of synergistic and antagonistic enhancement, ultimately regulating the amplitude and timing of defense gene expression within the sophisticated networks of ET/JA and SA pathways in both spatial and temporal manner.

# MYCs in ET/JA‑ABA crosstalk signaling

ABA can bolster plant defense when activated during the early stages of infection by promoting stomatal closure, ROS production, and callose deposition at cell walls triggered by recognition of pathogen-associated molecular pattern (PAMPs). These responses contribute to resistance against biotrophic and necrotrophic pathogens, as well as viruses (Alazem and Lin 2017; Melotto et al. 2006; Ton et al. 2009). Conversely, if a pathogen manages to establish itself within plant tissues, the induction of ABA can hinder plant defense by interfering with SA- and JA/ET-mediated defense signaling pathways (Anderson et al. 2004; Yasuda et al. 2008). The coronatine (COR) efector molecule, secreted by bacterial pathogen Pst DC3000, can enter leaves through stomata and promote the growth of the pathogen in the apoplast. It is known to cause elevated ABA biosynthesis in the host plant, leading to the suppression of SA accumulation, downregulation of basal defense gene expression, and ultimately increasing susceptibility to infection by Pst DC3000 (De Torres-Zabala et al. 2007; Geng et al. 2014).

Research has revealed that the ABA receptor PYL6 (also known as RCAR9) engaged in interactions with MYC2, thereby infuencing MYC2’s regulatory activity on various gene promoters (Aleman et al. 2016). This interaction results in efects such as elevated expression of JAZ8 and decreased expression of JAZ6, with the interaction being notably strengthened in the presence of ABA. The aba2 mutants defective in ABA synthesis exhibited substantially reduced expression of the JA-responsive genes in the frst and second exposure to drought or ABA treatment (Liu et al. 2016b), indicating a synergistic efect of JA and ABA on the expression of the JA-associated genes. Considering MYC2 function indispensable for suppression of PDF1.2 by ABA (Anderson et al. 2004), the MYC2-PLY6 complex may suppress the MYC-PDF1.2 branch pathway of JA/ET signaling through an unknown mechanism. Consistent with this notion, the ein3 mutant plants treated with ABA expressed higher transcription level of JA-induced and wound-responsive VSP2

insect defense gene than the untreated ein3 mutant plants (Anderson et al. 2004). This could be due to the positive regulatory efect of PYL6 on MYCs activity combined with the functional loss of EIN3, which normally interacts with MYCs and represses their transcriptional activator activity on VSP2 (Song et al. 2014). The MED25, which interacts with MYC2, is also required for the MYC2 function to activate JA/wound-responsive genes (VSP1, LOX2, JAZ6) and repress ET-responsive genes (ERF1 and PDF1.2) during ABA signaling (Chen et al. 2012). Hence, it appears that ABA negatively regulate ERF1-PDF1.2 branch of the ET signaling pathway and positively regulates MYC2-VSP branch of the JA signaling pathway. In agreement with this, expression of ANAC019, ANAC055, and ANAC072, which act downstream of MYC2 and upstream of VSP1 and LOX2, was shown to be induced by COR (a functional analog of JA-Ile) and ABA, as well as drought and high salinity that triggers ABA accumulation (Bu et al. 2008; Tran et al. 2004). The MED25 also functions as a negative regulator of ABA signaling by enhancing degradation of ABI5 protein (Chen et al. 2012; Skubacz et al. 2016). Thus, ABI5 and MYC2 are under control of a common co-regulator MED25 to balance between ABA and JA signaling.

In earlier studies, it was noted that MeJA treatment induced stomatal closure in Arabidopsis (Hossain et  al. 2011). This efect is attributed to MeJA’s ability to stimulate the expression of 9-CIS-EPOXYCAROTENOID DIOXY-GENASE3 (NCED3), a key enzyme involved in ABA biosynthesis, leading to an elevation in the endogenous ABA levels within guard cells. Conversely, ABA can trigger the accumulation of JA in Arabidopsis (Adie et al. 2007). This phenomenon can be elucidated by the requirement of LOX2 for JA biosynthesis in Arabidopsis leaves (Bell et al. 1995). Therefore, JA signaling pathway appears to overlap with ABA signaling pathway. This view is in line with the fnding that ectopic overexpression of AtMYC2 conferred hypersensitivity to JA and ABA, whereas mutations in AtMYC2 gave rise to insensitivity to JA and ABA with respect to seedling germination and growth (Abe et al. 2003; Lorenzo et al. 2004). The overlap between JA and ABA signal pathways also could explain the drought-induced pathogen resistance and pathogen-induced drought tolerance, as well as the positive role of ABA in pre-invasive plant defense (Ramegowda and Senthil-Kumar 2015). MYC2 has been shown to function as a transcriptional activator in ABA signaling (Lorenzo et al. 2004). Interestingly, like myc2 mutant, the anac019 anac055 double mutant exhibited attenuated VSPI and LOX2 expression, along with increased resistance to the necrotrophic fungus B. cinerea compared with the wild type (Bu et al. 2008, 2009). Conversely, transgenic lines overexpressing ANAC019 or ANAC055 showed enhanced expression of VSP1 and LOX2, along with decreased resistance to B. cinerea (Bu et al. 2008). However, ANAC032, whose

expression is also induced by ABA, suppresses the inhibitory efect of COR on ABA-mediated stomatal closure (Allu et al. 2016). Thus, stomatal closure triggered by ABA is attributed to the transcriptional repression of MYC2 and transcriptional activation of JAZs repressor genes caused by ANAC032 that functions upstream of ANAC019/055/072 (Allu et al. 2016). This would contribute to the SA-mediated resistance to biotrophic pathogens especially when ANAC032 expression is induced at the early stage of infection. It is noteworthy that ANAC032 expression is induced by all four major stress hormones ET, JA, SA, and ABA (Allu et al. 2016; Nakano et al. 2006). This leads to a hypothesis that, through precise spatiotemporal regulation of MYC2 levels, ANAC032 might orchestrate both cooperative and opposing enhancements to prioritize specifc pathways within the ET, JA, SA, and ABA-responsive systems. The observation that ABA-induced SA accumulation causes higher susceptibility to a necrotrophic pathogen Alternaria brassicae in Brassica juncea plant (Dixit et al. 2022), could be ascribed to the elevated levels of ANAC032 that suppresses ET/JA-dependent defense response to necrotrophic pathogens and herbivory through transcriptional activation and inhibition of JAZs and MYCs, respectively. Concurrently, ABA-induced ANAO032 may stimulate SA biosynthesis and signaling through transcriptional inhibition of NIMIN1. Therefore, analysis of temporal expression patterns of ANAC032, MYC2, and JAZs in combination with analysis of expression levels of terminal marker genes associated with each of stress hormones and the measurements of varying endogenous levels of stress hormones could ofer valuable insights into the role of both ANAC032 and MYC2 in defense response.

ABA was shown to negatively regulate JA- and ETresponsive ERF1 and ORA59 which activate the expression of pathogen defense gene PDF1.2, a well-known marker of gene associated with resistance to necrotrophic pathogens (Anderson et al. 2004). MYC2 negatively regulate ERF1 and ORA59 expression since myc2 mutant exhibits elevated expression of trans-activators ERF1 and ORA59 and their target gene PDF1.2, and ectopic overexpression of MYC2 leads to signifcant reduction of PDF1.2 expression (Anderson et al. 2004; Song et al. 2014). Furthermore, expression of JAMs, also called ABA-inducible bHLH-type transcription factors (AIB), is induced by MYC2, and MYC2 may negatively regulate the expression of ERF1 and ANAC019 through cooperative binding to the gene promoters targeted by JAMs and MYC2 (Nakata et al. 2013; Sasaki-Sekimoto et al. 2013). Both the ABA-defcient aba2-1 and ABAinsensitive myc2 mutants showed enhanced resistance to the necrotic fungal pathogen Fusarium oxysporum (Anderson et al. 2004). Hence, the inhibition of the MYC2-PDF1.2 branch pathway by ABA could be primarily attributed to the JAMs and MYC2 repressor activities facilitated by the PLY6/MYC2 interaction. In a nutshell, the inhibitory efect

of ABA on PDF1.2 expression is achieved through multiple mechanisms. Firstly, it involves the reduction of ERF1 and ORA59 transcript levels by the increased repressor activity of MYC2, which is a critical node in the crosstalk between JA and ABA signaling pathways. Secondly, up-regulation of ANAC019/055/072 by the increased activator activity of MYC2 contributes to the down-regulation of PDF1.2. Thirdly, increased expression of JAMs by MYC2 leads to a reduction of the expression of ERF1. Fourthly, ANAC032 increases the expression of JAZs while simultaneously reducing the expression of MYCs, thus leading to the suppression of JA signaling.

Meanwhile, ABA-induced elevated levels of ANAC032 would activate SA signaling pathway by repressing expression of NIMIN1. The resultant ABA-induced SA accumulation would give rise to the onset of SAR (Conrath 2006). It was reported that the activation of SAR inhibited the expression of the genes involved in ABA response and biosynthesis (Yasuda et al. 2008). This inhibitory efect of SAR on ABA signaling is likely attributed to the engagement of the NPR1- EDS1-PAD4 complex, which acts to suppress the activator function of MYC2 that serves as a pivotal junction between JA and ABA signal pathways. Conversely, SAR induced by ABA is suppressed by ABA in a dose-dependent manner, and this suppressive efect is independent of JA/ET signaling pathway (Yasuda et al. 2008). The inhibition of SAR development by ABA is likely due to the participation of ANAC019/055/072, whose expression is up-regulated by MYC2, consequently reducing the expression of SA-dependent genes.

In the presence of $1 0 0 \mu \mathrm { M }$ ABA, neither ET (200 ppm) nor MeJA $( 0 . 1 \ \mu \mathrm { M / L }$ air) can induce PDF1.2 expression (Anderson et al. 2004). Based on the previous fndings, this can be explained by the premise that high levels of ABA receptor PYL6 enhances the repressor activity of MYC2 for the MYC2-PDF1.2 branch of ET/JA pathway. The efect of exogenous ABA on disease resistance may be greatly infuenced by endogenous ABA content, which varies depending on plant age, plant tissue, plant species, and pathogen lifestyles (Stevens et al. 2023). With regard to the efect of ABA on PDF1.2 expression, a marginal concentration of ABA is unknown. Expanding research into the impact of diferent ABA levels on the expression of MYCs in conjunction with ET-, JA-, SA-, and ABA-responsive genes at both transcript and protein levels can ofer additional insights into the interplay among these stress hormones.

# MYCs in JA‑GA crosstalk signaling

Arabidopsis genome encodes fve plant growth repressors called DELLAs, whose degradation is facilitated by GA. These include GA INSENSITIVE (GAI), REPRESSOR OF GA1-3 (RGA), RGA-LIKE1 (RGL1), RGL2, and RGL3, all

of which are destabilized by GA (Lee et al. 2002). MYC2 physically interacts with all members of DELLAs (Hong et al. 2012). Among them, RGL3 expression is strongly induced by JA treatment, and JA-activated MYC2 rapidly induces the expression of the RGL3 throughout the plant by directly binding to the promoter of this gene (Wild et al. 2012). Accumulated RGL3 then interacts with JAZs to inhibit JAZ repressor activity, leading to the activation of JA-responsive genes in a positive feedback loop regulated by MYC2. Transgenic plants overexpressing RGL3 displayed constitutively enhanced expression of VSP2, TAT1, and LOX2 relative to the wild type, whereas rgl3 mutant plants were more susceptible to the necrotrophic pathogen B. cinerea but are more resistant to the hemitropic pathogen Pst DC3000. This is consistent with the previous report that DELLA-mediated resistance to necrotrophs and susceptibility to biotrophs is partly attributed to the change in the balance of JA and SA signaling, as indicated by the phenotypes and maker genes’ expression analyses of quadruple-DELLA mutants (Navarro et al. 2008). In the absence of GA and presence of JA, DELLAs bind to JAZs and COI1 degrades JAZs, resulting in the release of MYCs to promote JA signaling. Conversely, in the presence of GA and absence of JA, GA triggers degradation of DELLAs, and JAZs interact with MYCs to form a repressor complex, thereby promoting GA signaling and repressing JA signaling pathway. However, MYCs are only fully active when JA and GA are present because of the two interactive JAZs and DELLAs repressors.

Low inorganic phosphate (Pi) represents one of the most prevalent nutrient defciencies, which poses a limitation on plant growth. Pi starvation leads to the suppression of gene expression related to GA metabolism, thereby reducing the levels of bioactive GA to increase the accumulation of the DELLA repressor (Jiang et al. 2007). Furthermore, Pi defciency has been shown to induce the JA signaling pathway, enhancing resistance to insect herbivory caused by Spodoptera littoralis in Arabidopsis, tomato, and tobacco plants (Khan et al. 2016). In rice, under Pi starvation conditions, the OsPHR2 TF gene is induced, which subsequently activates the transcription of OsMYC2 to confer resistance to bacterial leaf blight caused by Xanthomonas oryzae pv. oryzae (Xoo) infection (Kong et al. 2021). Therefore, the dynamic interplay between JA and GA can impact the abundance and activity of MYCs regulated by their interaction with JAZs and DELLAs. This, in turn, infuences the equilibrium between JA-induced defense responses and GAdriven growth responses.

When Arabidopsis plants are initially infected with Pst DC3000, EDS1 is rapidly induced to direct SA biosynthesis by directly activating ICS1 expression and consequently promotes the stabilization of DELLAs and the expression of SA-responsive PRs, leading to growth arrest accompanied by defense responses (Li et al. 2019d). However, RGL3

also interacts with EDS1/PAD4 to form a heterodimer complex to suppress SA overproduction that causes excessive defense response. This negative feedback regulatory loop enables plants to maintain a balance between growth and defense. Since GA decreases stability of RGL3 protein and JA increases stability of RGL3 through JAZ degradation followed by enhanced MYCs activity, the relative amounts of bioactive GAs and JA accumulated in the plant tissue may infuence the amplitude of JA signaling in a spatiotemporal manner. Therefore, MYCs appear to serve as an integrator of GA, JA, and SA signals for fne-tuning a trade-of balance between growth and defense to optimize plant ftness in response to resource limitations.

# MYCs in phytohormones‑phytochromes‑circadian clock components crosstalk signaling

The plant's defense is known to exhibit heightened functionality during dawn, accompanied by the highest accumulation of COI1 transcript, rather than dusk (Shin et al. 2012; Venkat and Muneer 2022). The circadian clock regulator TIME FOR COFFEE (TIC) was found to interact with MYC2, leading to the proteasome-mediated degradation of MYC protein and inhibiting JA signaling pathway in the evening phase (Shin et al. 2012). The tic mutant exhibited contrasting phenotypes when compared to the mys2 mutant, including elevated expression wound-responsive VSP, increased susceptibility to Pst DC3000, and reduced expression of pathogen defense gene PDF1.2. The evening complex (EC), a core component of the Arabidopsis circadian clock, attains its peak level during dusk, actively repressing the expression of PIF4/5, resulting in the inhibition of hypocotyl growth. Conversely, near dawn, EC level reaches its minimum, facilitating the heightened expression of PIF4/5 to promote growth (Nusinow et al. 2011). In addition, EC directly binds to MYC2 promoter and represses its expression, leading to attenuated leaf senescence (Zhang et al. 2018d). In Arabidopsis, shade (low R/FR ratios) induces stabilization of FAR-RED ELONGATED HYPOCOTYL 3 (FHY3) and FAR-RED IMPAIRED RESPONSE 1 (FAR1), which activate the expression of two bHLH TFs PHY-TOCHROME RAPIDLY REGULATED (PAR1) (Liu et al. 2019c). Subsequently, PAR1/2 form heterodimers with PIFs to inhibit the expression of the growth-related genes, thus preventing excessive elongation growth. On the other hand, FHY3/FAR1 interact with MYC2 to enhance the JA-induced expression of defense-related genes in the shade conditions. In the absence of JA, JAZ proteins impede the activities of FHY3/FAR1 and MYCs through physical interaction. The quality and quantity of light infuence the stability of MYC2/3/4, since phyAphyB null mutants that display shade avoidance syndrome characterized by elongated growth of stems and petioles have reduced endogenous levels of

MYC2/3/4 proteins (Chico et al. 2014). The canopy shade triggers the inactivation of PHYB, leading to the stabilization of JAZs, COP1, and PIFs, and the destabilization of MYC2/3/4 and DELLAs. This in turn facilitates suppression of JA-mediated defense response, allowing plants to allocate resources more efectively for growth.

DELLAs play a crucial role as negative regulators in the growth and elongation processes. High levels of DELLAs (RGA) interact with PIF3 to form a repressor complex and prevent PIF3 from binding to its target gene promoters and inducing expression of the genes for the growth and elongation processes (Feng et al. 2008; Hou et al. 2010; Yang et al. 2012). On the other hand, high levels of JAZs interact with DELLAs to interrupt DELLAs-PIF3 complex, and this allows more PIFs to activate the expression of growthrelated genes. While both PHYB and DELLAs can inhibit PIF activity, PHYB is believed to exert stronger repression on PIF activity than DELLA proteins, particularly under fuctuating light intensity conditions, as evidenced by the increased photosystem II efciency (ΦII) in a pif1/3/4/5 quadruple mutant (pifq) and the lower ΦII of phyB mutant compared to della quintuple mutant (dellaQ) leaves (Campos et al. 2016). In the absence of GA, stabilized DELLAs interact directly with both MYCs and JAZs, preventing their activities and thus dampening the JA signaling pathway (Hong et al. 2012; Hou et al. 2010; Yang et al. 2012). Conversely, in the presence of GA, DELLAs undergo degradation, releasing MYCs and JAZs. This allows JAZs to interact with MYCs, repressing their activities to maintain a proper level of defense gene expression. When JA levels are low, JAZs interact with both MYC activators of JA signaling and DELLA repressors of GA signaling. This interaction results in a decrease in the activator activity of MYCs on defense response and the repressive activity of DELLAs on growth-promoting PIFs (Hou et al. 2010; Yang et al. 2012). As a result, MYCs exhibit full activity only when JA and GA are present under light conditions. Conversely, PIFs are fully active only when JA is absent, and GA is present in dark or shaded conditions. Besides functional interaction between MYCs and PIFs at the level of protein–protein interaction, several PIFs target genes have also been identifed as targets of MYC2/3/4 (Li et al. 2020; Luo et al. 2022; Campos et al. 2016). Additionally, MED25 physically interacts with PIF4 at the promoter regions of PIF4 target genes and recruits RNA polymerase II to initiate transcription of genes encoding auxin biosynthesis and auxin signaling proteins, leading to shade-induced hypocotyl elongation (Sun et al. 2020). MED25 also interacts with PIF1/3 and histone deacetylase HDA19 to mediate transcriptional repression of the PHYB-dependent seed germination and anthocyanin biosynthesis by PIF1/3 in the dark (Guo et al. 2023a). However, it remains unknown whether MED25 dissociates from MYCs during the MED25-PIFs interaction. The interactions

among MYCs, MED25 and PIFs highlight the crucial role of MYCs in maintaining a delicate balance between growth and defense in a timely and cost-efective manner. MYCs play a pivotal role in regulating growth and development under the infuence of phytochromes and circadian clock components, as well as in orchestrating adaptive responses to biotic stress (Song et al. 2022). In conclusion, we present a diagram illustrating the complex interplay between hormones, phytochromes, and circadian clock components, orchestrating the temporal regulation of MYCs at various levels of protein abundance and activity, as well as transcript (Fig. 8).

The PHYB shown in the fgure represents an active Pfr form that is translocated to the nucleus. Inactivation of PHYB by low R/FR light promotes the stability of PIFs, COPI, and JAZs while destabilizing MYCs and DELLAs, thus relieving PIFs and JAZs from the inhibitory efect of DELLAs and allowing PIFs to activate downstream genes and promote growth at the expense of compromised defense. Concurrently, activation of FHY3 and FAR1 by low R/ FR light induces the expression of PAR1/2, enabling the physical repression of PIFs by PAR1/2 to prevent excessive elongation growth of hypocotyl at the expense of compromised defense. Thus, inactivation of PHYB and activation of FHY3/FAR1 under shade conditions are instrumental in sustaining a proper level of balanced growth by exerting an antagonistic efect on PIFs. By contrast, activation of PHYB by high R/FR inhibits plant growth through directly or indirectly suppressing PIFs activities. FHY3/FAR1, coupled with MYCs, stimulate the expression of JA-induced defense-related genes in the shade conditions, while in the absence of JA, JAZs inhibit the activities of FHY3/FAR1 and MYCs to attenuate defense response. Under shade conditions, GA, PHYB, and FHY3/FAR1 jointly inhibit an exaggerated growth triggered by PIFs. In addition, EC reaches its peak level during dusk, actively repressing the expression of MYCs and PIFs. TIC interacts with MYCs and destabilize them in the dark. Near dawn, EC reaches to the lowest level, enabling the elevated PIFs expression to enhance growth. MYCs are only fully active when JA and GA are present in the light conditions. PIFs are only fully active when JA is absent, and GA is present in the dark or shade conditions. Refer to the text for further details.

# MYCs in plant‑virus interaction

Apart from the plant–microbe interaction, SA-mediated signaling of SAR induction is activated during hypersensitive reaction (HR) to viruses, and RNA interference (RNAi) pathway is also involved in the SA-mediated repression of viral infection (Piau and Schmitt-Keichinger 2023). It was demonstrated that ABA can enhance plant resistance to potyviral infections by causing genome-wide disruption in the

host’s RNA metabolism (Pasin et al. 2020). Furthermore, there exists regulatory crosstalk between the JA signaling and antiviral RNAi pathways leading to the up-regulated expression of Argonaute 18 (AGO18), a core RNA silencing component (Yang et al. 2020a). While various phytohormone signaling pathways respond to virus invasion (Zhao et al. 2021), JAZ-MYC is one of the common targets of plant virus (Wu et al. 2020). For instance, while overexpressing OsMYC2 signifcantly boosts resistance against rice stripe virus (RSV), RSV infection suppresses the brassinosteroid (BR) levels to increase the accumulation of OsGSK2, which is a key negative regulator of BR signaling; this protein physically interacts with OsMYC2, triggering the phosphorylation-induced degradation of OsMYC2 and diminishing JA-mediated defense against RSV infection (Hu et al. 2020). The mechanism through which OsMYC2 imparts resistance to RSV and its potential connection to the activation of the RNAi pathway remains unclear.

# MYCs‑mediated signaling in plant responses to abiotic stresses

In addition to the roles in plant growth, development, and pathogen defense response, JA controls responses to abiotic stress, aiding plants in acclimatizing to adverse conditions (Wang et al. 2020).The $\mathrm { C a } ^ { 2 + }$ sensors, calmodulin-like proteins (CMLs), regulate MYC2 to participate in a distinct mechanism that mediates JA signaling during abiotic stresses (Khan et al. 2023). Within the JA signal transduction pathway, MYCs function as central mediators, integrating signals in response to various abiotic stresses. Among abiotic stresses, high salinity, drought and low temperature commonly causes the osmotic imbalance of cells that severely afects plant growth and development and are known to induce expression of many of the same genes in Arabidopsis through both ABA-dependent and ABA-independent pathways (Devireddy et al. 2021; Xiong et al. 2002). It has been reported that JA and ABA are involved in osmotic stress regulation, and that MYC2 positively regulates osmotic stress tolerance in tea plant and tomato (Zhao et al. 2023; Zhu et al. 2023). It is becoming progressively clear that MYC2 plays a pivotal role in regulating the interplay between the JA pathway and other hormone (ABA, SA, GA, IAA, BR, and ET) signaling pathways for adaptation to the specifc set of environmental conditions (Yang et al. 2019).

# Regulation of drought stress

In Arabidopsis, exogenous ABA treatment to the leaf tissue induces the expression of RD22 (RESPONSIVE TO DESSI-CATION22), a drought-responsive gene. RD22 has a MYB recognition site and two MYC recognition sites nearby MYB

recognition sequence within the RD22 promoter region (Iwasaki et al. 1995). Expression of AtMYC2, AtMYB2, and RD22 was induced by ABA and drought stress (Urao et al.1993). Double mutant of myc2myb2 was more sensitive to ABA-induced drought response than single mutants of myc2 or mycb2. The myc2 mutant was less sensitive to ABA with reduced expression level of RD22 (Abe et al. 2003). Hence, AtMYC2 and AtMYB2 cooperatively act as transcriptional activators in the dehydration- and ABA-inducible RD22 expression.

JA key regulatory genes coi-2, or myc2-2 mutants are more sensitive to dehydration (Li et al. 2019c). Dehydration stress activates numerous JA-associated genes (Liu et al. 2016b). The results indicated that both ABA and JA pathways are involved in drought tolerance. Apart from RD22, EARLY RESPONSIVE TO DEHYDRATION 1 (ERD1) can be up-regulated in the early stage of drought stress by either the binding of MYC2 or the binding of ANAC019/055/072 and ZFHD1 (ZINC FINGER HOMEODOMIN 1) to ERD1 promoter, conferring dehydration tolerance (Li et al. 2019c; Tran et al. 2007). Arabidopsis thaliana carries a total of 16 ERD genes whose promoters have ABA-responsive element (ABRE) and light responsive elements containing MYC recognition G-box sequence (Wu et al. 2023). Thus, JA signaling pathway participates in the ABA-mediated drought tolerance, and MYC2 acts as a point of crosstalk between the ABA and JA signaling pathways. PUB10 plays a negative regulatory role in the activity of MYC2 in both JA and ABA signaling through the destabilization of the MYC2 protein (Jung et al. 2015), contributing to the precise modulation of the cross-coordination between JA and ABA signaling. Intriguingly, ANAC019/055/072 also bind specifcally to a MYC-like cis-element (CATGTG) in the promoter region of ERD1; expression of ANAC019 and ANAC055 is induced by dehydration, high salinity, ABA, and JA; transgenic plants overexpressing ANAC019 or ANAC055 display improved drought tolerance, along with many of the up-regulated stress-inducible genes. Furthermore, when ABRE binding factors, ABF3 and ABF4, are phosphorylated by SnRK2C, they can directly bind to the promoters of ANAC019 and ANAC055 and activate their transcription (Hickman et al. 2013). Given that expression of ABFs is induced by ABFs themselves (Wang et al. 2019a), ABA-ABFs-ANACs transactivating cascade module may confer MYC2-independent drought tolerance. MYC2 has been demonstrated to act as a transcriptional activator in ABA signaling in Arabidopsis plants under drought stress (Abe et al. 2003). Consistent with this, recent research showed that in tomato plants under drought stress, MYC2 activates ABA signaling by inhibiting PP2C1 (type 2C protein phosphatase 1), a negative regulator of ABA signaling pathway, and RR26 (type B response regulator 26), a positive regulator of cytokinin signaling pathway (Zhao et al. 2023).

Stomatal closure and arrest of seed germination or seedling growth are important adaptive responses during drought stress to enhance long-term survival by conserving water and energy resources. During unfavorable environmental conditions triggering high levels of ABA, SnRK2s phosphorylates the SLAC1 and KAT1 ion channels, as well as RBOH, causing stomatal closure in guard cells (Kulik et al. 2011; Ng et al. 2014). Concurrently, SnRK2s phosphorylates and activates ABI5 to repress the expression of genes that promote seed germination and early seedling growth; recruitment of MED25 at the ABI5 target promoters is diminished, amplifying the impact of activated ABI5 (Chen et al. 2012). Interestingly, BBX21 is also known to be highly expressed in guard cell and down-regulate ABI5 expression by direct binding to ABI5 promoter to facilitate stomatal closure during drought stress (Kang et al. 2018). Upon stress relief and the presence of low levels of ABI5, MED25 directly interacts with ABI5 and inhibits its repressor activity at the promoter regions of ABI5 target genes, leading to the derepression of these genes and the resumption of growth. Surprisingly, Arabidopsis plants lacking MED25 exhibited drought resistance and signifcantly increased expression of drought-responsive genes (RD29A, RD29B, and DREB2a) in leaves compared

to wild-type plants (Elfving et al. 2011). This suggests that while MED25, which is normally associated with MYCs, is important for growth recovery after stress, it also negatively regulates drought resistance mechanisms. In support of this, the overexpression of ERF1 enhanced drought tolerance by binding to DRE (dehydration responsive element, TACCGA CAT) in the promoters of subsets of stress-responsive genes (Cheng et al. 2013). Drought also activates MAP3K17/18- MKK3-MPK1/2/7/14 signaling module through the phosphorylation of ABFs by SnRK2, leading to the induction of MAP3K17/18 expression (Sözen et al. 2003). Based on the information in the literatures, we propose a model illustrating the cross-coordination between JA and ABA signaling during drought stress (Fig. 9).

Tomato SlMYC2 was found to engage in regulating stomatal aperture in response to drought stress by directly inhibiting the expression of SICH1, which encodes a chalcone synthase 1 that controls the frst committed step of favonoids biosynthesis (Xu et al. 2022). Overexpression of SIMYC2 led to a reduction in favonoids content and an elevation in JA and ABA content. This was accompanied by enhanced drought tolerance and increased ROS accumulation and stomatal closure. In rice, overexpression of a

![](images/edd6c68ec8ef0ad6294240857764766a2809e69484953182b159efd2a60dd6e5.jpg)  
Fig. 9 Working model for drought tolerance mechanism involving MYC2 (based on well-known concepts from Arabidopsis). Abbreviations: CK, cytokinin; ABA, abscisic acid; HKs, histidine kinases; HPs, histidine phosphotransferases; RR26, type B response regulator 26; ABFs, ABA-responsive element (ABRE)-binding factors; ABI5, ABA-insensitive 5; CYP2, cyclophilin 2; DREB2A, DRE (dehydration response element)-binding protein 2A; ERD, early response to dehydration; LEA4-5, late embryogenesis abundant 4–5; OSM34,
