MYC-like bHLH gene OsbHLH148 (a functional homolog of AtMYC2), which is rapidly induced by MeJA, ABA, or abiotic stresses, conferred drought tolerance by up-regulating DEHYDRATION RESPONSIVE ELEMENT-BINDING 1 genes (DREB1s), which in turn stimulate the expression of stress-related genes (Seo et al. 2011a, b). The DREB genes are one of the best studied group of TFs involved in various abiotic stresses (Lata and Prasad 2011). Given that DREB TFs bind to the DRE cis-acting element in the promoter regions of many stress-responsive genes and activate their expression in either ABA-dependent or ABA-independent manner (Lata and Prasad 2011), OsbHLH148 might enhance the expression of OsDREB1 through direct activation of DREB TFs. However, the interaction between OsbHLH148 and OsMYC2 on the regulation remains unclear.

# Regulation in cold stress

Plants generally experience two types of cold stress: chilling stress, occurring within the range of $0 - 1 5 \ ^ { \circ } \mathrm { C }$ , and freezing stress, which occurs at temperatures below $0 ~ ^ { \circ } \mathrm { C }$ . When encountered with chilling stress, plants can acquire cold acclimation that enables them to withstand freezing stress as a result of prior exposure to chilling temperatures (Kerbler and Wigge 2023). MYC2 plays a vital role in chilling stress in many plants by interacting with INDUCER OF CBF EXPRESSION (ICE) to activate ICE–CBF/DREB–COR transcriptional cascade regulon, in which MYC2-activated ICE binds to the promoters of CBFs (C-REPEAT BIND-ING FACTORS)/DREB (DEHYDRATION RSPONSIVE ELEMENT-BINDING FACTOR) to up-regulate their expression, and CBFs bind to the C-repeat cis-elements (CCGAC) in the promoters of their downstream COLD-REGULATED GENES (CORs) to efectuate cold tolerance (Raza et al. 2023; Wang et al. 2022a). Furthermore, under chilling stress, CBFs interact with PIF3 to enhance the expression of COR genes by stabilizing the PHYB thermosensor and promoting the degradation of PIF1/4/5 that inhibit the expression of CORs (Jiang et al. 2020). Cold-stabilized PHYB acts downstream of CBFs to enhance freezing tolerance through the modulation of stress- and growth-related genes, including PIF1/4/5. Thus, the CBF-PIF3-PHYB module appears to serve as a mediator that integrates cold- and phytochromemediated light signaling networks to enable plants to adapt to and cope with cold stress (Kerbler and Wigge 2023).

In Arabidopsis, CBF1, CBF2, and CBF3 (also known as DREB1b, DREB1c, and DREB1a, respectively) are rapidly induced in response to chilling stress and ABA treatment (Knight et al. 2004). Apart from the ICE–CBF/ DRE–COR transcriptional regulon, $\mathrm { C a } ^ { 2 + }$ -activated calmodulin (CaM) binds to CaM binding transcription activator (CAMTA), which then activates the expression of CBFs (Doherty et al. 2009). The CBF/DREB can be involved in

ABA-independent pathway; Arabidopsis plants overexpressing CBF/DRE displayed enhanced tolerance of freezing, salt, and drought conditions (Bhadouriya et al. 2021). Among many ABF/DREB-inducible genes, CBF/DREB binds to the DRE (dehydration responsive element, TACCGACAT) in the promoter of RD29A to regulate its expression in the ABA-independent response to dehydration, salt, and cold (Yamaguchi-Shinozaki and Shinozaki 1994). In wheat and banana, MYC2 was found to physically interact with the constitutively expressed ICE1 in MeJA-induced chilling tolerance (Wang et al. 2022a; Zhao et al. 2013). In apple, overexpression MdMYC2 led to the increased expression levels of CBFs and enhanced freezing tolerance (Wang et al. 2019b). MdMYC2 was proposed to bind to the G-box element in the promoter of CBF1 based on yeast one hybrid and chromatin immunoprecipitation sequencing (ChIP-seq) assays.

In contrast to MYC2, MYC67 and MYC70 act as negative regulators of cold signaling and tolerance by physically interacting with ICE1 to suppress its activity in Arabidopsis (Ohta et al. 2018). Additionally, MYC67 and MYC70 bind to MYC cis-elements in the CBF3/DREB1A promoter, and their binding activity is increased after cold treatment. However, the expression of MYC67 is decreased after cold treatment, whereas expression level of MYC70 remains unchanged regardless of cold treatment. The dual actions of suppressing ICE1 activity and inhibiting CBF3/DREB1A promoter-binding activity by MYC67/70 efectively downregulate CORs for tight regulation of their expression level. Moreover, in the absence of JA, JAZ1/4/9 directly interact with ICE1/2 (AtbHLH116/13) to interfere with their activities (Chini et al. 2016). Accordingly, the activator activity of MYC2 competes with repressor activities of MYC67/70 and JAZ1/4/9 for the same ICEs promoter targets to modulate the optimal JA-transcriptional output and fne-tune the expression level of CORs in cold signaling.

Because the ICE–CBF/DRE–COR signaling cascade is conserved in several plants, similar MYC-interacting proteins may regulate cold tolerance in various species. Cold treatment stimulates anthocyanin accumulation in Arabidopsis, Chinese cabbage, and many other horticultural plants (He et al. 2020). CBFs were shown to interact with R2R3-MYBs to up-regulate anthocyanin biosynthesis genes (Zhou et al. 2020). Similar to cold stress, saline or dehydration stress strongly up-regulates the biosynthesis of anthocyanin, which contributes to the tolerance to salt and drought though its strong antioxidant activity (Mattioli et al. 2020). Thus, the transcriptional cascade module involving MYC2-ICE-CBFs-MYBs may also function to trigger anthocyanin biosynthesis under salinity and dehydration conditions. On the contrary, JAMs negatively regulate a set of anthocyanin biosynthesis genes by binding to the promoter of MYB75 (PAP1) (Sasaki-Sekimoto

et al. 2013). Under conditions of low stress that deplete JA, JAZ1/8/11 directly interact with MYB75 (PAP1) to hinder its activity (Chini et al. 2016). Competition between JAZ repressors and CBF activators for the shared MYB75 protein, coupled with inhibition of MYB75 expression by JAMs, may control the optimal JA-transcriptional output to fnely modulate the levels of anthocyanins in accordance with the severity and duration of stresses.

Light is also necessary for enhanced expression of cold-responsive genes during cold acclimation process in Arabidopsis leaves (Soitamo et al. 2008). This could be due to the stability of a short-lived MYC2 enhanced by PHYA/B in the presence of light (Li et al. 2022b) and/or activation of CBFs-independent CORs by CRY2-COP1- HY5-BBX7/8 cascade module (Li et al. 2021). During cold stress, phosphorylated CRY2 (activated by blue light) undergoes stabilization and competes with HY5 by interacting with COP1. This competition leads to the inhibition of HY5 degradation. Additionally, BBX7 and BBX8, identifed as direct targets of HY5, play a positive role in regulating cold tolerance by modulating the expression of a set of CORs and anthocyanin biosynthesis genes. Notably, this regulation occurs primarily independently of the CBF pathway. The PHYB enhances cold tolerance in both the light and dark in a CBFs-dependent manner, whereas CRY2 promotes cold tolerance only in the light independently of CBFs. However, the phosphorylated CRY2, stimulated by both cold and blue light, initiates the activation of the MKK3-MPK6 module, and MPK6 in this module physically interacts with and phosphorylates MYC2 to repress the expression of chalone synthase gene (CHS) essential for anthocyanin biosynthesis, as well as decreases ICE1 stability and cold tolerance (Li et al. 2017a; Sethi et al. 2014). Similarly, MPK3 and MPK6 of cold-activated MKK5-MPK3/6 module phosphorylate ICE1 to facilitate its degradation to attenuate cold tolerance (Li et al. 2017a). On the contrary, an independent signaling module comprising MEKK1-MKK2-MPK4/ MPK6 has been identifed for its involvement in enhancing cold tolerance (Teige et  al. 2004). The MPK6 in MEKK1-MKK2-MPK6 module phosphorylates and inactivates MYB15, a transcriptional repressor of CBF3, thus contributing to cold tolerance (Kim et al. 2017). Interestingly, the MEKK1-MKK2-MPK4 cascade constitutively suppresses MPK6 activities and has a positive role in the cold response (Zhao et al. 2017). Unlike MPK6, MPK4 is involved in high light $( 1 7 5 \mu \mathrm { m o l } \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ -induced anthocyanin accumulation through direct phosphorylation of MYB75 (refer to Section "Regulation of anthocyanins and phenolic acids") (Li et al. 2016). During cold stress, OST1 (SnRK2.6), a Ser/Thr protein kinase involved in ABA signaling, phosphorylates and stabilizes ICE1, and this action prevents degradation, consequently promoting

the expression of CBFs and freezing tolerance (Ding et al. 2015). Hence, cold tolerance is either positively or negatively regulated depending on the type of MAPK modules that are activated.

Overexpression of wheat MYC2 (TaMYC2) in Arabidopsis gave rise to cold tolerance in parallel with signifcantly higher antioxidant enzyme activities and lower ROS level compared to the wild type. In trifoliate orange (Poncirus trifoliata) under cold stress, MYC2 activates the transcription of BADH-l encoding betaine aldehyde dehydrogenase involved in the synthesis of glycine betaine, an osmolyte and ROS scavenger that protects against abiotic stresses, and consequently enhances cold tolerance (Ming et al. 2021). In tomato, SIMYC2 promotes cold tolerance by activating the expression of SIGSTU24 encoding glutathione S-transferase that mitigates cold-induced oxidative stress (Ding et al. 2022b). In addition, JA increases cold tolerance through SIMYC2-activated transcription of ADC (arginine decarboxylase), ODC (ornithine decarboxylase), ARG1/2 (arginase 1/2) involved in the biosynthesis of putrescine, which elevates total phenolics and antioxidant enzyme activities and consequently attenuates oxidative stress by reducing cold stress-induced ROS level (Ding et al. 2021; Min et al. 2021). ABA has been identifed as a factor enhancing cold tolerance in various plants by promoting accumulation of putrescine, a precursor of spermidine and spermine acting as antioxidants or stabilizers of macromolecules or cell membranes (González-Hernández et al. 2022). In tomato leave, accumulation of JA parallels that of ABA in response to cold stress, and SIMYC2 plays a crucial role in cold tolerance and JA-triggered ABA accumulation, achieved through the activation of NCED2, which encodes 9-CIS-EPOXYCA-ROTENOID DIOXYGENASE 2, a rate-limiting enzyme in ABA biosynthesis (Ding et al. 2022c). In Arabidopsis, putrescine is the only polyamine with increased levels that control ABA levels in response to low temperature by modulating NCED3 expression (Cuevas et al. 2008). Thus, MYCs emerge as crucial orchestrators in a network of responses to cold stress, encompassing JA and ABA signaling pathways, putrescine production, and the regulation of antioxidant and ABA biosynthetic genes (Fig. 10).

# Regulation in heat stress

Under heat stress, plants adjust the biosynthesis and signaling of various hormones (Devireddy et al. 2021). Currently, the efect of heat stress on the phenotypes of plants overexpressing or defcient in MYC2 has not been extensively documented. However, it has been reported that heat stress $( 3 8 ~ ^ { \circ } \mathrm { C } )$ increased accumulation of JA, JA-Ile, and ET in Arabidopsis, suggesting the contribution of de novo JA and ET biosynthesis to thermotolerance (Clarke et al. 2009). The Arabidopsis coi1-1 mutant showed higher susceptibility to

![](images/4b7ac944ccbda58bee1ea1efd7af76d9e4238860fd60e4504a803a9f438fc76a.jpg)  
Fig. 10 Working model for cold tolerance mechanism involving MYC2 (based on well-known concepts from Arabidopsis and tomato). Abbreviations: ADC, arginine decarboxylase; ANN1, calcium-permeable transporter ANNEXIN1; ARG, arginase; BL, blue light; CNGC, cyclic-nucleotide-gated channel; CaM, calmodulin;

heat stress with more electrolytic leakage than the wild type. Heat stress induced PDF1.2 and VSP1 with a much greater expression of PDF1.2 in Arabidopsis. This contrasting expression of PDF1.2 compared with VSP1 is in line with the previous reports that both the ET and JA signaling pathways need to be simultaneously activated to stimulate the expression of PDF1.2, and that expression of ERF1/ORA59 as a positive regulator of PDF1.2 is modulated by JA and ET (Penninckx et al. 1998; Pré et al. 2008). Overexpression of DREB2C was observed to stimulate the activation of numerous heat stress-inducible genes that contain CRT/ DRE cis-elements in their promoters, resulting in enhanced thermotolerance in transgenic Arabidopsis (Lim et  al. 2007). Furthermore, JA pathway activates the expression of DREB2A, which encodes a transcriptional activator that promotes the expression of heat stress-inducible genes by contributing to the formation of a heat stress-specifc transcriptional complex at the promoter of target genes, thus promoting heat tolerance (Tian et al. 2020). Apart from JA and ET, heat stress also stimulates ABA signaling and accelerates ABA synthesis (Li et al. 2021). MYC2 has been

CAMTA, CaM binding transcription activator; CDPK, calciumdependent protein kinase; MAPK, mitogen-activated protein kinase; CORs, cold-regulated genes; JAMs, jasmonate-associated MYC2- like; NCEDs, 9-cis-epoxycarotenoid dioxygenases; ODC, ornithine decarboxylase; SPMS, spermine synthase

shown to function as a transcriptional activator in ABA signaling (Lu et al. 2015; Zhao et al. 2023). Proteomic analysis of MeJA-treated myc2 mutant and wild-type plant revealed that MYC2 positively regulates HSP20 (Guo et al. 2012). In the context that MYC2 acts as a central hub regulator of JA signaling pathway that intricately interplays with ABA, SA, and ET pathways, MYC2 is likely to be involved in regulating heat tolerance. Moreover, JA was found to collaborate with SA to confer basal thermotolerance in Arabidopsis (Clarke et al. 2009). The molecular mechanism of this synergistic efect of JA and SA on thermotolerance remains to be elucidated.

# Regulation in salt stress

Salt stress is one of the most detrimental abiotic stresses, which negatively afects plant growth and reproduction such as seed germination, seedling formation, and fowering by making osmotic imbalance, ion toxicity, and oxidative stress (Kim and Park 2008). To counteract the deleterious efects, plants stimulate biosynthesis of an ET precursor,

ACC (1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid), in response to salt stress (Riyazuddin et al. 2020). However, since ET also stimulates senescence and PCD, it may positively or negatively afect plants’ responses to salt stress depending on ET levels infuenced by duration and severity of stresses. Hence, the fne-tuning of ET action would be necessary for salt stress tolerance in plants. Similar to ET, salt stress causes increased levels of JA. However, exogenous application of JA or MeJA impairs salt tolerance in Arabidopsis or improves it in other dicot and monocot plants (Delgado et al. 2021; Song et al. 2021). Salt stress activates the expression of core components of JA signaling pathway, including JAZs and MYC2, with the result of inhibition of primary root growth (Valenzuela et al. 2016). In line with this, it was observed that thermospermine (tSPM) promotes the transcriptional activation of genes involved in JA biosynthesis and signaling including JAZs and MYCs and enhances salt tolerance (Zarza et al. 2017). Notably, tSPM induced signifcantly higher expression of MYC4 than MYC2/3 after salt treatment. Overexpression of MYC2 signifcantly inhibited root growth in response to JA, whereas overexpression of MYC4 does not confer hypersensitivity to JA in root growth inhibition despite the fact that overexpression of MYC2 or MYC4 results in the repression of PDF1.2 and activation of VSP2 (Niu et al. 2011). The efect of MYC4 overexpression does not seem to be the same as tSPM application because tSPM induced the expression of both PDF1.2 and VSP2. The increase in MYC4 expression induced by tSPM may be a part of a complex regulatory network, and the exact role of MYC4 in salt tolerance needs to be experimentally validated. AtPAO5 (polyamine oxidase 5), which catalyzes conversion of tSPM into SPM, was found to be transcriptionally induced in response to salt stress and detrimental for salinity tolerance. Thus, higher expression levels of AtPAO5 than ACL5 (tSPM synthase) would contribute to salt intolerance. The spatiotemporal expression patterns of the two genes may determine the degree of salt tolerance and vary depending on the development stage and plant species.

Arabidopsis plants, when pre-exposed to ET, exhibit increased tolerance to salt stress, and two ET-activated TFs, EIN3 and EIL1, are necessary and sufcient for the enhanced salt tolerance (Peng et al. 2014). This aligns with the previous fnding that ERF1, a downstream target of EIN3/EIL1, is highly induced by high salinity (Cheng et al. 2013). Interestingly, besides salt tolerance, ERF1 activates specifc sets of genes associated with tolerance to drought and heat shock as well as pathogen resistance (Figs. 5, 9 and 11). The elevation of salinity triggers the accumulation of EIN3/EIL1 proteins by promoting the proteasomal degradation targeting EIN3/EIL1-specifc F-box proteins, EBF1/2 (Peng et al. 2014). The EIN3 selectively binds to the promoters of many SIED (Salt-Induced and EIN3/EIL1- Dependent) genes including PODs encoding peroxidases to

decrease ROS accumulation and thus exert salt tolerance. Consistent with this, EIN3-overexpressing Arabidopsis exhibits enhanced seed germination and seedling growth under salinity (Li et al. 2015). The overexpression of ERF1 conferred salt tolerance, as evidenced by a $1 0 0 \%$ seed germination rate compared to $4 \%$ in the wild type under high salinity (Cheng et al. 2013). Given that AtMYC2 not only inhibits the activity of EIN3/EIL1 but also promotes the expression of EBF1/2 (Zheng et al. 2017), AtMYC2 may play a negative role in salinity stress tolerance. Indeed, Atmyc2 mutants exhibited much higher percentage of seed germination and root growth under high salinity (Verma et  al. 2020). In contrast, mutations in MED25, which physically interacts with MYC2, conferred salt susceptibility characterized by a signifcantly reduced seed germination rate under salinity (Elfving et al. 2011), The contrasting phenotypes of Arabidopsis myc2 and med25 mutants highlight that MED25 supports MYC2's negative regulatory role in salt tolerance. The atmyc2/3/4 mutation attenuated the salt-mediated inhibition of root cell elongation (Valenzuela et al. 2016). On the other hand, overexpression of AtMYC2 conferred the increased susceptibility to salt stress by inhibiting the transcription of P5CS1 encoding delta 1-pyrroline 5-carboxylate synthase 1, a rate-limiting enzyme in biosynthesis of proline, which plays an important role in mitigating a wide range of stresses by acting as an osmolyte, a metal chelator, a hydroxy radical scavenger, and a redox signaling molecule (Meena et al. 2019; Verma et al. 2020). Under salt stress, AtMYC2 is activated through the MKK3-MPK6-MYC2 module to function as a transcriptional repressor of P5CS1 gene (Verma et al. 2020). The expression of MPK6 and MKK3 is assumed to be negatively and positively regulated by MYC2, respectively, in response to salt stress. Intriguingly, beyond the MKK3- MPK6-MYC2 cascade, a separate signaling module comprising MEKK1-MKK2-MPK4/MPK6 has been recognized for contributing to the mediation of salt tolerance (Teige et al. 2004). Although both MPK4 and MPK6 are activated by high light, unlike MPK6, MPK4 is involved in anthocyanin accumulation, polyamine synthesis, chloroplast integrity and photosynthesis (Li et al. 2016; Lin et al. 2022).

Another negative efect of MYC2 on salt tolerance is mediated by the repression of CAT2, which encode a catalase that alleviates salt-induced oxidative damage (Song et al. 2021). The repression of both P5SC1 and CAT2 by MYC2 would result in attenuated antioxidant and osmoticadjustment capacities in response to salinity stress. By contrast, overexpression of ERF1 activates the transcription of P5SC1 (Cheng et al. 2013). The fact that salt stress induces the expression of both MYC2 and EIN3/EIL1 implies that plants need a balanced level of proline by modulating the expression of P5CS1 because of the toxic efect of excessive proline (Su and Wu 2004). Meanwhile, AtMYC2 activates expression of senescence-associated SAG29, which encodes

![](images/b3f68dc505a7b6bf12bd4533778335780e95fab24ff3b270ca2e56becc07fc3e.jpg)  
Fig. 11 Working model for salt tolerance mechanism involving MYC2 (based on well-known concepts from Arabidopsis). Abbreviations: ABA5, abscisic acid (ABA) insensitive 5; ACC, 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylate; ACL5, ACAULIS 5; ARG, arginase; ADC, arginine decarboxylase; CAT2, catalase 2; CTR1, constitutive triple response 1; ERD7, early response to dehydration 7; ETR, ethylene receptor; LEAs, late embryogenesis abundant proteins; NCEDs,

a member of the SWEET sucrose efux transporter family proteins. (Goossens et al. 2017). While Arabidopsis plants overexpressing SAG29 showed accelerated senescence and hypersensitivity to salt stress, the SAG29-defcient mutants displayed reduced sensitivity to high salinity (Seo et al. 2011a, b). Consequently, the reciprocal inhibitory action between MYC2 and EIIN3/EIL1 (Hong et al. 2012) allows plants to balance the ET and JA action between the salt tolerance and senescence followed by PCD. In the salt-tolerant variety of peanut plants that are exposed to salt stress, COI1 and JAZ were up-and down-regulated, respectively, while CONSTITUTIVE TRIPLE RESPONSE 1 (CTR1), which encodes a Raf-like kinase that negatively regulates the ET signaling pathway, was down-regulated (Wang et al. 2024). This suggests active JA and ET signaling pathways. Concurrently, ABA signaling components PYR/PYL and SnRK2 were down-regulated, while PP2C and ABFs were up-regulated. The former indicates a reduced ABA signaling response, and the latter further suppresses ABA responses. However, MYC2 and DELLA were both up- and downregulated in response to salt stress. Enhanced JA signaling coupled with increased ET responses suggests a synergistic

9-cis-epoxycarotenoid dioxygenases; GLP9, germin-like protein 9; LEA, late embryogenesis abundant; ODC, ornithine decarboxylase; OSM34, osmotin 34; PAO5, polyamine oxidase 5; P5CS1, pyrroline-5-carboxylate synthetase; POD, peroxidase; RD, response to dehydration; SPM, spermine; SPMS, spermine synthase; tSPM, thermospermine; SAG29, senescence-associated gene 29

role of these pathways in promoting salt resistance. The upand down-regulation of MYC2 and DELLAs indicates that they are fnely tuned to respond to varying levels of JA and ET signals. Thus, the fuctuating expression levels of MYC2 and DELLA might be part of an adaptive mechanism to balance growth and defense responses under salt stress. This balance is crucial as excessive activation of defense pathways can hinder plant growth, while insufcient defense can lead to stress susceptibility. The down-regulation of $P Y R /$ PYL receptors and SnRK2 kinases, combined with the upregulation of PP2C phosphatases, leads to less activation of SnRK2 kinases. Despite the increased expression of $A B F s$ , their activity would be limited due to the reduced activation by SnRK2 kinases, resulting in an overall repression of ABA response in salt-tolerant plants.

Unlike Atmyc2 mutants, the tomato simyc1 mutant displayed a notable reduction in its capacity for salt stress tolerance compared to the wild type. This was evident through a shorter root length and higher levels of ROS after NaCl treatment (Sasi et al. 2022), suggesting that SIMYC1 plays a positive role in salinity stress tolerance. Thus, SIMYC1 exhibits contrasting efects compared to AtMYC2 in terms

of ROS scavenging and salt tolerance. The expression of all 14 SIMYCs including SIMYC1 was up-regulated following MeJA treatment but was generally down-regulated over 12 h after NaCl treatment. The role of the remaining 13 SIMYCs in relation to salt tolerance remains unclear. Much like SIMYCs, nine ThMYC genes in Tamarix hispida plants, which thrive in arid and saline soils, exhibited up-regulated expression in spatiotemporal manner after NaCl treatment (Ji et al. 2012). In rice, MYC2-like gene overexpression enhanced salt tolerance by binding to the ABA-responsive element (ABRE) element in the promoter of CYCLOPHILIN 2 (CYP2), which encodes a protein acting as a molecular chaperone (Liu et al. 2022a). Thus, MYCs seem to play a positive or negative role in salinity tolerance depending on the plant species, and salt adaptation requires fne-tuning of the activities between MYCs and EIN3/EIL1 (Fig. 11).

Drought stress signal transduction mechanism involving osmo-sensors and CDPK/MAPK cascade system is described in detail elsewhere (Khan et al. 2023. The JA and ABA signaling pathways interact diferently during multiple encounter with the drought stress. JA synthesis and MYC2 expression increase sharply upon the frst exposure to the drought stress, after which JA-activated MYC2 inhibits PP2Cs repressor activity, leading to expression of ABAresponsive genes including ABA biosynthesis gene. ABAactivated PLY6 also suppress PP2Cs to release SnRK2Cs. Activated SnRK2 kinases phosphorylate ABFs TFs, which induce the expression of ABA-responsive genes containing ABRE cis-elements in their promoters. Concurrently, MYC2 inhibits the expression of RR26 activity, resulting in suppression of cytokinin signaling pathway. During the second exposure to the drought stress, increased levels of MYC2 caused by its own transcriptional activation triggers the expression of JAZs that suppress MYC2 activity, forming an auto-regulatory negative feedback loop to terminate JA signaling via JAZs-MYC2 interaction. Subsequently, ABA produced during the frst encounter of the drought stress induces ABA-responsive stress-inducible genes through ABA signaling pathway, during which ABA-activated PLY6 interacts with MYC2 to change MYC2 regulatory activity, and PUB10 negatively regulates MYC2 activity by destabilizing MYC2 protein to fnely tune the cross-coordination between JA and ABA signaling in a stress- and tissue-specifc manner. When stress is elevated, SnRK2s phosphorylates the SLAC1 and KAT1 ion channels, resulting in stomatal closure in guard cells to prevent transpirational water loss. In addition, ABI5 phosphorylated by SnRK2s negatively regulate the genes for seed germination and seedling growth, and recruitment of MED25 is reduced at the ABI5 target promoters, enhancing the efect of activated ABI5. Conversely, when stress is relieved with low levels of ABI5, MED25 physically associates with ABI5 in promoter regions of ABI5 target genes to positively regulate expression of

downstream ABI5 target genes. The model could explain the positive efects of drought priming to tolerate drought stress. Expression AOC1, JAZ9, TRAF1, ERD1, and RD22 genes are strongly induced by JA signaling upon the frst encounter to drought stress. By contrast, RD29A expression is induced upon the multiple exposures to drought stresses. This differential expression pattern could be due to the absence and presence of DRE cis element in their promoter regions. The same model could be applied to salt and cold stress that cause the osmotic imbalance and the elevated levels of JA and ABA.

The model was constructed based on the published literatures (Aleman et al. 2016; Anderson et al. 2004; Barrero et al. 2006; Chen et al. 2012; Cheng et al. 2013; Diao et al. 2020; Hickman et al. 2013; Hossain et al. 2011; Jung et al. 2015; Liu et al. 2016a, b; Ng et al. 2014; Shinozaki and Yamaguchi-Shinozaki 2000; Tran et al. 2004; Zhao et al. 2023).

Cold stress signal transduction mechanism involving the activation of calcium channels and CDPK/MAPK cascade system is described elsewhere (Kerbler and Wigge 2023). In response to cold stress, MYC2-ICE-CBFs transcription cascade system is activated to enhance the expression of CORs. CBFs interact with PIF3 to enhance the expression of CORs by stabilizing the PHYB thermosensor and promoting the degradation of PIF1/4/5 under chilling stress. Additionally, calcium-activated CaM-CAMTA-CBFs transcription cascade module increases the transcription of CORs. Contrary to MYC2, MYC67-MYC70 heterodimer interacts with ICE1 and CBF3, while in the absence of JA, JAZ1/4/9 interact with ICE1/2 to interfere with their activities, resulting in inhibition of CORs expression. Thus, the binding activity of ICE1 to the promoter of CBF3/DREB1A may be suppressed by MYC67/70 and JAZ1/4/9, leading to the reduction of cold tolerance. Expression of RD29A (aka, COR78 or LT178) can be activated either by CBFs in a ABA-independent manner or by ABA signaling pathway (Fig. 9), owing to the presence of cis-acting elements of both CRT/DRE and ABRE. CBFs can activate CORs in either ABA-dependent or ABAindependent manner. Upon perception of cold stress, the MYC2-ICE-CBFs-MYBs transcriptional cascade module activates the biosynthesis of anthocyanin, and this modular system may also operate under drought and salinity conditions. Conversely, JAMs negatively regulate a specifc set of anthocyanin biosynthesis genes by binding to the promoter of MYB75 (PAP1). Under conditions of low stress that reduce JA, JAZ1/8/11 directly interact with MYB75 to interfere with its activity. This competitive interactions between JAM repressors and MYC activators, coupled with inhibition of MYB75 activity by JAZs, allow plants to fnely regulate the levels of anthocyanins in response to the severity and duration of stresses. Moreover, under chilling stress, CBFs interact with PIF3 to stabilize the PHYB thermosensor

and facilitate the degradation of PIF1/4/5, which otherwise inhibit CORs expression. Consequently, the CBFs-PIF3- PHYB module functions as a molecular hub, efectively integrating cold- and phytochrome-mediated light signaling networks to enable plants to adapt to cold stress. Besides the activation of CBFs-dependent CORs by MYC2-ICEs-CBFs and CBFs-PIF3-PHYB cascade modules, CRY2- COP-HY5-BBX7/8 module, which is activated by cold and blue light during the daytime, triggers expression of CBFs-independent CORs to enhance cold tolerance. While MPK6 in MKK3-mpk6 module phosphorylates MYC2 to decrease anthocyanin accumulation and cold tolerance, MEKK1–MKK2–MPK4/6 cascade can suppress the activity of MPK6 to enhance plant cold tolerance. Cold stress also activates ABA signaling pathway in which activated OST1 protein kinase stabilizes ICE1 by phosphorylation, thereby increasing CBFs expression and freezing tolerance. See the text for further details. The model was constructed based on the published literatures (Chang et al. 2019; Ding et al. 2021; Doherty et al. 2009; Jiang et al. 2020; Min et al. 2021; Ohta et al. 2018; Raza et al. 2023; Sasaki-Sekimoto et al. 2013; Shinozaki and Yamaguchi-Shinozaki 2000; Yamaguchi-Shinozaki and Shinozaki 1994; Zarza et al. 2017; Ding et al. 2015).

Early salt stress signal sensing mechanism involving $\mathrm { C a } ^ { + 2 }$ and ROS and CDPK/MAPK cascade system is described in detail elsewhere (Zelm et al. 2020). Low MYC2 activity increases EIN3/EIL1 activity by inhibiting EBF1/2 expression and stimulating EIN3/EIL1 activity, thus leading to the enhanced expression of POD and ERF1 accompanied by salt tolerance. The opposite efect occurs with salt intolerance when MYC2 activity is high. In Arabidopsis, elevated accumulation of tSPM triggers enhanced expression of JAZs and MYC4, accompanied by salt tolerance. In Arabidopsis, MYC2 plays a negative role in salt tolerance, whereas MYC4 plays a positive role in salt tolerance through unidentifed regulatory mechanisms. MKK3 activates MPK6 in a blue light-dependent manner. Salt stress also increases cellular ABA levels dramatically through MYC2-activated expression of NCED2. There is overlap between salt and drought stress tolerance mechanisms mediated by ABA. Under stress, phosphorylated ABI5 activate transcriptions of LEAs, which encode osmo-protective proteins that sequester ions accumulating in the cells and act as chaperones. See the main text for further details.

# Regulation of mechanical touch

When plant are repeatedly exposed to mechanical perturbations such as wind, rain, and animal touch, they typically display not only altered growth and development, including stunted growth, inhibition of inforescence elongation, and delayed fowering time, referred to as thigmomorphogenesis,

but also increased disease resistance against insect and fungal pathogens (Benikhlef et al. 2013; Chehab et al. 2008). MYC2/3/4 were found to play a role in thigmomorphogenesis, as Arabidopsis myc2/3/4 mutants did not display delayed fowering time and reduced rosette size observed in wild types after touch. RNA-seq analysis revealed that in response to touch, MYC2/3/4 directly or indirectly regulate 266 early responsive genes, including JA biosynthesis, JA signaling, stress-related, and other TF genes, among which 20 TF genes including bHLH19 and ERF109 are directly targeted and regulated by MYC2 (Van Moerkercke et al. 2019). It has been reported that through repetitive wounding treatments, a signal from shoots to roots reaches below-ground organs, and that this JA-mediated signal hinders growth by inhibiting both cell proliferation and cell elongation in the primary root tip (Gasperini et al. 2015). Unlike aerial organs of plants, growing roots most likely experience constant mechanical stress during soil penetration that could trigger JA production. MYC TFs (MYC2, MYC3, and MYC4) are known to have a role in the inhibition of primary root elongation when seedlings are grown in the presence of exogenous JA (Chen et al. 2011; Gutierrez et al. 2012; Lorenzo et al. 2004).

# MYCs‑mediated signaling in plant secondary metabolites biosynthesis

Secondary metabolites in plants play an important role in both biotic and abiotic stresses due to their antimicrobial, anti-insect, predator-attracting, antioxidant, and UVprotective activities (Chen et al. 2022). JA elicits a wide range of species-specifc and structurally diverse secondary metabolites in medicinal plants, as shown by the accumulation of secondary metabolites preceded by JA biosynthesis when plants encounter environmental stresses (Pauwels et al. 2009). JA plays a role in integrating and orchestrating primary and secondary metabolic pathways in response to environmental and developmental cues. In this process, MYC2 and MYC-like TFs play a pivotal role in quantitative feedback regulation of the synthesis of a variety of secondary metabolites (Fig. 12), including terpenoids, alkaloids, favonoids, and sulfuric-containing compounds (Luo et al. 2023). MYCs collaborate with numerous co-regulators to avoid excessive JA signaling that can severely inhibit plant growth and development.

# Regulation of terpenes

Volatile terpenes contribute to the plant defense responses, as they are repellent or toxic to herbivores, or attract predators (Singh and Sharma 2015). In Arabidopsis, AtMYC2 binds to the promoters of two sesquiterpene

synthase genes, AtTPS21 and AtTPS11, to activate their expression (Hong et al. 2012). The complete induction of AtTSP11/21 in Arabidopsis inforescences though MYC2 requires JA-induced activation of MYC2 and deactivation of JAZs in response to environmental cues, as well as GA-induced DELLA degradation driven by developmental programs. In Arabidopsis, MYC2 and PIF3 induce TPS (terpene synthase) gene expression by binding to different G-box-like elements of the promoter region in the light and dark, respectively (Zhao et al. 2021). In addition, MYC2 can interact with PIF3 to form a heterodimer complex that binds to the promoters of the TPS genes and synergistically enhance terpene biosynthesis in a lightdependent mode as a defense against whitefy (Bemisia tabaci). However, MYC2-PIF4 complex down-regulates TPS expression. Besides PIF3/4, AtMYC2 interacts with AtMYB21 to cooperatively transactivate AtTPS14 and

AtTPS21 promoters and enhance production of a terpene alcohol linaool (Yang et al. 2020b). AtMYC3/4/5 also function redundantly with AtMYC2 in regulating Arabidopsis terpene biosynthesis. Unlike the positive regulator of AtMYCs in terpene biosynthesis, FhMYC2 in Freesia hybrida acts as a negative regulator in terpene biosynthesis. During the initial stages of Freesia fower development, the highly prevalent FhMYC2 prevents the induction of TPS1 expression by FhMYB21. As the fower development progresses, the transcript level of FhMYC2 decreases, while that of FhMYB21L2 increases. This shift in expression patterns results in the induced expression of TPS1 and an augmented linalool biosynthesis in blooming fowers. In tomato, SIMYC2 is not involved in terpene biosynthesis, but SIMYC1 diferentially regulates mono- and sesquiterpene biosynthesis in glandular trichome cells of stem and leaf (Xu et al. 2018).

# Regulation of gossypol

Gossypol, classifed as a sesquiterpene lactone, serves as a crucial antimicrobial specialized metabolite in the defense of cotton (Gossypium hirsutum) against pathogen invasion and insect damage. GhMYC2 binds to the G-Box sequences in the promoter regions of consecutive gossypol synthesis genes (CDNC, CYP706B1, DH1, CYP82D113, CYP71BE79) to activate their expression and regulate gossypol synthesis (Han et al. 2022). Moreover, GhMYC2 could also interact with a bHLH TF GoPGF, which plays a dual role in regulating gland morphogenesis and gossypol biosynthesis (Zhang et al. 2024). GoPGF was found to form a heterodimer complex with a MYB TF CGP1 to increase their afnity and specifcity for DNA binding to the promoters of gossypol biosynthesis pathway genes (Jan et al. 2022). Thus, GhMYC2, GoPGF, and CGP1 TFs work in concert to regulate the biosynthesis of gossypol.

# Regulation of paclitaxel

Paclitaxel (Taxol®) is a diterpene alkaloid widely used for anticancer drug whose biosynthesis is highly regulated by JA in various Taxus species. MYC2/3/4 of Taxus media activate the promoters of the paclitaxel biosynthetic genes by recruiting the Mediator 25 (MED25) to up-regulate the genes in each step of paclitaxel synthesis pathway, while JAMs inhibit expression of paclitaxel synthesis genes by binding to the E-box motifs in their promoters (Cui et al. 2019). As a homolog of AtMYC2, TcMYC2a identifed in Taxus chinensis induces paclitaxel biosynthesis either directly by binding to the promoter of TASY (taxadiene synthase) or indirectly by binding to the promoter of ERF12/15 (Zhang et al. 2018b). Overexpression of TcMYC2a in T. chinensis cells signifcantly increased expression of most paclitaxel biosynthesis genes, especially acyltransferase genes (TAT, BAPT, DBTNBT, and T13H). TcMYC2a overexpression also enhanced the expression of TcERF12 and TcERF15, which encode a transcriptional repressor and activator of TASY gene, respectively. This coordinated regulation of both of both transcriptional activator and repressor by TcMYC2a may prevent hypersensitivity of JA response, fne-tuning the amplitude and timing of gene expression to maintain a balanced level of paclitaxel in plants. On the other hand, TcJAMYC1/2/4, homologs of AtMYC2 discovered in Taxus cuspidate, negatively regulate paclitaxel biosynthesis genes (Lenka et  al. 2015). Unlike TcMYC2a, TcJAMYC1/2/4 could not activate TcERF15 but does TcERF12, thereby inhibiting TASY gene expression as a species-specific responses following JA application. The contrasting efects of JA-activated MYCs on paclitaxel biosynthesis gene expression could also be ascribed to factors such as sampling time and conditions. In the case of T. media cell cultures,

samples were directly collected within $^ { 2 4 \mathrm { ~ h ~ } }$ of MeJA treatment. Agrobacterium-mediated transformation was used for expression analysis in T. chinensis cell cultures, whereas particle bombardment was used for transient expression assay in T. cuspidate cell cultures $5 4 \mathrm { h }$ after MeJA application. This distinction in experimental setup and timings may contribute to the observed diference. In support of this idea, the induction of TcMYC transcription, which was observed in response to high salinity and drought stresses, showed a signifcant up-regulation at 2 and $4 \mathrm { { h } }$ after coronatine (COR) treatment, but subsequently decreasing at 12 and $2 4 \mathrm { h }$ (Yang et al. 2018).

# Regulation of terpenoid indole alkaloids

Catharanthus roseus is an important source of numerous terpenoid indole alkaloids (TIAs), including the anticancer therapeutics, vincristine and vinblastine. They are cytotoxic defense molecules and are all derivatives of strictosidine, which is a condensation product of an indole moiety of tryptamine and a monoterpene component of secologanin (Liu et al. 2021a, b). The immediate-early JA-responsive CrMYC2 not only directly transactivates TDC (tryptophan decarboxylase), ORCA3 (octadecanoid-responsive Catharanthus alkaloid 3) and ORCA2 by binding to their promoters, but also indirectly up-regulates ORCA4/5 (Paul et al. 2017; Sui et al. 2018; Zhang et al. 2011). Apart from AP2/ERF family TFs ORCAs, CrMYC2 induces transcriptional repressor genes, bHLH TF RMT1 (Repressor of MYC2 Targets 1), bZIP TFs GBF1/2 (G-box binding factor 1, 2), and ZCT1/2/3 (zinc fnger Catharanthus transcription factor 1, 2, 3) (Liu et al. 2021a, b). The expression of RMT1 is induced by CrMYC2 and BIS1/2 (bHLH iridoid synthesis 1/2), and RMT1 acts as a transcriptional repressor of both CrMYC2 and its target gene ORCA3, enacting a negative feedback circuit to fne-tune the magnitudes of activation of CrMYC2/ORCA3 (Patra et al. 2018). BIS1/2 transactivate the expression of all genes in the iridoid monoterpene pathway, thus working synergistically with ORCAs to produce TIAs (Van Moerkercke et al. 2015). However, both CrMYC2 and BIS1/2 are physically repressed by JAZs in the absence of JA.

GBF1/2 dimerize with CrMYC2 and bind to the same cis-elements (T/G-box) as CrMYC2 in the promoters of its target genes TDC, STR (strictosidine synthase), and ORCA3, resulting in their attenuated repression caused by GBFs (Sui et al. 2018). Three ET-type zinc fnger proteins ZCT1/2/3 also bind to the TDC and STR promoters to repress their activities (Pauw et al. 2004). Coordinated transcriptional induction of ORCAs activators and RMT1-GBFs-ZCTs repressors by CrMYC2, coupled with physical repression of CrMYC2 and BIS1/2 by JAZs, would assist in fne-tuning the magnitude and timing of JA signaling to balance TIAs

accumulation in plant. ORCAs transactivate the expression of multiple TIA pathway genes (ASα, TDC, DXS, G10H, SLS, LAMT, STR, SGD), though their individual expression difers among diferent transgenic systems including cell culture, hairy root and whole plant. Similarly, CrERF5 induces the expression of key genes in both the upstream and downstream pathways of TIAs biosynthesis (Liu et al. 2017). Interestingly, CrERF5 induced by ET or JA shows the highest expression level in the older leaves. Unlike ORCAs and CrERF5, AP2/ERF family TF CR1 (Catharanthus roseus 1) negatively regulates seven key genes for the upstream and downstream pathways (G10H, SLS, TDC, STR, SGD, DAT and PRX) (Liu et al. 2017). CrWRKY1, which is induced by JA, GA, and ET and preferentially expressed in C. roseus root, activates the expression of several genes for the two parallel indole and monoterpene iridoid pathways (AS, TDC, DXS, SLS) and downstream pathway (SGD), as well as ZCT1/2/3 repressor genes (Suttipanta et al. 2011). However, CrWRKY1 represses the expression of CrMYC2 and ORCA2/3. This dual function of CrWRKY1 adds to a multifaced level of the complexity of feedback regulation of TIA pathway. ORCA2/3/4/5/6 are clustered in the same genomic scafold and are diferentially induced by MeJA and ET treatment (Singh et al. 2020). Both ORC3/4/5 and CrMYC2 are activated by phosphorylation mediated by a MAP kinase cascade system consisting of MAPKKK1, MAPKK1, and MAPK3/6 (Paul et al. 2017). Accordingly, CrMYC2 appears to be regulated at the levels of transcription, post-translation, and protein-proteins interaction to modulate TIAs biosynthesis based on the developmental stage and environmental cues (Liu et al. 2021b).

CrMYC2 is expressed in all tissue with preferential expression in root (Sui 2017). Surprisingly, the overexpression of CrMYC2 in C. roseus hairy roots resulted in the down-regulation of most of TIA pathway gene expression, along with the up-regulation of TIA transcriptional repressors (CrGBFs and ZCTs). Auxin, which can be synthesized in both shoot and root, drives root development (Overvoorde et al. 2010). A miRNA-regulated auxin response TF, CrARF16, binds to the promoters of key TIA pathway genes (TDC, G10H, STR) and repress their expression (Shen et al. 2017). In the late TIAs pathways, conversion of tabersonine into vindoline involves 7 enzymatic steps, where tabersonine biosynthesis occur in all plant parts, while the last 5 steps in vindoline biosynthesis are restricted to plant leaves (DeLuca et al. 1986). Light-induced vindoline biosynthesis is regulated by GATA and PIF TFs in C. roseus (Liu et al. 2019a). In the absence of light, CrPEP1 down-regulates CrGATA1, T16H2, and DAT genes. Conversely, in the presence of light, CrPEP1 is destabilized. The derepressed CrGATA1 then transactivates the genes involved in vindoline biosynthesis in the downstream TIAs pathway (T16H2, T3O, T3R, D4H, DAT), ultimately resulting in the accumulation of

vindoline, one of two condensing precursors for vinblastine and vincristine.

# Regulation of anthocyanins and phenolic acids

Anthocyanins, a class of water-soluble flavonoids, are induced during adverse stress conditions. The accumulation of anthocyanins signifcantly decreased in myc2/jin1 mutants (Dombrecht et al. 2007). In line with fndings from the jaz1/3/4/9/10 quintuple mutant (jazQ), which showed a signifcant increase in anthocyanin content (Campos et al. 2016), Arabidopsis seedlings overexpressing MYC2, MYC3, or MYC4 all accumulated more anthocyanins than wild-type plants, with the highest levels of anthocyanins in MYC3- overexpressing lines (Niu et al. 2011). This was accompanied by up-regulation of a key anthocyanin biosynthesis gene DIHYDROFLAVONOL REDUCTASE (DFR). MYC2 binds to G-box motif in the promoter of R2R3-type MYB75/ PAP1 (PRODUCTION OF ANTHOCYANIN PIGMENT 1) and increases its transcription (Yang et al. 2022). MYC2/3/5 activate the expression of HY5 in white light and under PHYB-dependent red light (Ortigosa et al. 2020; Putterill et al. 1995). HY5 binds either directly to the PAP1 promoter or to the promoters of early and late biosynthesis genes for anthocyanins (Li et al. 2022b; Shin et al. 2013). Thus, MYCs either directly trans-activate PAP1 or indirectly activates anthocyanin biosynthesis genes via HY5. In contrast to the positive impact of MYCs on anthocyanin accumulation, it has been reported that MYC2, when activated by MKK3- MPK6 module under BL condition $( 3 0 \ \mu \mathrm { m o l } \ \mathrm { m } ^ { - 2 } \ \mathrm { s } ^ { - 1 } )$ ), represses HY5 activity, thereby indirectly inhibiting anthocyanin accumulation, as well as light-inducible genes CAB1, RBSC-1A, and CHS (Chakraborty et al. 2019; Sethi et al. 2014; Yadav et al. 2005). This implies that besides light intensity, light quality infuence MYC2 activity and anthocyanin biosynthesis (Fig. 13).

MYB75 (PAP1) promotes anthocyanin accumulation by directly transactivating 10 structural genes in Arabidopsis developing seeds (Guo et al. 2023b). However, the biosynthesis of anthocyanins is known to be directly controlled by MYB-bHLH-WD40 (MBW) transcriptional activation complex where MYB75 (PAP1) interacts with one of the three bHLH TFs (TT8, EGL3, or GL3) and the WD-repeat TF, TTG1 (Yang et  al. 2022). As positive regulators of anthocyanin biosynthesis in Arabidopsis, MYCs and HY5 bind to the promoter of PAP1 to stimulate PAP1 expression (Boter et al. 2015; Lotkowska et al. 2015; Yang et al. 2022). Moreover, HY5 positively regulates anthocyanin biosynthesis by binding simultaneously to the promoters of anthocyanin structural genes (CHS, CHI, F3H, and DFR) (Shin et al. 2007). In high light, phosphorylation of PAP1 by MPK4 increases the formation and stability of MBW complex (Li et al. 2016). As negative regulators of anthocyanin

![](images/595ba64b9598f0dfa86be0a06775c2cc9d4e2f1a0a7a51ca8d155533bf6c8972.jpg)  
Fig. 13 Schematic diagram depicting the MYCs regulatory circuits controlling the genes for the biosynthesis of anthocyanin (based on well-known concepts from Arabidopsis). Abbreviations: ADT, arogenate dehydratase; ANS, anthocyanidin synthase; MBW, MYBbHLH-WD40; BL, blue light; CAD5, alcohol dehydronase 5; CHS, chalcone synthase; CCoAOMT, cafeoyl coenzyme a-O-methyltransferase; 4CL1, 4-coumarate-CoA ligase1; COMT, cafeic acid

biosynthesis in Arabidopsis, JAZ3 and JAM1/2/3 inhibit anthocyanin biosynthesis by interacting with PAP1 to destabilize MBW complex (Sasaki-Sekimoto et al. 2013; Yang et al. 2022). In Arabidopsis, when subjected to inorganic phosphate (Pi)-defciency, the PHOSPHATE STARVATION RESPONSE1 (PHR1) TF gene is activated, and increased JA levels promotes the degradation of JAZs. This allows PHR1 to interact with MYC2 to form a protein complex that positively regulates JA-induced anthocyanin accumulation (He et al. 2023).

Interestingly, PAP1 acts as a transcriptional repressor of at least 10 genes involved in the lignin branch of the phenylpropanoid pathway (Bhargava et al. 2010), while MYC2/3/4 transactivates MYB46, whose protein directly activates downstream transcriptional regulators involved in the biosynthesis of cellulose and hemicellulose, as well as specifc phenylpropanoid genes involved in lignin biosynthesis (Im et al. 2024). Therefore, MYCs play a pivotal role in balancing diferent metabolic pathways by activating both PAP1 and MYB46, which have opposing efects on secondary wall biosynthesis. PAP1’s dual role in promoting anthocyanin production and repressing secondary wall

3-O-methyltransferase; DFR, dihydrofavonol reductase; F3H, favonoid-3-hydroxylase; F5H, ferulic acid 5-hydroxylase; 3GT, favonoid 3-O-glycosyltransferase; GST26, glutathione S-transferase 26; HCT, hydroxycinnamoyl-CoA:shikimate/quinate hydroxycinnamoyltransferase; 5MAT, malonyl-CoA cyanidin 3,5-diglucoside transferase; PAL, phenylalanine ammonia-lyase; UGT, UDP-glycosyltransferase.

biosynthesis may help plants allocate resources efciently under diferent physiological conditions, prioritizing pigment production when needed while suppressing secondary cell wall formation. The expression of favonoid genes linked to insect resistance in injured plants, referred to as MGAIs (MYC-related genes against insects), is notably infuenced by AtMYC3, having the most signifcant impact (Wang et al. 2021a). In rice, OsMYC2 drastically enhances the promoter activity of the OsNOMT, which encodes naringenin 7-O-methyltransferase (NOMT), a key enzyme for the biosynthesis of sakuranetin, a favonoid antifungal phytoalexin (Ogawa et al. 2017). In grapevine, VvMYC1, which belongs to the subgroup IIIf bHLH, interacts with diferent MYB TFs and activates the promoters of anthocyanin and proanthocyanidin pathway genes (Hichri et al. 2010; Matus et al. 2010). In Artemisia annua, AaMYC2 promotes anthocyanin biosynthesis in stem but not in leaf tissue (Shen et al. 2016). In Gynura bicolor, GbMYC1 and GbMYB1 induced by MeJA treatment work together to coordinately up-regulate anthocyanin biosynthetic genes through their binding to the promoters of DFR and ANS (anthocyanidin synthase) (Shimizu et al. 2011). In Salvia miltiorrhiza, SmMYC2 and

SmbHLH60 form a heterodimer to antagonistically regulate phenolic acids and anthocyanins biosynthesis; upon MeJA treatment, SmbHLH60 is down-regulated, while SmMYC2 is up-regulated. This, in turn, amplifes the expressions of DFR and TAT1 (tyrosine aminotransferase 1), resulting in an overall increase in the production of anthocyanins and phenolic acids (Liu et al. 2022b).

In various instances, the adaptive modulation of the trade-off balance between the primary metabolism for plant growth and secondary metabolism for plant defense responses involves the inhibition of MYCs activity by GA signaling repressor DELLAs, as well as the transcriptional activation of DELLAs by MYCs. The reciprocal inhibition between DELLA and JAZs may additionally play a role in maintaining a delicate trade-of balance between growth and defense. MYCs, in turn, instigate a feedback loop by inducing the expression of JAZs, thereby autonomously regulating the synthesis of defense metabolites under MYCs control and orchestrating the coordination between primary and secondary metabolism.

(a) Both MYC2 and PIF3 directly trans-activate TPS promoters in the light and dark, respectively. MYC2 also can interact with PIF3 to form a heterodimer and synergistically enhance terpene biosynthesis. However, MYC2-PIF4 heterodimer complex down-regulates TPS.   
(b) GhMYC2 can either directly trans-activate the promoters of GOS or associate with GoPGF to enhance gossypol biosynthesis. GoPGF also interacts with CGP1 to form a GhMYC2-GoPGF-CGP1 complex, which increases their afnity and specifcity for DNA binding to the promoters of GOS.   
(c) The simultaneous induction of transcriptional activator ERF15 and repressor ERF12 by TcMYC2a may serve to fne-tune the amplitude and timing of gene expression to balance the level of paclitaxel in plants. JAMs further tightly control biosynthesis of paclitaxel biosynthesis by repressing TASY gene.   
(d) In the absence of JA, JAZs physically interact with CrMYC2 and BIS1/2 and repress their stimulatory activities of TIAs biosynthesis. Upon JA elicitation, the freed CrMYC2 and BIS1/2 up-regulates expression of RMT1, which in turn down-regulates CrMYC2 and its target gene ORCAs by binding their promoters in a dosage-dependent manner. CrMYC2 physically interacts with GBFs to form a heterodimer complex that can attenuate the repression of target genes (TDC, STR). In addition, CrMYC2 up-regulates the expression of ZCTs, which in turn down-regulate TDC and STR. This establishes a negative feedback circuit aimed at fne-tuning the levels of activation within the CrMYC2/ORCAs system that regulates biosynthesis of TIAs. ERF5 and CR1 function as a transcriptional activator and repressor of

the multiple genes in the upstream and downstream TIAs pathway genes, respectively. CrWRKY1 serves as a dual-function TF, acting as a transcriptional activator by up-regulating AS, TDC, DXS, SLS, and ZCTs, and concurrently as a transcriptional repressor by downregulating CrMYC2 and ORCAs. MAPK cascade system modulates the activities of CrMYC2 and ORCAs by phosphorylation. In the absence of light, CrPEP1 down-regulates CrGATA1, T16H2, and DAT genes. In the presence of light, CrPIF1 is destabilized, and derepressed CrGATA1 transactivates vindoline biosynthesis genes in the downstream TIAs pathway (T16H2, T3O, T3R, D4H, DAT), leading to vindoline accumulation. See the text for further details.

The modulation of anthocyanin biosynthesis is orchestrated by various TFs that react to distinct environmental stimuli. The activation and inhibition of the genes and proteins through transcriptional and physical interaction activities involving AtMYCs, HY5, SPA1-COP1, PHYB, and CRY1/2 are described in Fig. 3. The biosynthetic genes are regulated directly by HY5 in response to light or indirectly by other signals through modulation of the expression of numerous TFs. As positive regulators of anthocyanin biosynthesis, MYCs positively regulate expression of PAP1 either directly or indirectly through HY5. In cold stress, MYCs stabilizes MBW complex through ICES-CBFs module. The positive regulation of anthocyanin biosynthesis by MYCs-ICEs-CBFs module is described in more detail in Fig. 10. The light-dependent MPK4 phosphorylates PAP1 to increase its stability. In addition, HY5 can directly bind to the promoters of multiple ABGs in a light-dependent manner. As negative regulators of anthocyanin biosynthesis in Arabidopsis, JAZ1hinder the process by interacting with PAP1, leading to the destabilization of the MBW complex. Under Pi defciency, PHR1 is activated to interact with MYC2 and facilitate anthocyanin biosynthesis. See the text for further details.

# Concluding remarks

MYCs function as a point of hormonal crosstalk and equilibrium modulators in multifaceted regulatory mechanisms that integrate internal and external signals to control almost every cellular process of plant growth and development, as well as adapt to a variety of biotic and abiotic stresses. In addition to facilitating the recruitment of the transcription machinery to promoters, MYCs actively regulate the catalytic activities of interaction partners, either enhancing or inhibiting them directly. To coordinate MYCs functions, precise control of MYCs level and activity is essential, achieved through the intricate regulation by a network of

interdependent proteins responsible for the stabilization and destabilization through PTMs and PPIs. The extent and duration of MYCs-targeted gene expression are infuenced by co-regulators, both directly and indirectly afecting MYC abundance and activity. This regulation occurs in collaboration with other independent TFs that respond to environmental cues and developmental changes. Future research should focus on uncovering additional TFs and co-regulators that interact with MYCs to enhance regulatory precision. Moreover, exploring the spatiotemporal expression patterns of TFs involved in the crosstalk between MYCs-regulated pathways and other signaling cascades could ofer comprehensive insights into the plasticity and adaptability of plant defense strategies. Additionally, the application of this knowledge in crop improvement and the development of varieties resistant to biotic and abiotic stresses holds promising avenues for sustainable agriculture.

# Future issues

The availability of spatiotemporal expression profles and information on the trafcking of co-regulators that afect the abundance and activity of MYCs is currently limited. While all plant tissues have the potential to be sensitive to stress, the molecular responses implemented to withstand adverse conditions may vary depending on organs, tissues, and developmental stages. The regulatory infuence of TFs in plant tissues can be both spatially and temporally modulated. Therefore, an integrative analysis of spatiotemporal transcriptomics data encompassing various environmental cues and developmental changes will enhance our understanding of the context-dependent roles of MYCs in regulating specifc metabolic pathway genes.   
The elucidation of the phosphorylation sites on MYCs by various protein kinases (MPK6, MPK1/2/7, EDR1) in relation to MYCs activities and their spatiotemporal expression patterns has yet to be compiled.   
Light responses and photomorphogenic traits are often linked to plant responses to biotic and abiotic stresses. The reciprocal regulation involving the two master regulators, MYCs and HY5 occurs at both transcriptional and physical interaction levels. The activities and target specifcities of both MYCs and HY5 may vary depending on tissue types, PTMs, and PPIs with diferent coregulators, whose activities can fuctuate in response to environmental and developmental cues. Particularly, the precise mechanism through which MYC2, when phosphorylated by BL-activated MPK6, either positively or negatively regulates HY5 is not yet fully understood. Gaining a deeper understanding of the factors infuencing the outcomes of this interactive regulation will help

to comprehend the context-dependent roles of MYCs in regulating the magnitude and timing of HY5 expression, as well as abundance and activity of HY5 protein in response to diverse environmental cues.

The functions of MYCs in the ripening of non-climacteric fruits have yet to be studied. Most research on plant response have focused on vegetative organs, particularly leaves and roots. The roles of MYCs during diferent stages of fruit ripening remain largely unexplored.   
• Our knowledge about the roles of MYCs in the induced systemic resistance (ISR) and synergistic efects of JA and SA on thermotolerance is still rudimentary.   
Examining the mechanism through which thermospermine regulates the expression of MYCs and JAZs unveils a new layer of complexity in hormonal crosstalk. Investigating how MYCs contribute to balancing JA- and SAmediated defense mechanisms by infuencing polyamine levels suggests potential implications for targeted interventions aimed at enhancing plant resistance.
